# 20

# Endogenous return and risk: Feedback effects on expected returns

- Feedback loops can amplify market movements if market participants respond to past performance. Irrational extrapolation may have evolutionary origins. Virtuous and vicious cycles may also reflect rational forces, such as wealth-dependent risk aversion, leverage cycles, and liquidity spirals.
- Such dynamics are especially powerful when applied to market direction (booms and busts) but they can also be overlaid on the performance of any asset or strategy. Presumably, they contribute to both short-term momentum and long-term reversal patterns observed for many assets.
- Recent success can make any asset or strategy more popular over time and thereby also more risky (beta like), especially when a number of years pass between realization of negative risk events. Crowdedness raises liquidation risks and makes the return stream more negatively skewed. Currency carry strategies and the August 2007 quant crisis are good illustrations.

The 12 case studies above contain limited discussion about market dynamics. However, an important feature of financial market regularities is that market participants tend to respond to past performance, often herding into recently successful strategies. Imitating success, or learning, may have been an evolutionarily useful approach for our ancestors, and thus hardwired into us, but in modern financial markets this tendency may be unhealthy, even dangerous. Such feedback effects make asset returns and risks endogenous. That is, the market's expected returns at least partly come from within: the subject is not independent of the observer. When market participants observe realized asset returns it influences their actions and thereby market prices—thus also affecting future returns and risks. As the market's expected returns are unobservable, many investors overextrapolate past realized returns, missing the notion that higher past returns can be indicative of lower future returns.

# 20.1 FEEDBACK LOOPS ON THE DIRECTION OF RISKY ASSETS

The partially endogenous nature of returns is especially important at the aggregate market level because, even if wealth gains do not make investors more optimistic about future cash flows, they can make investors more risk tolerant and lenders more willing to offer leverage. As discussed in the context of market bubbles in Section 6.3.1, such feedback loops amplify market moves even if they eventually prove self-defeating. These ideas echo Soros' reflexivity principle, which states that market prices not only reflect expectations of future outcomes but also shape economic outcomes through a feedback loop (see Section 6.3). When the boom turns into a bust, virtuous cycles turn vicious. Lawrence Summers has noted that, when multiple vicious cycles operate simultaneously and reinforce each other, normal self-stabilizing mechanisms in the market economy ("the genius of capitalism") may be overwhelmed. This is clearly what happened in 2007-2008. Besides irrational sentiment (animal spirits, the cycle of fear and greed) and extrapolation of recent performance, as well as feedback loops between the real economy and financial markets, here are several rational and purely financial mechanisms that boost procyclicality:

- time-varying risk premia due to wealth-dependent risk aversion or risk (falling wealth, and rising volatilities and/or correlations, raise required returns with the result that today's asset prices need to fall);
- leverage/loss/margin spirals (losses prompt financial intermediaries to shrink their own balance sheets, while margin calls due to losses, higher "haircuts", and investor redemptions force hedge funds and other levered customers to reduce positions, with the result that selling pressure pushes risky assets even lower);
- liquidity spirals (waning trust and worsening funding conditions cause forced liquidations; a synchronous rush for the exit causes market liquidity to potentially dry up);
- risk control spirals (VaR-based risk management, stop-loss rules, portfolio insurance strategies, and mark-to-market accounting combined with regulatory and rating pressures lead to risk reduction when market prices fall and measured volatilities and/or correlations rise).

Recent research emphasizes the interactions between these various mechanisms as well as the central role of financial intermediaries and their funding constraints. Higher volatility pushes haircuts higher and thereby hurts funding liquidity. The wealth effect makes the demand responses of leveraged investors for risky assets upward sloping and their supply responses amidst meltdowns downward sloping. Higher leverage expands the economy's risk-bearing capacity, procyclicality, and systemic risks. Position unwinds can suddenly transform stabilizing arbitrageurs (liquidity suppliers) into destabilizing liquidators (liquidity demanders). Combining leverage and illiquid assets is particularly dangerous because, when credit conditions tighten, distressed selling of crowded positions is more likely to occur at fire sale prices.

While the procyclical responses listed above can make sense individually for each institution, collectively such actions exacerbate market moves and make most investors worse off. In general, a heterogeneous ecology of investment approaches would make financial markets more balanced and robust than what we have seen in recent years. Perold-Sharpe (1988) discussed two mirroring dynamic investment approaches—buying risky assets as they fall (and selling them as they rise) vs. selling risky assets as they fall (and buying them when they rise). Today we call these approaches contrarian (reversal) and momentum (trend-following) strategies. Contrarian strategies are inherently market stabilizing while momentum strategies are destabilizing. The balance between the sizes of the two groups is essential for market behavior. We saw this in 2008 when falling wealth and rising risk aversion prompted most investors to reduce risky asset holdings—and value-oriented buyers failed to appear to the scene.

Perold and Sharpe stressed that when contrarian strategies succeed for a while, they attract followers and markets may become too stable (too slow to adjust to economic developments). Conversely, when momentum strategies succeed for a while, they attract new followers and markets may become too volatile (overreacting to economic news). In the post-crisis environment of 2010 we clearly are in the latter situation where "everyone is a risk manager" and stop-loss approaches are popular (stop-loss limits imply selling into weakness). However, as the authors emphasized, excess popularity of one approach eventually results in excess gains for the opposite style, a process which over time should drive the relative sizes of the two groups toward a balance.

At the time of writing (mid-2010), a less momentum-oriented approach also seems consistent with the other lesson of the past decade: contrarian market timing can add value over long horizons—especially if regulators are more keen than before to create countercyclic buffers and central bankers to lean against the wind. I suspect that investors will turn to this second lesson only if we see very large market moves and glaring value opportunities. The "buy on dips" mentality has been discredited in the current investor generation's mind, unless the dip is very deep.

# 20.2 FEEDBACK LOOPS ON LESS DIRECTIONAL POSITIONS

Feedback loops also matter beyond market-directional exposures. Similar return-chasing dynamics can be overlaid on the performance of any return source. Fluctuating fortunes and fashionability may explain the prevalence of short-term momentum and long-term reversal patterns observed in the return series of many financial assets and trading strategies (see Figure 20.1). These dynamics may also make any successful strategy more beta like and its return more asymmetric ("up the stairs, down the escalator"), with scant fundamental reason.

Figure 20.1. Similar speculative dynamics (Momentum + Reversal) in many financial strategies, 1990-2009.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-22/aa485235-5155-4ade-b88b-be1df0035d75/4be8d09ed260114a1a063d86e54d7cb3fc78aa95f1d23c08d3164c63b29a70ed.jpg)

Sources: Bloomberg, LPX, Bank of America Merrill Lynch, Barclays Capital, Hedge Fund Research, Ken French's website, S&P GSCI, MSCI Barra, FTSE, own calculations.

The first bar shows the (auto)correlation between adjacent month returns for each major asset class or strategy that I cover in this book (i.e., short-term momentum of the asset class or strategy). The second bar shows the correlation between the latest 6-month return and the 6-month return 18 months earlier, measuring "long-term" reversal. I present evidence only for the 1990s and 2000s because data availability is better for a broad set of assets during this period and because this period gives us pure out-of-sample results compared with the first studies reporting such speculative dynamics (Cutler-Poterba-Summers, 1991) [1]. Across the investments in Figure 20.1, average short-term momentum is  $+0.20$  and average long-term reversal is  $-0.11$ . The evidence on short-term momentum is especially compelling although a part of it may reflect illiquidity and return smoothing. (Even the two exceptions with mild negative autocorrelations here—commodity trend following and the size effect in U.S. equities—have mild positive autocorrelations over longer sample periods.)

More fundamentally, analysts' earnings forecasts exhibit similar short-term momentum and long-term reversal patterns. Ghayur et al. (2010) argue that this is the main reason for the success of momentum and value strategies.

For illustration, let us consider the evolution of a profitable investment strategy—say, FX carry or harvesting illiquidity premia. Figure 20.2 shows a stylized lifecycle of the ex ante Sharpe ratio. Once the strategy's past success attracts widespread attention, it triggers profit-seeking inflows through extrapolation or learning. Inflows may temporarily boost profitability but growing competition gradually eats up the ex ante opportunity, while the realized return remains deceivingly good. In the absence of good forward-looking anchors, the expected return may even become negative, due to overvaluation and overcrowded positions. The liquidation phase is likely to wait until broader bad times, correlating the strategy's fortunes with the equity market. When bad times do arrive, even though plummeting performance revives the strategy's ex ante attractiveness, speculative capital gets removed from the losing strategy. If the regularity that caused the strategy to be profitable in the first place reflects a (time-varying) risk premium, the new equilibrium should involve a sustainably positive Sharpe ratio, albeit lower than before the regularity was widely known. If the regularity reflects market inefficiency, the Sharpe ratio should converge toward zero once markets have learned about it.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-22/aa485235-5155-4ade-b88b-be1df0035d75/7e54ea6b2b7e40a2dd310beba9b715cb105fc5160ff56dbec10805f093f778ce.jpg)

Figure 20.2. Stylized lifecycle of a regularity's ex ante Sharpe ratio.

As discussed in the context of FX carry, this evolution can be characterized as alpha morphing into beta—and not just into any beta but into a bad, insidious variety. The strategy's riskiness is not easily recognized in good times, and losses tend to materialize in bad times.

Strategies with inherently asymmetric payoffs are especially dangerous. Because rare disasters happen rarely, benign conditions can last longer, and more and more investors get sucked into the strategies. FX carry, index volatility selling, front-end credit trading, and harvesting illiquidity premia are examples where the time span between disasters could be a decade or more. Worse, the losses do not materialize at random points in time but in environments like 1998 and 2008 when they are least welcome. Market dynamics are different in those rare occasions: for example, losses become more synchronous when funding conditions become binding and levered traders unwind previously successful positions.

Other profitable strategies do not inherently have negative-skew or positive-beta characteristics but may acquire them largely because their past success—if persistent enough—makes them popular, crowded among leveraged traders, and more likely to fail sharply during financial crises.

Another real-world example: Khandani-Lo (2007) and Pedersen (2009) discuss the quant strategy meltdown in August 2007, when fundamentally and historically uncorrelated equity long-short strategies began temporarily to move in lock step. The combination of common positions and deterioration in liquidity conditions triggered a joint rush for the exit, leading to unprecedented losses over a few days. A particular feedback loop is that liquidations made mispricings worse in popular relative value positions, causing losses to widen and spreading to systematic strategies in other asset classes.

The lesson was that common holdings among hedge funds give rise to a new type of risk which is purely "technical" (in the sense of reflecting the supply and demand for assets, rather than their fundamental values) and which we might call crowded trade risk ("we have found a new risk factor—it is us"). The risk is that when a popular strategy becomes unpopular, would-be sellers will demand liquidity but there is no one buying. That these dangers were not specific to quant investing became abundantly clear during the following year. Arguably, any historically successful strategy has a tendency to become popular among speculative traders. The holdings of leveraged traders and intermediaries are especially important because they may be forced to unwind their positions.

# 20.3 AGENDA FOR MARKET-TIMERS AND RESEARCHERS

Endogeneity contributes to momentum and value effects (see Chapters 14 and 12), and maybe also to carry and liquidity premia (Chapters 13 and 18). Here is one angle. Financial folklore is filled with wise but conflicting clichés, such as "The trend is your friend" and "Avoid the crowds". Both contain a seed of truth but cannot be right in every context. Likewise, the empirical lesson that "hanging on to losers" is a common, costly investment error is hard to reconcile with evidence that "return chasing" or "excessive firing and hiring of managers" hurts long-run performance. Different time horizons can help resolve these apparent contradictions (though other distinguishing contexts may also be relevant). Selling winners and keeping losers over a multi-month horizon hurts if returns exhibit short-term momentum. The opposite action—return chasing—hurts when done over a multi-year horizon if long-term reversals exist, and generally they do. Indeed, Figure 20.1 suggests that market participants' collective actions create a short-term momentum bias and a mild long-term reversal bias [2].

These patterns encourage "market timing" any return source by exploiting short-term momentum (joining the bandwagon) or by resisting market excesses (contrarian positioning). Yet, easy opportunities exist only with hindsight. In real-world risk taking, contrarian trading will lose money if no reversal occurs (outsized returns reflect true structural change or a sustained risk premium) or if the reversal positions are entered too early.

Investment practitioners have rightly become wary of consensus trades and deleveraging risks. They have learned to monitor, on one hand, excessive crowding and stretched market valuations to identify future trouble spots, and, on the other hand, any signs that could trigger liquidations—tightening financial conditions or heightened market turbulence and risk aversion.

The topic of endogeneity provides a fertile field for research. It has broadened from its behavioral origins, in researching bubbles and positive-feedback trading (Shiller, Summers, Shleifer, and others), to rational analyses that have proved useful in explaining the 2007-2008 credit and liquidity crunch. Markus Brunnermeier, Lasse Pedersen, Hyun Song Shin, John Geanakoplos, and others have led the academic work, forming a new school of thought sometimes called the liquidity movement. In addition, policymakers are keen to develop macro-prudential regulation and economic policies that counter various feedback loops and related procyclicality.

Andrew Lo's adaptive markets hypothesis (see Section 5.3) spearheads one interesting strand of this literature. It is not a single well-developed model but a collection of insights based on evolutionary principles. Competition should drive alpha toward zero but not in a linear fashion. The degree of market efficiency and the attractiveness of trading strategies can wax and wane depending on the environment. Relative populations in market ecology will evolve over time: there will be more momentum traders after contrarian value strategies fail, louder and better respected market bears after a large selloff, etc. The ability to adapt to changing environments through innovation and flexibility is the key to investment success, just as it is to evolutionary success.

Stein (2009) addresses an interesting question in the same spirit: "Do smarter investors make markets more efficient?" He notes that typical market participants have likely become more sophisticated over time (more professional investment, more quantitative analysis, more leveraged players). This tendency should make any simple regularities less profitable. However, data mining, especially quant investors' repeated use of the same empirical history and similar models and ideas to develop strategies, gives rise to new problems, such as overcrowding, and subsequent liquidations at fire sale prices. Thus, it is not clear whether financial markets have really become more efficient.

# 20.4 NOTES

[1] The reversal measure seems arbitrary (contrasting the most recent 6-month vs. 18-to-24-month-ago returns). Results are broadly similar for any past 6-month window beyond the first year; during the first year, the momentum effect still dominates. The reversal indicator I use is not really multi-year, but with a 20-year data window I have too few independent observations to study truly long-horizon reversals. Looking at longer histories, 3-to-5-year stock returns appear to be negatively autocorrelated in both the 19th and 20th centuries, but the same is not true for real bond returns. In any case, such mean reversion evidence is relatively weak and plagued with econometric problems. Mean-reverting deviations from some fundamental value anchor often give better trading signals than simple past returns. Finally, relative mean reversion across assets may well be stronger than absolute mean reversion if general risk appetite exhibits multi-year trends.

[2] The disposition effect (tendency of individuals to sell winners rather than losers from the existing portfolio) can cause momentum through underreaction effects (resistant flows delay information dissemination into market prices). Moreover, the success of any asset or strategy tends to attract net inflows, which generate short-term momentum. The widespread selling of an asset class after a couple of years of underperformance may create temporary undervaluation and give rise to later reversal.

Source notes. On feedback effects that create endogenous return and risk, see Shiller (2000), Lo (2004), Soros (2008), Brunnermeier-Nagel-Pedersen (2008), Brunnermeier-Pedersen (2009), Danielsson-Shin-Zigrand (2009), Geanakoplos (2009), Summers (2009), and Shin (2010). On crowded trades, see Perold-Sharpe (1988), Khandani-Lo (2007), Pedersen (2009), and Stein (2009). On short-term momentum and long-term reversals, see Cutler-Poterba-Summers (1991) and Ghayur et al. (2010).

# 21

# Forward-looking measures of asset returns

- Value and carry indicators can be useful predictors of future asset returns, especially over longer horizons, even if we do not know the underlying reason for the generic cheapness.
- Popular indicators include earnings yields and dividend yields for equities, real yields and yield curve steepness for Treasuries, and various credit spreads for non-government bonds.
- Prospective real long-term returns can be compared across asset classes (apples with apples).
- In a building block approach, each asset's or strategy's prospective return is expressed as a sum of carry, expected cash flow growth, and expected valuation change. This framework could be useful for assessing the prospects of several asset classes in a consistent manner.

Forward-looking return signals are surprisingly good indicators of long-term return outlook. Here, I review popular value and carry measures for major asset classes. They have their shortcomings but these are partly compensated by their simplicity and real-time observability. More complex value indicators exist but involve more specific assumptions (model specification risk), more elaborate statistical estimation (estimation risk), and often more hindsight bias. Carry and value indicators can usefully (if noisily) predict future returns even when we don't understand the reason for the risk premium and even when we don't know whether the source of predictability is a rational risk premium or irrational mispricing. This generic nature of carry and value indicators is a clear advantage, given our inherently limited understanding of the ultimate drivers of expected asset returns.

If expected returns vary over time, how can we know the current level of expected returns? In most cases we cannot. Sadly, the market's expected returns are unobservable. The best we can do is estimate them, by hook or crook. The simplest and more popular approaches involve relating current market prices to some value anchors. If current market prices are low compared with such anchors (which can be as simple as historical average price, yield or spread, or something more complex), expected returns are deemed to be above average. Such value indicators do not reveal whether high current expected returns reflect irrational underpricing or rationally high risk premia. Moreover, these value indicators can be poor short-term predictors. They may fail even over longer horizons if structural change shifts the long-run mean.

Carry indicators are often related to value indicators but details differ. Carry captures an asset's income return over time. Besides cash income, broader carry measures include other returns earned under conditions of stable or unchanged markets (e.g., if the term structure of interest rates or of commodity futures prices stays unchanged). If the yield curve is upward sloping and remains unchanged, bonds earn capital gains simply by aging and rolling down the yield curve. Similarly, if, say, a backwardated crude oil futures price curve remains unchanged over a quarter, a futures contract earns roll gains as its maturity shrinks and it rolls up the futures price curve.

Value and carry, discussed in this chapter, are objective, ex ante return indicators based on market prices. Chapter 23, in contrast, tackles survey-based (subjective) measures of ex ante returns. Chapter 24 reviews tactical approaches to predicting returns based on multiple indicators, in contrast to the slow-moving strategic signals provided by the value and carry approaches treated in the current chapter.

# 21.1 POPULAR VALUE AND CARRY INDICATORS AND THEIR PITFALLS

# Equities

For equities, the earnings yield or earnings per share (E/P)—the inverse of the price/earnings (P/E) ratio—is the most popular value indicator, especially since the growing popularity of share buybacks reduced dividend yield's information content. Under rather restrictive assumptions, a stock's earnings yield equals its expected real return. Experts debate which earnings measure and which time window or horizon to use. Firms and sell-side analysts tend to focus on "operating earnings" that ignore non-recurring items, such as writeoffs, which may distort the general picture of earnings trends. Such data have been available since the 1980s. However, since non-recurring items typically are negative, operating earnings are regularly higher than reported earnings. Advocates of using reported instead of operating earnings point out that, while any particular writeoff is non-recurring, writeoffs are the way that companies report the results of business decisions that did not work out. Such decisions are commonplace, so a certain level of writeoff activity should be expected, on average, each year in the future.

Moreover, the operating earnings number that is usually cited by sell-side analysts is the forward-looking consensus analyst estimate of next year earnings, rather than trailing earnings. Forward earnings are invariably higher than trailing earnings, partly reflecting the normal uptrend in earnings, and partly analyst overoptimism that is related to their incentives. Reported earnings have made a comeback after the equity bubble years; other alternatives have been developed but none has a history as long as that available for reported earnings yields. If one is going to use trailing earnings, how many years' trailing earnings should be used? One-year trailing earnings are common but can exhibit cyclical fluctuations that are too strong (e.g., giving rise to the high P/E ratios in the 1932, 2002, and 2009 bear markets). For this reason, smoother long-term earnings—up to a 10-year window—may be preferred.

Note that  $\mathrm{E} / \mathrm{P}$  is an absolute value indicator—it relates a firm's current  $\mathrm{E} / \mathrm{P}$  level to its own history.  $\mathrm{E} / \mathrm{P}$  and other value measures can also be compared across individual equities or equity market sectors and countries, thus making them relative value indicators.

Equity risk premium measures based on the dividend discount model (DDM) directly incorporate some estimate of future cash flow growth. However, the DDM is an accounting identity and not a fully developed model. Under the assumption that current market valuations will not change over time, the expected return on an equity investment reflects just dividend income plus the expected growth rate of dividends, where dividend growth is achieved through retention of earnings (i.e., reinvestment into the company of that part of earnings not paid out as dividends). (Analysts may substitute, in place of dividends, a broader measure of cash flow to the investor.) The DDM can be expressed in either nominal or real terms; the real version says that expected real equity return equals dividend yield plus expected real dividend growth. The equity premium over bonds is thus the difference between expected real equity return and real Treasury yield [1].

The classic carry proxy, dividend yield (D/P), has become too narrow a measure of carry because firms increasingly use means other than dividends to distribute cash to investors, including share repurchases and cash acquisitions. In particular, share repurchases have substituted dividend payments. I discussed broader payout yields in Section 8.4.

# Government bonds

Yield to maturity (YTM) is a bond's internal rate of return and the traditional value measure in fixed income markets. YTM measures an asset's expected return over its lifetime if it is held to maturity and if cash flows can be reinvested at the same YTM rate. (Whenever yield curves are not exactly flat, it follows that different reinvestment rates are inconsistently assumed for different bonds.) Over short horizons, YTM equals expected return if a bond can be sold exactly at the yield at which it was bought. These assumptions are of course highly restrictive but, for straight (non-optionable) bonds, YTM can be a decent proxy for expected return. YTM clearly captures the yield income (carry) component, and the yield spread between two bonds may also predict capital gains if the spread is mean-reverting.

Bond yields reflect the market's rate expectations and the required bond risk premium (BRP). If we have an observable proxy of the market's rate expectations, we can isolate the two components of the yield curve and thus get a cleaner BRP proxy. Some analysts use term structure models or econometric analyses to isolate the two components but I prefer to use survey data. The survey-based BRP is simply the difference between 10-year Treasury yield and the consensus forecast of average short-term Treasury bill rates over the next decade.

Expected real bond yield is another useful value indicator (and more easily available); it is the difference between bond yield and some deflator. Real yields may use as deflators either the trailing inflation rate, a market-based inflation estimate, survey-based expected inflation, or some econometric estimate of expected inflation. I prefer to use maturity-matched consensus forecasts of long-term inflation, which have been available since the late 1970s (statistical estimates of expected inflation can be used before this). The introduction of inflation-linked bond markets (in 1981 in the U.K. and in 1997 in the U.S.) gave us a market-based alternative for assessing real yields; however, linker yield clearly differs from the expected real yield of nominal bonds due to time-varying inflation and liquidity premia.

# Non-government bonds

YTM is the constant discount rate which, when applied to expected cash flows, produces the prices of the bond. Raw YTM spreads involve some unreasonable assumptions and maturity mismatches. In the olden days, YTW or yield to worst (i.e., "worst call" or call date least favorable to the investor) was an improvement over raw YTM, which simply ignored embedded options in, say, callable corporate bonds. To incorporate the value of embedded options, YTW computed the bond's yield to maturity and to various potential call dates, and then used the lowest of the resulting yields. The option-adjusted spread (OAS) was developed in the 1980s as a more consistent measure of a bond's probability-weighted yield spread or advantage over a reference curve (often Treasuries or interest rate swaps).

The OAS was originally developed for bonds with embedded options but it is also used for "bullet" bonds with known cash flows. For such bonds, the OAS is the size of parallel shift in the reference curve needed to make the bond's present value using the adjusted curve to match the bond's market price. The OAS takes all bond cash flows into account so that a corporate bond is effectively duration-matched to reference curve assets. For bonds with uncertain cash flows, the OAS is calculated using some term structure model and some volatility assumption. OAS estimates can differ widely across data providers, depending on model specification and on the assumed level of interest rate volatility. The OAS is closely related to credit default swap (CDS) spreads (when both use swaps as the reference curve), but exact calculations differ. Only the CDS spread incorporates non-zero recovery values, which are important for high-yield debt.

So does the OAS or the yield spread between a higher yielding bond and a lower yielding Treasury properly capture the expected return advantage of the former? The OAS incorporates the fact that the investor has effectively written an option (or many) to the issuer/borrower. A simple yield spread misses this optionality and thereby overstates the expected return advantage. But both measures are overstated because the yield spread also reflects expected losses due to defaults or downgradings (see Chapter 10).

Carry and financing rates. If the corporate bond and government bond had the same financing costs, the OAS or yield spread would not only measure the value but also the carry of the long-corporate, short-Treasury position. However, these financing costs do differ. As a good approximation, corporates can be financed at deposit rates (such as LIBOR) while government bonds can be financed at repo rates, which are lower: either the general collateral (GC) rate or the even lower special repo rate. Thus, a proper carry measure for levered credit investors also includes a financing spread; carry is positive only if the asset's credit spread (corporate minus Treasury) exceeds the financing spread (LIBOR minus GC). An unlevered investor simply considering where to allocate his capital may ignore the financing rate difference; for such an institution, yield spread also measures carry advantage. More generally, financing costs vary not only across assets but also across investors for the same asset: margins or haircuts may be larger and bid-ask spreads wider for lower rated investors and for smaller customers.

For money market spreads, a particular pitfall is that quotation conventions vary across assets, typically for historical reasons. For example, compounding frequencies or day-count practices can differ, and some assets (Treasury bills and commercial paper) are quoted as discount rates whereas others (deposits and CDs) are quoted as money market rates. Any meaningful analysis starts by making different quotations comparable.

Figures 21.1 and 21.2 give a flavor of forward-looking expected return measures in several asset classes. The first graph shows reasonably comparable measures (ex ante real yields) while the second graph shows more typical but less comparable ex ante indicators for equities and bonds. Also see Figure 2.7 for a century-long history.

# 21.2 BUILDING BLOCKS OF EXPECTED RETURNS

The toolkits investors use to assess future return prospects often differ across asset classes, reflecting dissimilarities between asset types as well as investor specialization. This book emphasizes the similarities between these toolkits (e.g., by highlighting parallel value and carry indicators in different contexts). Still, subtle or less subtle differences in indicators can lead to misleading comparisons across asset classes. In this section, I sketch a building block approach that can give a consistent framework for evaluating multiple investment types.

Recall the building blocks for prospective equity market returns in Figure 8.3: Yield income (carry) + Expected cash flow growth (inflation plus real growth) + Expected valuation change. A similar analysis using building blocks may be useful for assessing the long-term returns of all asset classes and strategy styles. Carry and value indicators have key roles in such a building block analysis:

- Carry captures the first building block. It can refer to income or, more broadly, to return in an unchanged capital market scenario, including some rolldown effects and fee or cost effects. (Roll effects are discussed in Sections 9.2, 11.3, and 22.2.)
- Value indicators are key for determining the third building block if we expect any reversal tendency toward a value anchor. Valuation changes are important in accounting for realized returns, but the impact on return predictions is often modest because mean reversion tendencies can be weak. It is often sensible to assume unchanged valuations even if valuations differ somewhat from their long-run averages (this makes the third term zero, by assumption). It is more reasonable to assume some mean reversion if prevailing valuations are exceptionally high or low. Even then, other evidence may point to structural changes in valuation ratios, such as the recent persistent upward shift in the relative valuations of emerging markets vs. developed markets.
- Expected nominal cash flow growth is the second building block. Stocks also benefit from expected cash flow growth, although the growth rate that one can reasonably expect is more modest than many investors believe. Similarly, real estate benefits from rent growth. In contrast, Treasuries have fixed cash flows, and credit-risky bonds can expect some default losses. One can debate where to classify fees, trading style costs, trading profits or losses of hedge funds, and trend-following strategies; my choices are shown in Table 21.1.

The boundaries between building blocks can be fuzzy. Note that expected inflation is captured in the first building block for bonds (as well as for commodity futures and hedge funds), but in the second block for stocks and real estate. For long-short strategies (the last four rows), inflation or short rates wash out in the long and short

Figure 21.1. Forward-looking real yields for U.S. equities, credits, Treasuries, and housing, 1970-2009.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-22/aa485235-5155-4ade-b88b-be1df0035d75/ddc36574688579726a05e0b6778c9ee15f067d161f87283d765d45fefb72dce4.jpg)

Table 21.1. Three building blocks of expected returns for diverse investments

<table><tr><td></td><td>Carry</td><td>Expected (E) cash flow growth</td><td>Expected (E) valuation change (e.g., mean reversion)</td></tr><tr><td>Stocks</td><td>Payout yield (D/P + Buybacks net of new issues)</td><td>E(EPS growth)*</td><td>E(ΔPrice/Earnings)*</td></tr><tr><td>Government bonds</td><td>Yield + Roll</td><td>—</td><td>E(ΔYield)</td></tr><tr><td>Credit-risky bonds</td><td>Yield + Roll</td><td>E(default losses)</td><td>E(ΔYield) including E(downgrading bias)</td></tr><tr><td>Real estate</td><td>Rental yield – Capex</td><td>E(rent growth)</td><td>E(ΔPrice/Rent)</td></tr><tr><td>Commodity futures</td><td>Roll + Collateral rate</td><td>—</td><td>E(ΔPrice)</td></tr><tr><td>Hedge funds, private equity funds</td><td>Underlying assets&#x27; yield income minus fund fees</td><td>E(profits from investment activity)</td><td>—</td></tr><tr><td>Equity value</td><td>Relative payout yield (between value and growth stocks) + Trading costs</td><td>E(relative EPS growth)</td><td>E(ΔRelative P/E)</td></tr><tr><td>Currency carry</td><td>Relative carry across currencies + Trading costs</td><td>—</td><td>E(ΔExchange rate)</td></tr><tr><td>Commodity momentum</td><td>Relative carry across commodities + Trading costs</td><td>E(profits from trading activity)</td><td>—</td></tr><tr><td>Index volatility selling</td><td>Spread between implied volatility and recent realized volatility</td><td>—</td><td>E(ΔVolatility)</td></tr></table>

* For consistency, the growth term and the valuation change term for stocks should reflect growth or change in the same payout flows to investors that we use in the income term. Mixing dividend yields or broader payout yields with expectations of EPS growth and changing P/E ratio, as I do in this table, is only justified if we assume a constant earnings retention ratio.

legs. For comparability with the first four rows, these rates would have to be added back when adding them to total expected returns.

Value and carry indicators are reviewed above for stocks and bonds. Value and carry indicators for real estate, commodities, and currencies are discussed in Sections 11.2, 11.3, and 13.3. Value is typically based on deviation of current market price from some valuation anchor (such as purchasing power parity for currencies or rental income for real estate). In the absence of better value anchors, investors may use average real market price over the past few years to capture any mean reversion tendency.

Natural value indicators exist for some systematic strategies (e.g., the dispersion of valuation ratios between value and growth stocks, or the implied-vs.-realized volatility gap for volatility selling). For FX carry, the combination of carry dispersion and expected spot rate changes from value dispersion across currencies capture ex ante opportunity. Useful measures of ex ante opportunity also exist for certain hedge fund strategies, such as merger arbitrage or convertible arbitrage. However, for trend following and for general hedge fund investing there are no obvious value anchors.

The relative importance of the three components varies across investments, often by construction. For example, the currency carry strategy earns nearly all of its long-run profits by being systematically long carry (buying high-yield currencies against selling low-yield currencies). In contrast, the commodity momentum strategy has ambiguous and incidental carry because recent winners can just as well have higher or lower carry than recent losers. Most of this strategy's profits come from the tendency of recent multimonth performance to persist. I classify the associated trading profits as cash flow growth. Given the limited guidance from carry or value indicators, it is hard to assess the prospective return of the commodity momentum strategy beyond assuming that "future equals past" for its long-run track record.

The advantage of Table 21.1 is that it enables apples-to-apples comparisons between asset classes and strategies, thereby giving a useful framework for multi-asset-class investors. The sum of the three components is total nominal return (when cash income is added to long-short strategy returns). If real or excess returns are studied, inflation rates or financing costs should be subtracted in a consistent way from all investments.

Admittedly, many inputs require significant judgment and can be debated even if we agree on the building block framework. Moreover, the exhibit is very coarse. I will not fill the table with numbers because they can differ widely within each group. Stocks include markets as diverse as those of the U.S., Japan, and emerging countries. Government securities include nominal and inflation-linked bonds and these are issued by countries with varying credit qualities and currency situations. The spectrum of exposures to credit is so wide that it cannot be briefly described. Real estate includes (at the time of writing) hot China, cold Florida, and dull Germany. Commodity futures include materials as different as oil, gold, and corn. Hedge funds include equity market neutral, global macro, fixed income arbitrage, and just about every other strategy that has ever been devised.

The proposed approach is simpler and more arbitrary than the econometric multifactor model discussed in Section 16.1, which also offers a consistent tool for multi-asset-class investors. In practice, different types and frequencies of data as well as different return drivers across asset classes may make this building block approach a more practical tool. Moreover, one could conceivably use econometric tools to forecast the different components of investment returns—such as their mean reversion tendencies—and do this consistently across asset classes or strategies.

The ideas developed in this section are obviously work in progress. Readers can feel free to take them further.

# 21.3 NOTES

[1] The DDM assumes a constant discount rate and a constant dividend (or other cash flow) growth rate. It is pretty straightforward to modify the constant growth rate assumption so as to move from a single-stage DDM to a multi-stage DDM (e.g., one can use analyst forecasts in the early years and then assume a gradual decay towards some "normal" long-run growth rate). It is harder to modify the assumption of a constant discount rate but Campbell-Shiller (1988) develop an approximate (linearized) dynamic DDM that allows time-varying expected returns.

Source notes. On the terminology of equity valuation measures, see Ilmanen (2003a), of bond valuation measures, see Ilmanen (1996), and of credit yield spreads, see O'Kane-Sen (2004) and Pedersen (2006).

# 22

# Interpreting carry or non-zero yield spreads

- Non-zero yield spreads can reflect the market's expectations about future rate changes or growth, or they can reflect higher yielding assets' prospective return advantage. In all asset markets we can identify interpretations analogous to these two. The two alternative interpretations can be called risk-neutral hypotheses and risk premium hypotheses.
- The uncovered interest parity hypothesis for currencies, the pure expectations hypothesis for interest rates, and analogous risk-neutral investor hypotheses for other asset classes presume that all assets have the same expected returns as the riskless asset. For consistency, market expectations must then imply price movements that cause capital losses just sufficient to offset any initial yield advantage.
- Risk premium hypotheses make the polar opposite claim: yield spreads only reveal required return differentials and reveal nothing about market expectations on future rates.
- Empirical "horse races" show that yield spreads have been better predictors of future returns than of "offsetting" market rate or growth changes. Over long histories, yield-seeking (positive-carry) strategies have been profitable in all asset classes studied. Such evidence contradicts risk-neutral hypotheses and supports risk premium hypotheses. (Even if carry losses are rare, their size and timing can justify large risk premia.)
- However, empirical evidence does not speak to the rationality of high prospective returns when carry is high. Survey evidence suggests that biased (irrational) expectations, in addition to or instead of rational risk premia, may have contributed to the historical success of yield-seeking strategies.

# 22.1 INTRODUCTION

This section discusses two opposite interpretations of why prospective yields differ across assets and presents empirical evidence to help assess which interpretation is more consistent with reality. Interestingly, this same question is raised in virtually all asset classes. Here are a few examples:

- If you see higher short-term nominal interest rates, say, in the U.S. than in Japan, does the U.S. yield advantage tell us that the market expects the dollar to depreciate against the yen or does it say that global investors require a higher near-term return for U.S. deposits than for unhedged Japanese deposits?
- If you see an upward-sloping yield curve, does it mean that the market expects rising yields in the future or that investors require higher returns for holding longer term bonds?
- If you see higher earnings yields (i.e., lower P/E ratios) for finance stocks than for energy stocks, does this yield difference indicate that the market expects slower future earnings growth (and thus more gradual capital gains) for finance stocks, or does it mean that investors currently require higher returns for finance stocks than for energy stocks because finance stocks are riskier?
- If you see a steeply backwardated (i.e., downward sloping) term structure of crude oil futures prices, is it a sign that the market expects oil prices to fall in the near term (maybe reflecting expectations of mean reversion after a sharp runup) or does it mean that investors require a high-return premium for owning the oil futures (earned if the futures price rolls up toward the higher spot price)?

Do you see the symmetry? In each case, the first interpretation says that yield spreads represent the market's expectation of future rate or price changes just sufficient to cause capital losses that exactly offset any initial carry advantage. It is thus assumed that all assets, regardless of their risk, have the same ex ante return, that of the riskless asset. The second interpretation says WYSIWYG (what you see is what you get), at least on average: a positive yield spread represents a required ex ante risk premium that tends to be subsequently realized, on average.

The above interpretations are alternatives within the rational paradigm: one extreme is associated with risk-neutral investors (all assets have the same expected return), the other with risk-averse investors and time-varying risk premia. The empirical analysis discussed below can distinguish whether wide yield spreads tend to predict "offsetting" future changes in rates (or prices) or, instead, high near-term returns, but they leave the question of rationality open.

# 22.2 FUTURE EXCESS RETURNS OR MARKET EXPECTATIONS?

To gain algebraic intuition to the information in yield spreads (forward rates), it is useful to decompose the realized return on any investment into two parts:

$$
\text {T o t a l} \approx \text {C a r r y} + \text {C a p i t a l} (\text {l o s s e s}) \tag {22.1}
$$ where "carry" refers to income return (more broadly: return in an unchanged capital market scenario) and capital gains refer to percentage price changes due to changing capital market conditions. Loosely speaking, the two components reflect expected and unexpected returns. (However, for equities, it is important to recall that expected returns also include prospective capital gains related to cash flow growth; see Table 21.1.)


For currencies, carry reflects the short-rate differential while capital gains reflect spot exchange rate changes. For government bonds, carry is yield income while capital gains are well proxied by duration times yield decline. For equities, carry reflects dividend yield while capital gains reflect stock price changes. For commodity futures, carry reflects the so-called roll effect (as well as the riskless rate on the futures margin).

$FX$  example. Any pair of assets earns the same total realized returns (over some holding period) only if one asset's initial carry advantage is exactly offset by capital losses caused by subsequent rate/price moves. This is a logical statement and an accounting identity—not a hypothesis. Starting with a currency example, if one-year U.S. deposits offer an interest rate  $3\%$  higher than that offered by Japanese deposits, the dollar-yen exchange rate must depreciate by  $3\%$  over the next year to make both deposits earn the same return (and their long-short position to break even). Forward exchange rates are break-even future exchange rates; in this example the one-year forward rate is  $3\%$  weaker than the spot dollar-yen rate.


The break-even concept is useful for investors but may be abused—when forward break-even rates are uncritically treated as the market's rate expectations. The problem is apparent when we solve (22.1) for carry, and restate the equation in expectational form: carry reflects expected near-term asset returns (including required risk premia) as well as expected capital losses due to the market's rate expectations:

$$

\text {C a r r y} \approx E (\text {t o t a l r e t u r n}) - E (\text {c a p i t a l g a i n s / l o s s e s f r o m r a t e c h a n g e s}) \tag {22.2}

$$

For the currency example, let us study the relation between log spot and forward exchange rates  $S$  and  $F$ . (Certain mathematical and statistical analyses are easier when we use log exchange rates. We can deal with additive relations rather than ratios and multiplicative relations. The same is true for expressing bond yields as continuously compounded or log rates.) Covered interest arbitrage ensures that the forward premium  $F - S$  is proportional to the cross-country deposit rate differential (carry). However, it does not follow that the break-even forward rate equals the market expected future spot rate. The latter statement is only a hypothesis—uncovered interest parity (UIP)—which is valid in a risk-neutral world where expected returns on deposits are equal in all countries. (I ignore here a small nonlinear effect called Siegel's paradox or Jensen's inequality.)

Macroeconomists embraced the UIP hypothesis for decades, despite contradictory empirical evidence that high-yielding currencies tend to outperform low-yielding currencies. These UIP violations were dubbed the forward premium puzzle (see Chapter 13). Another classic finding, that spot exchange rates behave empirically like a random walk, actually supports a carry-oriented alternative hypothesis to UIP. If spot exchange rate changes are unpredictable (so that the best forecast of the future spot rate is the current one), then any initial yield advantage will be retained, on average, and will not tend to be offset by future depreciation.

Conceptually, we can add and subtract the unobservable market expectation of the future spot rate,  $E(S)$ , to and from the currency carry measure  $(F - S)$ . Equation (22.3) then shows that, as in (22.2), carry reflects two components: expected excess return  $[F - E(S)]$  and expected future change in the spot rate  $[E(\Delta S)]$ :

$$

F - S \approx [ F - E (S) ] + [ E (S) - S ] \tag {22.3}

$$

In Section 22.3, I review the literature and run empirical tests ("horse races") to see whether positive carry predicts high future excess returns (as the random walk hypothesis implies) or large future spot rate changes (as the UIP hypothesis implies).

IR example. For government bonds (and interest rate swaps), the implied forward curve next year shows how much the yield curve has to shift to make all bonds earn the same return as a one-year bond (presumably deemed riskless by an investor with a one-year time horizon). Thus, if such a curve is realized in a year's time, all bond positions financed at the one-year rate will exactly break even. If the yield curve is upward sloping, longer bonds have an initial yield advantage over the riskless bond, which gives them some cushion against capital losses caused by rising yields. Forward yield quantifies the size of this break-even cushion. If actual bond yields one year hence turn out to be higher than those implied by today's forward rates, then longer bonds will underperform the one-year bond.


Here, too, we often hear observers say that "the market expects rising rates" when forwards imply rising rates. I repeat: break-even forward curves represent the market's rate expectations only if risk premia are zero. The pure expectations hypothesis (PEH) is a close cousin of the UIP hypothesis. It presumes that investors are risk neutral, with the result that all bonds have the same near-term expected return, regardless of their maturity. In the assumed absence of risk premia, yield differentials across the curve only reflect market expectations of future yield changes (so that expected capital gains or losses exactly offset the impact of initial yield differences) [1]. However, it is a mistake to assume the absence of risk premia.

Equation (22.4) shows an approximate accounting identity that decomposes curve steepness into rate change expectation and risk premium components (cf. equation 9.7), analogous to the components in the two previous equations:

Curve steepness  $\approx$  Required bond risk premium

$$
+ \text {E x p e c t e d} \tag {22.4}
$$

An identity is not a theory. The ex post counterpart of the ex ante statement in (22.4) is a tautology. It simply says that if the yield curve is upward sloping, either bond yields rise enough to cause capital losses that offset their positive carry or longer bonds earn higher realized returns than short bonds. If it is not one (with future rate changes "offsetting" yield advantage), it must be the other (realized return advantage). The economic content of the identity in (22.4) comes from assuming that investor expectations are rational and consistent: given an upward-sloping yield curve, investors in the aggregate either expect rising yields to offset positive carry or they expect (and presumably require) a return advantage for longer bonds.

In reality, forward rates (curve steepness) likely contain a mixture of two components—the market's expectations of rate changes and required risk premia. Yet, studying the extremes—the PEH and the risk premium (or random walk) hypothesis—is informative because each argues that only one component matters (see Table 22.1), enabling us to focus on that component and understand it more deeply.

# Detour into roll

The distinction between carry as running yield income and capital gains as price changes in (22.1) is muddled whenever price changes are highly predictable. One obvious example is a short-term Treasury bill that is issued at a discount; the bill earns its fully predictable return through price appreciation, accruing toward par. Similarly, any discount or premium coupon bond earns part of its return (the part in addition to coupon income) via the "pull-to-par" effect as the bond ages. A bond's yield to maturity captures this feature despite its other shortcomings. Thus for bonds it is often reasonable to use yield to maturity for carry and "duration times yield decline" for capital gains.

Table 22.1. The implications of two hypotheses about yield curve behavior

<table><tr><td></td><td>Pure expectations hypothesis</td><td>Risk premium hypothesis</td></tr><tr><td>What is the information in forward rates (yield curve steepness)?</td><td>Market&#x27;s rate expectations</td><td>Required bond risk premia</td></tr><tr><td>What future events should forward rates forecast?</td><td>Future interest rate changes</td><td>Near-term return differentials across bonds</td></tr><tr><td>What is the best predictor of a 5-year zero-coupon bond&#x27;s 1-year return?</td><td>The 1-year riskless spot rate</td><td>The 5-year zero&#x27;s “rolling yield” (which is also the 1-year forward rate after 4 years)</td></tr><tr><td>What is the best predictor of next year&#x27;s spot yield curve?</td><td>Implied spot yield curve one year forward</td><td>Current spot yield curve</td></tr></table>

There are obviously more sophisticated ways to analyze any asset but these back-of-the-envelope calculations are useful shortcuts.

Roll or slide is another nuanced aspect of carry. The random walk hypothesis assumes that the current yield curve is the best predictor of the future yield curve. If an upward-sloping yield curve remains unchanged over the next year, a long-term bond's yield income advantage over the one-year bond will be augmented by capital gains through a "rolldown" effect. For example, if the current 4-year rate is 20 bp lower than the current 5-year rate, the assumption of an unexpected yield curve implies that the bond's yield will fall by 20 bp simply as a result of aging and rolling down the yield curve. The consequent "rolldown return" gives roughly a  $0.8\%$  capital gain (a 4-year duration times a 20 bp yield decline) even if the constant maturity yield curve is unchanged. We can calculate a broader carry measure that incorporates both yield income and rolldown return; it is called "rolling yield" and reflects return in an unchanged curve scenario. (All I have done here is to describe mechanically how expected returns are realized in a random walk world, but the empirical analyses in Section 22.3 will show that a random walk is not a bad base assumption.)

Rolling yield indicates the return of a government bond in an unchanged capital markets environment, specifically one in which the yield curve is unchanged. There are other examples where an asset's own characteristics change over time in an unchanged capital markets environment, resulting in yield or price changes merely due to the passage of time:

- Credit spread curves are often mildly upward sloping. Under such conditions, a corporate bond can outperform the government bond beyond its yield spread by "rolling down the spread curve". This feature boosts the corporate bond's expected return in excess of the duration-matched Treasury; in addition, both bonds can benefit from the "rolling down the yield curve" effect if the Treasury curve is upward sloping.
- When the term structure of commodity futures prices is in backwardation (downward sloping), commodity futures tend to "roll up the curve" as they approach expiration. (Note that both rolling down the yield curve and rolling up the price curve imply rolldown price gains.)

- Options losing their time value have a similar characteristic: prices change simply due to the passage of time. This time decay makes long option positions "a wasting asset". Short option positions make money if markets are stable and lose money in large market moves.

Let us go back to equation (22.1) and approximate the "Carry + Capital gains" expression with "Expected return + Unexpected return". Rolling yield is a broad measure of carry that includes expected return from highly predictable price changes. Under the risk premium hypothesis, unexpected returns reflect capital gains or losses from unexpected rate changes. To break even, any position with a positive expected return must suffer exactly offsetting unexpected capital losses (same size, opposite sign) during the evaluation period. If subsequent market moves tend to be smaller than the breakeven analysis implies, positive-carry strategies add value in the long run. This question can only be addressed empirically, and I turn to it next.

# 22.3 EMPIRICAL HORSE RACES FOR VARIOUS ASSETS

Do forwards reflect the market's rate expectations, required risk premia, or both? Are forward rates or current spot rates better forecasts of future spot rates? Are expected returns equal across assets or do yield spreads predict near-term differences in returns? I now evaluate these questions empirically. The UIP and the PEH are mere hypotheses, and pretty extreme at that, with the truth almost certainly between these extremes, on the one hand, and the opposite extreme risk premium hypotheses, on the other hand. The evidence I present below contradicts the extreme assumption of equal expected returns across assets. I stress how analogous questions can be evaluated in virtually all asset markets, with some modifications.

Where possible, I use so-called complementary regressions that effectively split the predictive ability in ex ante yield differentials (forward-spot premia) into future rate or price changes and near-term return differentials (see Fama-Bliss, 1987). I regress future realized asset price or rate changes on the changes implied by forward rates and I regress future excess asset returns on forward-spot premia. In these "complementary regressions" the regression slopes must sum to one.

# Currencies

I discussed UIP and its violations in Section 22.2, while Chapter 13 focused on the long-run success of the FX carry strategy, so I will be brief here. Table 22.2 shows the slope coefficients of complementary regressions for several dollar pairs, using monthly data over 32 years. As numerous studies since the 1980s have shown, when future exchange rate changes are regressed on forward-implied changes, slope coefficients tend to be negative, thereby contradicting the UIP (which predicts a slope of 1 in the first column). The "wrong" (negative) sign implies that, on average, the carry advantage of a higher yielding currency has been augmented by subsequent currency appreciation instead of being offset by subsequent depreciation. It follows that slope coefficients in the second regression type—future excess returns on yield differentials—exceed one.

The complementary regressions presume that the split between the yield spread's ability to forecast future rate changes and its ability to predict near-term returns is

Table 22.2. Regression coefficients when predicting future-spot exchange rate changes and excess currency returns on initial carry advantage (exchange rate forward-spot premium), 1978-2009

<table><tr><td></td><td>dFX</td><td>excFXret</td></tr><tr><td>EUR</td><td>-0.7</td><td>1.7</td></tr><tr><td>GBP</td><td>-2.2</td><td>3.2</td></tr><tr><td>CHF</td><td>-1.2</td><td>2.2</td></tr><tr><td>JPY</td><td>-2.6</td><td>3.6</td></tr><tr><td>CAD</td><td>-0.8</td><td>1.8</td></tr></table>

Sources: Bloomberg, Citigroup, own calculations.

constant. Unpublished subperiod analysis suggests some time variation although this might reflect sampling variability. Rolling 10-year slope coefficients when regressing excess currency returns on carry do consistently exceed zero and even one, suggesting that the assumption of the risk premium hypothesis (or random walk hypothesis, slope  $= 1$ ) has been quite conservative. Empirically, the horse race is not really between the UIP and random walk hypotheses (slopes 0 and 1) but between the latter hypothesis and some more aggressive pro-carry story; the UIP is rejected.

# Interest rates

I now turn to complementary regressions in the Treasury market using monthly data over 1972-2009. The PEH implies slope coefficients of 1 and 0 in the two regressions while the risk premium hypothesis implies coefficients 0 and 1. Leibowitz-Bader-Kogelman (1995) label the slope coefficient on the regression of excess return on carry as the persistence factor (PF). The idea is that if the current yield curve has a strong tendency to persist (remain unchanged), carry tends to be earned, and  $\mathrm{PF} = 1$ . If, instead, the current yield curve gives way to the forward curve, there are no predictable excess returns, and  $\mathrm{PF} = 0$ .

The results in Table 22.3 are almost as devastating for the PEH as they were for the UIP in the currency markets. Yield change regressions have the "wrong" sign—a pattern Frederick Macaulay noted as early as 1938—in that upward-sloping yield curves tend to be followed by subsequent yield declines. Since consequent capital gains augment any rolling yield advantage that long bonds have, the PFs exceed 1. For the 10-year maturity, the PF is  $+2.5$ . The correlation between curve steepness (or, in jargon, the forward-implied yield change) and next quarter change in the 10-year yield is  $-0.13$  while the correlation between curve steepness and next quarter excess bond return is  $+0.21$ . Shorter maturity bonds have lower PFs. Other studies show that long-term

Table 22.3. Regression coefficients when predicting future Treasury yield changes and excess bond returns on initial carry advantage (interest rate forward-spot premium), 1972-2009

<table><tr><td></td><td>dY</td><td>excBondRet</td></tr><tr><td>U.S. 2 year</td><td>-0.2</td><td>1.2</td></tr><tr><td>U.S. 5 year</td><td>-0.6</td><td>1.6</td></tr><tr><td>U.S. 10 year</td><td>-1.5</td><td>2.5</td></tr></table>

Sources: Bloomberg, Citigroup, Federal Reserve Board, own calculations.

changes in short-dated assets behave more in line with the PEH than short-term changes in long-dated assets.

Again, complementary regressions presume that there is a constant split between the yield curve's ability to forecast future rate changes and its ability to forecast near-term returns. Subperiod analysis suggests some time variation, a tendency of coefficients to decline over time. Coefficients have almost always been positive, fluctuating around  $\mathrm{PF} = 1$ , a finding broadly consistent with the risk premium hypothesis.

In unpublished analysis, I find that the results for German Bunds (government bonds) are comparable with those for U.S. Treasuries. Curve steepness has over time become a less effective predictor of future returns in duration-timing models and curve-trading models. Yet, carry-oriented strategies across G10 bond markets (overweighting currency-hedged bonds in countries with steep curves and underweighting them in countries with inverted or flat yield curves) have achieved good long-run SRs.

# Corporate credits and interest rate swaps

For credit spreads, I ask whether the (option-adjusted) spread reflects expected future default losses and expected spread widening (which, when combined, would exactly offset the initial carry advantage) or, instead, a true expected return advantage for bearing default and liquidity risks. There is again the PEH-like proposition in which risk-neutral investors bid down all assets' expected returns to the same level. According to this story, the credit spread shows how large the combined effects of defaults and spread widening are expected by the market to be, and it tells nothing about future return differentials across assets. The opposite hypothesis is that all of the credit spread reflects default and liquidity premia—an ex ante return advantage that investors should get to hold, on average; this hypothesis cannot be literally true if there are any losses from default.

For reasons I have already suggested, neither of these extreme hypotheses is likely to be true in practice. First, historical average default losses are much lower than historical average yield spreads, suggesting that part of the spread reflects an ex ante premium. Second, there is evidence of time variation in credit premia. The correlation between the Lehman corporate credit index spread and subsequent excess return of credits over Treasuries is  $+0.12$  using monthly data and  $+0.48$  using annual data (both covering 1973-2009). The rolling 10-year correlation has been positive virtually throughout the sample period.

# Equities

Here the two polar hypotheses are that high equity "yields" reflect either market expectations of slower future growth rates or higher required risk premia. Yields and matching growth rates can refer to dividends or a broader measure of cash payouts, but any of these measures, if it is to be useful, ultimately translates to the growth rate of the stock price. In a risk-neutral environment, any asset with an income advantage should have lower expected capital gains (growth) so that total expected returns are equalized across assets.

In a market-timing context, we can evaluate equity market yields as they vary over time. Complementary regressions then ask, for example, whether high dividend yields (i.e., low market valuations) predict a low future dividend growth rate or high future equity returns. As Cochrane (2008a) stresses, the assumptions needed to imply that dividend yields must forecast either of them are quite weak. Since there is little evidence of dividend growth predictability, he concludes that dividend yields must primarily predict future returns. Evidence from the pre-World War II period and from outside the U.S. is more equivocal on the information content of dividend yield, however.

In a cross-sectional context, we can compare dividend yield, earnings yield, or other valuation multiples between individual stocks (or between sectors or countries). The polar hypotheses are whether the yield gap between "value" and "growth" stocks primarily reflects different expected earnings growth rates (and ultimately different price appreciation rates that offset any initial yield advantage) or different required returns. Empirically, it is clear that value stocks tend to earn higher long-run returns. It is also true that growth stocks tend to grow faster than value stocks in the early years—but the growth gap is not as strong or as persistent as the valuation gap implies. Thus the data are more supportive of the value managers' story that cheap stocks outperform in the long run, than with the growth managers' story that the fast future appreciation of growth stocks justifies their high valuations.

# Commodities

The term structure of commodity futures prices can be in backwardation (downward sloping) or in contango (upward sloping). Backwardation, in which the spot price exceeds the futures price,  $S > F$ , implies that the convenience yield for holding the spot commodity exceeds storage and financing costs. Steeply inverted price curves tend to coincide with low inventories, as scarcity temporarily boosts the spot price. Conversely, upward-sloping price curves tend to coincide with high inventories, abundant supply, and a historically low spot price.

In parallel with the twin hypotheses above, the shape of the commodity term structure may reflect expected changes in the spot price or risk premia. Scarcity-induced spikes in the spot price may well be deemed temporary, causing an expectation of price decline. However, if the term structure remains unchanged as time passes, an exceptionally large gap between the spot price and the nearby futures price implies that the futures contract will earn high returns simply because its price drifts (rolls up) toward the high spot price.

Several empirical studies show that backwardated commodities tend to have higher subsequent returns than commodities in contango. Recall from Figure 11.8 that average long-run returns of commodity futures are largely explained by their average roll returns. In addition, I ran some historical regressions using WTI Crude Oil futures since 1987. The data seem split between the expectations and risk premium hypotheses. Steep backwardation predicts falling spot prices (to offset the roll advantage of longer futures); when the subsequent spot price change is regressed on the roll (futures-implied price change), the coefficient is 0.5. Steep backwardation also predicts future excess returns; when next month excess return is regressed on the roll, the slope coefficient (PF) is also 0.5. Some agricultural futures may exhibit even greater predictability of spot price changes, given the seasonally steep backwardation associated with scarcity (low inventories) and short-lived spot price spikes.

# Housing

Rental yield is the most common carry measure in real estate markets. The analogue to the twin hypotheses above is that relatively low rental yield may reflect either market expectations of faster price appreciation or lower required total returns. Some empirical studies suggest that low rental yields (say, in "hot" cities) coincide with persistently and predictably faster house price appreciation. Other evidence links low rental yields with low future returns.


# Volatility

Comparing options with different maturities, an inverted volatility term structure may not only predict falling volatility (typically market expectations of mean reversion from high volatility levels) but may also signal abnormally high rewards for volatility selling. Carr-Wu (2009) regress future realized variances on variance swap rates (separately for various stock indices and some individual stocks), testing the expectations hypothesis. Coefficient values are typically below one, suggesting that a time-varying volatility premium also plays a role. High prices for variance swaps predict high future realized volatilities but also an abnormally high reward for volatility selling. I find a similar split between PEH-like and risk premium hypotheses in the FX market when I study implied volatilities for the EUR/USD exchange rate.

# 22.4 CONCLUSIONS

Empirical evidence clearly shows that carry measures are better at predicting near-term asset returns than at forecasting future rate or price changes that would offset initial yield advantage. Yield-seeking strategies have been profitable in all asset classes studied. Thus, risk premium hypotheses are pretty well supported by data, while hypotheses relying on risk-neutral investors (UIP, PEH, and other expectations hypotheses) fare poorly.

However, this verdict may be too critical of expectations hypotheses. In many cases, the data partly support an expectations hypothesis. Even when they do not—recall the empirical violations of the PEH in interest rate markets—the outcome could reflect investor irrationality and expectational errors. As stressed above, empirical evidence of return predictability cannot distinguish between rational and irrational explanations. When we use information from consensus forecasts to proxy for the market's rate expectations, near-term return predictability appears to be due more to biased investor expectations than to time-varying risk premia (see Chapter 23). In addition, my analysis of yield curve behavior in Section 9.4 suggests that mean-reverting rate expectations are a key driver of the changing yield curve shape over time. Overall, I conclude that carry reflects both required risk premia and the market's rate and/or price expectations.

Investors should not seek yield too blindly. One reason is that yield-seekers often move in a crowd and thus are vulnerable to crowding and de-leveraging risks. Another pitfall is that if a country gradually drifts toward hyperinflation, and market expectations capture this prospect, mechanical yield-seeking investors will maintain long currency or interest rate positions until they have lost all their purchasing power. Finally, naive yield-seekers are prey to abuse by ruthless sellers. Some products have high notional yields and yet low prospective returns, due to high default risk, embedded options, or other complex features that conceal elevated risks and costs.

# 22.5 NOTES

[1] In theory, expected returns may be equal across assets if risk premia are bid to zero and assets are fairly priced. In a risk-neutral world, all assets have the same expected return, and that return is the riskless rate (expected return on the riskless asset). Not all investors need to be risk neutral; it is sufficient to assume that there are some risk-neutral arbitrageurs who are both extremely smart (can correctly identify any ex ante opportunities) and rich (have infinitely deep pockets so that they won't be stopped out if trades go temporarily against them). Such arbitrageurs would take advantage of any expected return differentials and remove them. In reality, even the most risk-tolerant investors—hedge funds, private equity funds, and sovereign wealth funds—are risk averse (just less so than other investors), make mistakes, and face capital constraints. These investor groups' profit-seeking activities have narrowed expected return differentials across assets—but they have not fully eliminated them.
The risk neutrality assumption is central in derivatives-pricing literature. Relative-pricing models, starting from the Black-Scholes-Merton option-pricing model, rely on arbitrage arguments and do not concern themselves with the level of expected asset returns. It is convenient to assume risk-neutral pricing even if risk neutrality is a poor description of the real world. There is no contradiction because derivative-pricing formulas do not rely on the risk neutrality of real-world investors. Using arbitrage arguments and certain assumptions, it is possible to show that derivative instruments, traded together with underlying assets, give riskless payoffs and thus should earn the same return as the riskless asset. One can compute relative derivatives prices in a risk-neutral world and expect these "arbitrage-free" prices to hold even in the real world. The beauty of the Black-Scholes-Merton model and its descendants is that no information on expected returns of underlying assets (stocks, bonds) is needed for pricing and hedging derivatives. Conversely, these models tell us nothing about those expected returns.
[2] The irrational paradigm allows a third alternative interpretation: that spread reflects the market's rate expectations but that these are systematically biased. The stories of biased expectations and time-varying risk premia have the same empirical implications: high yields tend to predict high near-term returns. Because expectations and risk premia are not directly observable, we cannot empirically distinguish between these two stories. Thus, even if historical analysis shows that yield spreads predict near-term returns, it cannot tell whether the time variation in expected returns is caused by irrational market expectations or by required risk premia. (We can address this question only if we take survey consensus forecasts as a proxy for the market's rate expectations.)
[3] I discuss these issues in Section 7.1 and in Ilmanen (1996). Given the broader audience for this book, I focus here on the loose concept of curve steepness (or term spread). I leave out various details, including a more exact treatment of forward rates.

Source notes. For interpreting yield spreads, see Fama (1984) and Fama-Bliss (1987) who pioneered complementary regressions that explore whether yield spreads better predict future rate changes or excess returns (also see Ilmanen, 1996).
