For comparable empirical analyses in equities and volatilities, see Cochrane (2008a) and Carr-Wu (2009). It was Leibowitz-Bader-Kogelman (1995) who came up with the term "persistence factor".

# 23

# Survey-based subjective expected returns

- Survey-based expected returns provide useful real-time information about investor views.
- Representativeness and timeliness problems can cause survey forecasts to be noisy proxies of the market's expectations.
- Consensus forecasts may not be great predictors of the future and may not even be rational. This would not preclude a survey from being unbiased and representative, as the same criticism can apply to market forecasts.
- If survey evidence is taken at face value, it can help distinguish between rational and irrational interpretations of return predictability.
Retail survey views of prospective equity returns tend to be procyclical, in contrast to countercyclical estimates from market valuation indicators.

The market's expected returns and rate expectations are not directly observable (with the exception of the prospective return of a horizon-matching default-free bond). Under the rational paradigm, any evidence that econometric forecasting models or valuation measures can predict future returns is interpreted as time variation in the market's reasoned return expectations. Empirical forecasting and/or valuation signals typically indicate that objectively feasible future equity market returns are low after several years of high realized returns and rising valuation ratios. This could reflect rational investors accepting lower expected returns due to lower perceived risk or it could reflect risk aversion in good times. However, an irrational alternative explanation is that systematic forecasting error or time-varying sentiment causes return predictability. Consensus forecasts are one way to proxy market expectations; retail surveys, especially, suggest that subjective investor expectations are irrationally high after extended booms and low following downturns. Hence the tension between objective and subjective expected return concepts: many academic studies see (objectively) high expected returns when investors are at their most pessimistic while most laypeople associate (subjectively) high expected returns with bull market peaks.

I take surveys more seriously than most academics have done; fortunately, there is growing interest in this area. Given the importance of time-varying expected returns I think that survey-based premia are often the most honest measures of subjective real-time ex ante premia. Surveys are likely to be roughly right because they are timely, simple, and intuitive—evidence "straight from the horse's mouth". In contrast, more complex and formal approaches—inflaming market expectations based on theoretical or statistical models—are sometimes unrealistic and use hindsight that was not available to investors in real time [1].

Note that surveys may be poor predictors of future asset price moves or future economic developments because forecasting such events is inherently difficult. Professional forecasters are an easy target for critics such as Nassim Taleb and James Montier. Others criticize survey forecasts for being inefficient and irrational. Even if this is true, the same criticism may apply to markets. Thus, even if their predictive record is no better than that of a coin toss, survey-based consensus forecasts may still be unbiased and representative proxies of market views.

# Box 23.1. Problems with survey data

- Unbiasedness/Representativeness. Some economists ignore survey data because talk is cheap, while market prices reflect people putting their money where their mouth is. Gaming and dishonest responses are possible, but inattentiveness seems a greater incentive problem to me. How unbiased or representative are surveys of the views they are supposed to be reflecting? Most individual biases wash out in the consensus view. Some surveys are systematically biased for a reason: analysts' overoptimistic earnings forecasts reflect incentive or agency issues, and corporations' overoptimistic pension return assumptions make it possible to manipulate earnings. Such forecasts have been rightly criticized in the past decade. Many other surveys may be unbiased but may not be representative. I think economist polls are representative for predicting macro-fundamentals and short-term rates but less so for predicting long-term yields, let alone equities and other risky assets. It is conceptually not even clear what survey would be representative of the equity market—certainly not a democratic poll of the whole population, but perhaps a wealth-weighted poll of professional investors.
- Timeliness/Staleness problems. Many surveys ask about future market prices and/ or yields. To translate these point forecasts into predicted returns, survey forecasts need to be compared with market prices or yields at the time of the survey. Such date matching is inexact because institutional forecasters often use the institution's latest published forecast, which can be stale by the survey date.
- Mean/Median/Mode. Outlier responses in surveys often reflect data errors or ignorant participants. The simplest remedy is to use the median response among survey participants, instead of the mean, as the consensus forecast. Another problem is that many survey participants report their base case scenario (the mode, or most likely outcome) instead of a probability-weighted average of many scenarios. If most respondents agree on the base scenario and on the most important asymmetric risk scenario, the survey may not capture this asymmetry. For example, U.S. long-run inflation forecasts have been well-anchored near  $2.5\%$  since the late 1990s but the stability in published forecasts may conceal how the main risk has shifted over time between deflation and inflation concerns. New European data show a positive relation between the inflation risk premium and the skewness of inflation forecasts.

The Philadelphia Fed has published annually (since 1992) a survey of expected 10-year returns of Treasury bills, bonds, and stocks, as well as 10-year inflation. From these series we can also infer estimates of the bond risk premium over bills (BRP) and the equity risk premium over bonds (ERP). The narrowing gap between the middle two lines in Figure 23.1 suggests a decline in BRP from  $2.5\%$  to a low of  $0.5\%$  in 2007, followed by a rebound (one that is not evident in the blue-chip surveys discussed below). The ERP has been more stable but only around  $2\%$ , except for a spike in 1994. This is an exceptional survey in that it polls respondents directly about the expected returns of each asset class. The professional forecasters that are surveyed are typically economists; I would think that they are reasonably representative of fixed income investors but less representative of equity investors.

Figure 23.1. Long-term expected asset returns: Median forecasts in the Survey of Professional Forecasters.
![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-22/aa485235-5155-4ade-b88b-be1df0035d75/029512d2ddc1c7082928688dfa74b1de521f58c6a25d270baf4c2b2f25a8ee73.jpg)
Source: Federal Reserve Bank of Philadelphia.


Section 8.5 reviews evidence regarding professional and retail investors' subjective equity return forecasts. Retail investors' return forecasts appear to be overly extrapolative and optimistic (especially after bull markets). The three retail surveys discussed in Section 8.5 used different investment horizons and covered different periods between 1998 and 2006. In the University of Michigan survey of wealthy investors, return forecasts are higher following strong market returns and during perceived good economic times, and lower following equity downturns and during recessions. This procyclical pattern contrasts with the countercyclical pattern found in rational return expectations that are based on econometric forecasting models and valuation ratios. Another study finds revealing differences across investors around the turn of the millennium: younger investors, less experienced investors, and those with better investment success were more optimistic. Professional investors and CFOs have given forecasts more in line with forward-looking market valuation indicators. Notably, survey forecasts of the long-term equity premium rose between 2007 and 2008 as markets sold off.

Unlike the ERP, the survey-based BRP introduced in Chapter 9 is not directly queried in surveys but is the difference between market yield and the consensus forecast of future interest rates. My main data source for the BRP is the Blue Chip Economic Indicators survey, published semiannually since 1983, which unfortunately only covers decades with falling inflation and yields. To complement the BRP for the 10-year Treasury, Figure 23.2 also shows BRPs from money markets, contrasting three different survey forecasts—the first of which (Goldsmith-Nagan) dates back to 1969—with matching Treasury bill forward rates [2]. Although details vary, all of the series share similar contours. BRPs trended higher during the 1970s, peaked between 1979 and 1985, and then trended lower for 20 years. The 10-year BRP is the series I highlighted in Chapter 9 as my favorite measure of ex ante reward for bearing duration risk.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-22/aa485235-5155-4ade-b88b-be1df0035d75/7fee2b91df5aa01ab47296f9a734994d611b3c5760b3b786f33ef402c883caa7.jpg)
Figure 23.2. Various survey-based bond risk premia, 1969-2009. Sources: Goldsmith-Nagan (GN) Bond and Money Market Letter (courtesy Kenneth Froot), Wall Street Journal (WSJ), Consensus Economics (CE), Blue Chip (BC) Economic Indicators, Citigroup, Federal Reserve Board, own calculations.


The above exercise takes survey-based consensus forecasts at face value as if they exactly represented market expectations at the survey time. In reality, there are measurement errors (depending on the representativeness and timeliness of the survey) but these can be lessened. Other methods to extract expected returns also involve noise (as well as hindsight not present in survey data). The noise in theoretical or empirical model-based expected returns is related to the empirical validity of the particular assumptions behind the model. The tradeoff between measurement errors in surveys, on the one hand, and specification and estimation errors in model-based approaches, on the other hand, is an under-researched topic. Perhaps the best approach is to combine both types of data as Kim-Orphanides (2007) do when studying term premia: using a theoretical term structure model but using survey data as one admittedly noisy input—an input that is not available if one is restricted to using only market yield data.

Besides consensus forecasts, survey evidence about uncertainty, disagreement, and asymmetric views may contain information about future asset returns.

Survey evidence—if taken seriously—can help distinguish between rational and irrational explanations for asset return predictability. New studies by Bacchetta-Mertens-van Wincoop (2009) and Piazzesi-Schneider (2008) suggest that much of the observed asset return predictability in classic predictive regressions is not related to ex ante (survey-based) risk premia but rather to systematic forecasting errors—or perhaps just to overfitting/hindsight by the econometrician running the classic predictive regressions.


Finally, I mention one important set of long-run expected returns: expert views on multiple asset classes. I am not aware of efforts to collect these views from several contributors, but here are two famous examples that are regularly published. The Yale Endowment (2009) assumes  $6\%$  long-run real returns for developed market stocks ( $8\%$  for emerging markets),  $2\%$  for fixed income,  $6\%$  for real assets, and  $11\%$  for private equity (incorporating an assumption that Yale can pick good managers). These forecasts are very slow moving and do not appear very sensitive to market valuations. In contrast, the GMO Quarterly Letter's seven-year asset class forecasts are highly dependent on perceived valuations: the latest forecasts in Grantham (2010) predict  $2.9\%$  annual real returns for U.S. large-cap stocks,  $4.9\%$  for non-U.S. large-caps, and  $6.6\%$  for emerging market stocks. Small-cap stocks are predicted to fare less well and high-quality stocks better. Government bonds are expected to deliver near  $0\%$  annual real returns, but  $2.2\%$  for emerging market bonds.

# 23.1 NOTES

[1] Overall, I want to encourage central banks, sell-side firms, and other financial institutions to conduct and publish more investor surveys. Time series of such surveys—standalone or linked to market data—can help us better analyze and understand asset market movements and valuations. In my old sell-side strategist role, each quarter I polled  $100+$  global investors on their long-term inflation and interest rate views.
[2] Specifically, the *Blue Chip* forecast of next decade average Treasury bill rate is compared with the 10-year Treasury yield. Goldsmith-Nagan survey forecasts of 3-month rates three months hence are compared each quarter with 3-month-forward 3-month Treasury bill rates implied in market pricing. Likewise, the *Wall Street Journal*'s semiannual survey forecasts of 6-month Treasury bill rates six months hence are compared with 6-month-forward 6-month Treasury bill rates. Finally, *Consensus Economics* survey forecasts of 3-month rates three and 12 months hence are used to estimate the expected average money market rate for the year beyond the first three months. This expectation is then compared with the matching forward rate. The advantage of the last approach is that the results are less sensitive to the assumed timing of the survey, when the survey forecast is compared with market forward rates from a particular day. While current yield levels can vary quite a bit day to day, spreads among different maturity rates are more stable. I use term structure data from the Federal Reserve Board and from Citigroup to estimate forward rates for Treasury bills.

Source notes. Survey data are used for polling equity premium views in Welch (2000, 2009), Vissing-Jorgensen (2003), Amromin-Sharpe (2008), and Graham-Harvey (2010), and for extracting better bond premium estimates in Best-Byrne-Ilmanen (1998), Kim-Orphanides (2005), and Garcia-Werner (2010). Also see Beber-Breedon-Buraschi (2010) and Bacchetta-Mertens-van Wincoop (2009). On expert surveys, see Yale (2009) and Grantham (2010).

# 24

# Tactical return forecasting models

- Systematic forecasting models may focus on (1) timing a single asset or an asset pair or (2) selecting a portfolio across a cross-section of assets.
- Such models often use multiple indicators, such as value, carry, and momentum.
- The trading horizon can vary from within-day to a year. A monthly or weekly trading frequency is common.
- Numerous techniques have been applied to return forecasting. In out-of-sample evaluations, simple models often perform just as well as more complex ones.
- Data mining and crowding problems are inevitable. They can be mitigated but not fully avoided.

# 24.1 INTRODUCTION

Valuation indicators are effective in providing a long-horizon (multi-year) view on an asset's prospects. In this chapter, I turn to dynamic trading strategies and forecasting models that have a shorter horizon (one week, month, or quarter). I first describe the generics—model types, assets traded, indicators—and then comment on possible improvements and pitfalls for the systematic trading style. I keep this chapter brief so as to retain some of my proprietary trade secrets, but Chapters 8 through 10 review several publicly known market-timing indicators for equities, duration, and credit, while Chapters 12 through 15 review four popular dynamic trading strategies: equity value, foreign exchange carry, commodity momentum, and volatility selling.

What types of models are used? Single-trade timing models and multi-asset selection models are the two main classes. Dynamic strategies may focus on (1) timing a single asset (say, market timing Treasury bonds) or a single asset pair (say, sector timing the yield spread between corporate credits and Treasuries) or (2) selecting a portfolio of assets from a broader universe. Leveraged investors often construct a long-short portfolio that may be superior to a long-only portfolio because the former hedges away some directional risks.

Which assets are typically traded? Any liquid assets can be traded on a standalone basis (directionally) or on a relative value basis. Liquidity and low trading costs are more important the shorter the trading horizon.

What kind of predictive indicators are used? Both timing models and selection models can use a multitude of indicators. The best known are related to value, carry, and momentum. I discuss various types of models.

# 24.2 WHAT TYPE OF MODEL?

# Timing models

The simplest timing model is a binary trend-following model that goes long or short an asset based on its recent price trend. But there is no need to rely only on the price of the asset itself as an indicator, or for that matter on any single indicator. I illustrate these ideas with a bond market (duration) timing model. One possible single-indicator model is to go long one unit of Treasuries if equity markets have performed poorly in recent months but to sell short one unit if equity markets have been strong. (This model may be called “ $1/ - 1$ ” in Treasury bond units held long/units held short. Another variant—the “ $1/0$ ” trading model—might never sell Treasury bonds short but only invest in cash when faced with a bond-bearish signal.)

Multiple indicators can be used, and each can be expressed as a simple binary (bullish/ bearish) signal, as above, or each can be scaled by current signal strength and/or perceived predictive ability.

There are a number of ways to aggregate multiple indicators into a composite market-timing signal. The simplest approach involves averaging individual indicators using equally weighted binary signals. For example, Figure 24.1 shows the monthly signals and cumulative profits of a duration-timing model whose composite signal (and thus active Treasury position) is an average of three  $1 / -1$  trading signals—bullish if equities have lost money in the past six months, if bond market momentum is positive, or if bond carry is positive. This particular duration-timing strategy achieved an SR of 0.66 and a hit rate of  $60\%$  (i.e., the correct sign in  $60\%$  of months).

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-22/aa485235-5155-4ade-b88b-be1df0035d75/9c69cf58d67e735da2f8c5a9a878f4fe17f6452a8b2a6924d10d20c84efdea68.jpg)
Figure 24.1. Signals and performance of a bond-market-timing strategy, 1978-2009. Sources: Bloomberg, Citigroup, own calculations.

A slightly smarter timing model is based on a straightforward linear regression approach. We can identify a set of predictive indicators with which we forecast the next period (say, next month) excess return of Treasuries over cash. Predicting excess returns makes sense because timing trades often involve a self-financed long or short position in an underlying asset or an equivalent derivatives position [1]. Mechanically, we just regress monthly excess Treasury returns on a set of indicators that are available at the beginning of the month—preferably using at least five years of monthly data. The fitted value in this regression is predicted excess Treasury return for next month. One approach is to size the position each month according to this fitted value. To evaluate the model's past track record, we can study the correlation of these fitted values with actual monthly excess returns; alternatively, we can compute the hit rate (how often the model predicted the correct sign of next month market direction) or, most practically, the Sharpe ratio of a trading rule based on the model's prescribed positions.

We can strengthen our understanding of the regression model's mechanics if we normalize all indicators before running the forecasting regression. (Normalizing or "Z-scoring" involves subtracting the sample mean from each series and then dividing by sample standard deviation.) Such a modified regression has the same fitted values as before but now the contribution of each predictor is in units that are quite intuitive. The regression coefficient (multiple regression beta) of each predictor now equals the partial correlation between the predictor and next month excess Treasury return—a pure statistical measure of predictive ability [2]. Thus, the contribution of each indicator to predicted excess bond return is a product of the "β" and "Z" terms (i.e., the indicator's past predictive ability and its current signal strength). Total predicted excess return can be conveniently decomposed into the sum of these contributions plus the regression intercept. Table 24.1 shows a stylized example.

In this modified regression, the intercept reflects the sample mean of excess bond return. If this sample mean appears too high and misleading for the future—arguably reflecting the strong bond rally that occurred in the past—we can easily constrain this mean to be a lower number and thus reduce "neutral" future excess return (the return forecast that would make us indifferent between bonds and cash). I emphasize that these convenient interpretations of the regression model are possible only after all predictive indicators have been normalized. One interesting extension involves incorporating economic priors into the regression. If there is a strong case for, say, only a positive relation between an indicator (say, curve steepness) and future returns, one can constrain the relevant regression coefficient to be non-negative.

More complex variants of a market-timing model exist, including nonlinear models and data-intensive learning models. Yet complexity does not necessarily enhance performance, and these two approaches—summing up binary signals and using a normalized regression model—are among the most popular in practice. It is worth contrasting how differently the two approaches use the same information set. The first approach only sums up the signs of various predictors; it thus ignores information about each indicator's signal strength, predictive ability, and diversification ability which the regression model captures by incorporating signal strengths and partial correlations. However, the simplicity of the first approach can make it more robust, not an insignificant advantage. Indeed, in real-world comparisons, simple approaches are often competitive with any theoretically superior approaches.

Table 24.1. A stylized example of a predictive regression where positive predicted excess bond return mainly reflects prevailing equity weakness, while rising bond yields give a bearish signal

<table><tr><td></td><td>β: Partial correlation</td><td>Z: Signal strength</td><td>Contribution</td></tr><tr><td>Equity weakness</td><td>0.15</td><td>1.0</td><td>0.15</td></tr><tr><td>Bond momentum</td><td>0.08</td><td>-0.8</td><td>-0.06</td></tr><tr><td>Carry</td><td>0.14</td><td>0.5</td><td>0.07</td></tr><tr><td>Intercept (average excess return)</td><td></td><td></td><td>0.06</td></tr><tr><td>Predicted excess return (sum)</td><td></td><td></td><td>0.22</td></tr></table>

# Cross-asset selection models

Timing models can be extended to analyze pre-selected asset pairs or sector decisions. Typically, a correlated pair is identified and tracked over time; once excessive deviation from historical relations occurs, a mean-reverting long-short trade may be put on to benefit from expected normalization. Many fixed income arbitrage strategies and equity pairs trading strategies (the latter being original statistical arbitrage) were based on this idea, which was implemented using some simple spread analysis or more complex statistical models (e.g., co-integration and error correction) or mathematical models (such as term structure models). Again, it often makes sense to use multiple predictors, and this is commonly done when timing the relative performance of corporate vs. government debt or of growth stocks vs. value stocks.

The above examples involve favoring one of two pre-selected assets. It is of course restrictive to pre-select just two candidate assets. More open asset selection models study a whole cross-section of assets—say, the largest 1,000 stocks in the U.S. equity market, or the G10 currencies—and identify the most and least attractive assets in this universe based on some criteria [3].

If we only use one criterion, we can just sort or rank all assets each period based on that criterion. Two popular approaches—due to their empirical success in recent decades—rank U.S. equities based on some value measure, or the G10 currencies based on carry (see Chapters 12–13). A leveraged long-short strategy would then select some top-ranked assets to buy and some bottom-ranked assets to sell. (A constrained long-only investor may only be able to use the information about the top half. This fact gives a key advantage to unconstrained investors such as hedge funds, and has recently prompted relaxation of the long-only constraint among many previously constrained investors; it has also aided introduction of so-called 130/30 funds.)

Cross-sectional strategies, too, are often based on multiple indicators, and this again complicates analysis. How should the various inputs (criteria) be combined? In practice, there are two common approaches: composite-ranking models and cross-sectional regressions.

A composite-ranking model can be as simple as sorting assets along a set of indicators and then taking an average of an asset's ranks across the various indicators as its "total score". A low total score implies an attractive average rank in many dimensions. For example, G10 currencies may be traded based on their average ranks along carry, momentum, and value criteria. I have used this approach in Ilmanen-Sayood (2002) and noted that it works surprisingly well.

Analogous to the discussion about simple binary models vs. regression models for single-asset timing, this simple ranking approach misses information about signal strength, past predictive ability, and diversification ability in different indicators that would be captured by a more formal optimization model.

Cross-sectional regressions help disentangle many correlated influences on asset returns and identify their distinct rewards. Consider cross-sectional regression of U.S. equity excess returns on various risk factors and other characteristics (say, estimated market beta, market capitalization, book-to-market ratio, and recent price momentum). By construction, the estimated slope coefficients in such cross-sectional regressions reflect the reward for each factor or characteristic while keeping the others constant. That is, the slope coefficient represents the return of a "factor-mimicking portfolio" that has maximal correlation with one factor and zero correlations with all other factors. Such cross-sectional regressions are run each period and then the slope coefficient of each factor or characteristic is averaged over time to give an estimated "pure" premium for the factor or characteristic. Past average factor premia can be used as inputs into portfolio construction—or the expected premia may be forecast using an econometric model. If the model is taken at face value, it gives the expected returns of each asset as a function of the asset's factor exposures or characteristics and estimated expected factor premia. However, these estimates are subject to measurement errors (even if we ignore likely time variation in these premia, many factor exposures are estimated with noise), and to specification errors (the model may omit important factors, the assumption of linear relations may be faulty, etc.).

# Combining models

While I will not review portfolio construction issues in this book beyond the discussion in Chapter 28, I note that so-called Black-Litterman optimizers are particularly well suited for combining information from systematic trading models. Black-Litterman optimizers enable (i) blending historical experience with anchoring priors (such as perceived market equilibrium returns) and/or with active views; (ii) inputting expected return views on particular trades (instead of on each asset separately); and (iii) incorporating a measure of uncertainty for each view. Both inputs from trading model backtests (quality of trading performance, current signal strength) and the user's subjective assessments of model reliability can be included. New studies have extended the classic model beyond the mean variance world.

# 24.3 WHICH ASSETS/TRADES?

Active trading requires liquidity, the more so the higher the trading frequency. It is important to assess the tradeoff between higher gross gains in any dynamic trading strategy and the associated increase in trading costs. High-turnover strategies (fast signals) implemented at high frequencies require either very high gross Sharpe ratios or low trading costs, preferably both. Low-turnover strategies (slow signals) can afford lower gross Sharpe ratios or higher trading costs. For a given rate of turnover, less liquid assets have higher trading costs.

Equities may be traded directionally (market timing) or on a relative value basis (stock selection). Between these two extremes, we can take active positions across countries, across industry sectors, or across styles. Fixed income can likewise be traded directionally (duration timing), on a relative value basis (issue selection) and on a sector basis along many dimensions (maturities, countries, sectors, credit ratings—and mixing these dimensions by, say, boxing government curve flatteners with credit curve steepeners). Commodities are mostly traded directionally but specialists can put on active positions along the futures term structure, across commodities, or between commodity futures and commodity-related stocks or currencies.

While I discuss systematic strategies here, it is important to remember that most investors and traders are discretionary, and there are diverse paths to success. Some hedge funds focus on one market niche (say, emerging markets, distressed debt, or merger arbitrage), while other funds take a broader view and trade across asset classes (global tactical asset allocation, capital structure arbitrage, or convertible arbitrage). Some of the all-time great investors were generalists, but the majority of successful traders are probably discretionary specialists in one area.

# 24.4 WHICH INDICATOR TYPES?

Investors use a large variety of trading signals and there is no consensus how these could best be classified. The most popular themes are value, carry, and momentum, but many other indicators do not belong to these styles. Table 24.2 suggests one plausible classification scheme—price patterns, seasonals, other indicators—and presents examples of each for both time series and cross-sectional trading.

Price patterns (information discernible from the asset's own price). Short-term and/or medium-term momentum as well as long-term reversal are common tendencies of many asset return series—and even of many dynamic strategies. Yet, the exact patterns observed for cross-sectional equity trading do not travel well. That is, few other asset classes share the exact timing observed in equities: relative reversal patterns for the first

Table 24.2. One simple taxonomy of financial market regularities

<table><tr><td></td><td>Time-series predictability</td><td>Cross-sectional predictability</td></tr><tr><td rowspan="2">Price patterns</td><td>Short-term momentum</td><td>Short-term and long-term reversal</td></tr><tr><td>Long-term mean reversion</td><td>Intermediate momentum</td></tr><tr><td>Seasonals</td><td>January, Hallowe&#x27;en, turn of month</td><td>Carry-strong and momentum-poor in January, etc.</td></tr><tr><td rowspan="5">Other indicators</td><td>Value and carry indicators</td><td>Value and carry indicators</td></tr><tr><td>Cyclical and inflation indicators</td><td>Underreactions (profitability,</td></tr><tr><td>Supply–demand factors</td><td>management actions, etc.)</td></tr><tr><td>Sentiment, liquidity, and risk indicators</td><td>Supply–demand (net issuance, inventories, etc)</td></tr><tr><td></td><td>Sentiment, liquidity, and risk indicators</td></tr></table> month, followed by a medium-term momentum effect, and a long-term reversal effect beyond 12 months.


Seasonal regularities will be discussed in Chapter 25 but few are strong enough to be exploited.

"Other indicators" include the kitchen sink. Carry and value are important, but the list also includes macroeconomic indicators, supply-demand factors, sentiment, liquidity and risk indicators, and others. Underreactions to news and lead-lag effects across asset markets are close cousins to (own price) momentum, but are included here.

An alternative classification scheme contrasts convergent and divergent strategies. Convergent strategies rely on relationships in markets moving toward their long-term averages and include carry seeking, relative value, mean reversion, and volatility selling. Divergent strategies rely on market relationships moving further away from their long-term averages and include trend following, momentum, and volatility buying.

Yet another scheme focuses on the investment time horizon and the speed of information decay in indicators. As a classic example, the information in value indicators decays slowly while that in short-term momentum or reversal indicators decays fast. This book focuses on strategies traded at relatively low frequencies. High-frequency trading strategies—especially active within-day trading—are seldom based on economic intuition and are feasible only for traders facing the lowest trading costs anyway. Such strategies often exploit short-term price reversals and effectively act as liquidity suppliers.

# 24.5 ENHANCEMENTS AND PITFALLS

# Enhancements

The strategies above imply stable relations between asset returns and predictors, as they involve market timing one asset or trading long-short pairs or portfolios across assets "in any weather". It seems plausible that style timing can refine these strategies by identifying conditions when these strategies are more likely to excel or fail. These conditions may reflect the general market environment (e.g., carry trading is known to underperform amidst heightened volatility and tightening liquidity). Style timing may also be based on the strategy's own history—many strategies' returns exhibit a mild version of well-known short-run momentum and long-run reversal patterns—as well as on seasonal patterns. Contextual models give the cross-sectional equivalent to style timing: they identify subsets of the asset universe where a given strategy is more likely to excel or fail. For example, trend following works better for poorly anchored macrotrades than for well-anchored micro-trades, while many stock selection strategies work better for small-cap than for large-cap stocks, at least on paper. Chapters 12 through 14 provide examples of such enhancements.

Improvements can come in other areas. Many investment managers put as much effort into smart portfolio construction, risk management, and implementation efficiency (cheaper and/or faster execution, and other cost saving) as they do on alpha generation through return forecasts. Quants use data-intensive learning algorithms for model selection, Kalman filter methods for dynamic parameter estimation, regime-switching models to capture instability in predictive relations, Bayesian or robust optimizers to replace standard mean variance optimizers, and other methods.

# Pitfalls

Data mining. In-sample regressions exaggerate a model's predictive ability. Since regression coefficients may evolve over time, it is more realistic to run rolling "out-of-sample" regressions over time, estimating coefficients each month using only the data available to investors in real time. Even this approach does involve some lookahead bias because a model's indicator selections are likely motivated, in part, by information gathered in hindsight. Chapter 7 discusses data mining further and argues that it is an inevitable feature of systematic trading models. The use of nonstationary (highly persistent) time series as predictors and applying long-horizon regressions are especially likely to result in econometric problems and spurious findings.

Overcrowding. Another shortcoming in systematic strategies is that their transparency makes them easy to replicate and therefore more exposed than discretionary strategies to consensus positioning or crowding risk. The 1998 and 2007-2008 experiences suggest that all systematic strategies—not just risk-seeking carry strategies—may be subject to de-leveraging risks when financial conditions tighten. Moreover, technological advances and greater data availability have made any observed regularities ever more fleeting as they quickly attract profit-seeking competition. Taming these endogenous risks is a new but central challenge for systematic investors (see Chapter 20).

# 24.6 NOTES

[1] Every long position is assumed to be financed by a cash money market rate and every short position involves re-investing the proceeds in the cash market. Cash returns for a long-short position naturally wash out. In reality, short selling is not quite so easy in equity markets but in Treasury, currency, and futures markets these approximations are reasonable.
[2] Partial correlation is simple correlation adjusted for the influence of other predictors. This is the same distinction as between a multiple regression beta and a simple regression beta. If different predictors are uncorrelated with each other, there is no difference between simple and partial correlations. High overlap between any two predictors raises the gap between simple and partial correlations; intuitively, their predictive impact should not be double-counted. Raw regression betas—without normalization—would also reflect the different relative volatilities of predictors as well as their correlations.
[3] Cross-sectional asset selection strategies are seen to benefit from a broader opportunity set and are better diversified than market-timing models. It is true that timing the whole market involves particularly concentrated risk. However, if we combine long-short timing models for several assets, the resulting composite portfolio may not differ much from that of a cross-sectional long-short strategy.

Source notes. For examples of simple forecasting models in the bond market context, see Ilmanen (1997), Ilmanen-Sayood (2002), and Naik-Balakrishnan-Devarajan (2009). For an example of fair value models, see Panigirtzoglou-Loeys (2005). There are hundreds, if not thousands, of practitioner books on trading models and academic articles on forecasting asset returns. For books on quantitative forecasting models and trading approaches, all with equity orientation, see Grinold-Kahn (1999), Qian-Hua-Sorensen (2007), and Fabozzi-Focardi-Kolm (2010). On factor-mimicking portfolios, see Melas-Suryanarayanan-Cavaglia (2010). On econometric issues that get a short shrift in my book, see Campbell-Lo-McKinlay (1996) and Cochrane (2005a).

# 25

# Seasonal regularities

- Seasonal patterns in average returns are often weak, possibly spurious, and costly to exploit.
- The best known seasonal pattern is the January effect. Not just small-cap stocks but also the most volatile stocks and high-yield credits earn much of their outperformance in January.
- The January effect and other regularities that appear related—the January barometer and the Hallow'en effect—may be due to seasonally high investor risk appetite at the beginning of the year. This story is closer to the traditional window-dressing explanation than to the tax-loss-selling explanation. Other evidence on heightened gambling preferences around New Year's Day supports this story.
- For commodity prices, seasonals can be pronounced for sound economic reasons (supply and demand).

# 25.1 SEASONAL, CYCLICAL, AND SECULAR PATTERNS IN ASSET RETURNS

This chapter and the next two cover seasonal, cyclical, and secular patterns in historical returns. Calendar seasonals can be within day, day of week, day of month, or month of year. Other seasonals cover patterns as diverse as announcement day, pre-holiday, lunar cycle, harvesting season, and political cycle. I focus on monthly seasonals and especially the most robust seasonal: January-related effects.

Most published seasonal studies deal with equity market regularities. I review empirical evidence in both equity and other markets. Historically, average equity market returns have been high in December-January and negative in September-October. In contrast, government bonds have earned low returns early in the year and high returns in the autumn. So what? How much of these patterns are spurious and sample specific?

The health warnings I make throughout the book may be especially important for seasonal regularities. It is easy to data-mine the one financial markets history which we have experienced that the danger of overfitting is huge. In addition, seasonal regularities are vulnerable to competitive speculators trying to anticipate and exploit them and thereby eroding them. For example, the famous January effect has been known to work in late December, and the turn-of-the-month effect has also shifted earlier. Many other seasonalities weakened or even disappeared after they became widely known. Finally, many patterns are so mild that trading costs eat most, if not all, of the paper profits for strategies that try to exploit them. One practical cost-effective approach may be to delay or avoid executing trades (that are motivated by broader reasons) when seasonal indicators give a clearly opposite short-term signal.


# 25.2 MONTHLY SEASONALS AND THE JANUARY EFFECT

The pattern that really stands out from Figure 25.1 is the January effect. This seasonal is apparent in many kinds of risk-taking strategies but especially strong for carry seeking (including credit carry, FX carry, and long U.S. dollar) and for small-cap stock and value stock overweights. The exception is that momentum and trend-following strategies tend to outperform in December and underperform (reverse) in January.

Why such seasonality? It is baffling that a large part of the long-term return advantage of one asset over another is earned in just one month of a year. The classic explanations are tax-loss selling and institutional window dressing. In addition, I review seasonal variations in investor risk appetite and in the market's liquidity conditions.

Tax-loss selling puts general pressure on asset prices before the year end, perhaps especially on past losers and on less liquid assets.

Institutional window dressing refers to the desire to show safe, liquid, and successful investments in the books when holdings are revealed at year end (and subject to greater scrutiny and publicity in annual reports). Such window-dressing motives prompt institutions to buy more government debt and recently-outperforming assets in November and December, so as to make the portfolio look as though it had been better all along.

It seems plausible that general investor risk appetite peaks early in the calendar year as investors "start afresh" in January, and then gradually recedes during the year. Institutional desire to show conservative portfolios at year end may not be the only reason for such a seasonal change in risk appetite: many traders and fund managers earn their bonuses for a calendar year and get their drawdown limits refreshed every New Year.

Figure 25.1. Average calendar month returns for various assets/strategies, 1927-2009 and 1970-2009.
![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-22/aa485235-5155-4ade-b88b-be1df0035d75/f738eda8aafd957b8bc969cd91b2fefc3323d673a34599c98b32846e17d6a37c.jpg)
Sources: Ken French's website, Ibbotson Associates (Morningstar), Bloomberg, Barclays Capital, Citigroup, J.P. Morgan, Brevan Howard, own calculations.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-22/aa485235-5155-4ade-b88b-be1df0035d75/22fa237318fe0a898a771625fa779fbccdda2027f1da06fc7004c5d8f112463b.jpg)
Figure 25.2. Average U.S. strategy returns in Januaries vs. other months, 1970-2009 and 1927-1969. Sources: Ken French's website, Ibbotson Associates (Morningstar), Bloomberg, Barclays Capital, Citigroup, own calculations.

New empirical research supports the idea of seasonally high risk appetite—or gambling preference, lottery seeking—at the beginning of the year. Doran-Jiang-Peterson (2009) show that lottery-type stocks (low price, high volatility, high skewness) outperform in Januaries and underperform the rest of the year. They also document more exuberant gambling in Las Vegas and the greater relative richness of OTM call options vs. ATM options in January than in other months. Interestingly, Chinese lottery-type stocks outperform around the Chinese New Year rather than in January.

If trading costs are high enough, even modest systematic valuation differences between late December and early January can explain these patterns. Little actual trading may occur when quoted prices fall before the year end and rebound thereafter. Lower liquidity and high trading costs may thus explain why this seasonal is so strong for small-cap stocks and corporate bonds.

Figure 25.2 drills into the January seasonality of long U.S. time series over the past 40 years and over the preceding 43 years. The patterns look consistent across the two subperiods: a moderately stronger equity premium in January than in other months; much higher small-cap, value, and reversal premia in January; and negative momentum in Januaries. However, to avoid getting carried away, I note that for both the equity premium and the value premium the sign flipped for the past 20 years: January returns were below average, perhaps reflecting the fact that the seasonal had been discovered.

Turning to a broader set of investments, Figure 25.3 presents evidence for only the 1970-2009 period. The contrast is most striking for the performance of high-volatility stocks relative to low-volatility stocks. High-yield debt, FX carry, and the U.S. dollar also tend to make money in January and fare much less well in other months. Oil and long-duration bonds have the opposite pattern, while broad equity markets and trend-following strategies do not have exceptional January returns.

More detailed studies show that the bulk of the January effect comes in the first few days of the year. The pattern is also found in many other countries, though not in all.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-22/aa485235-5155-4ade-b88b-be1df0035d75/67de24947a3f0c071ed9e22622fbe13cb783cea669b219d4e74c3fcd93ede4f3.jpg)
Figure 25.3. Average returns for various investments in Januaries vs. other months, 1970-2009. Sources: Bloomberg, MSCI Barra, Barclays Capital, Citigroup, J.P. Morgan, S&P GSCI, Lubos Pastor's website, Ken French's website, Ang et al. (2006), David Blitz, Ibbotson Associates (Morningstar), Bank of America Merrill Lynch, Hedge Fund Research, brevan Howard, own calculations.

In some cases, cross-country differences may be explained by different tax years (in the U.S. the tax year coincides with the calendar year).

Certain seasonal patterns appear surprisingly robust over time. Figure 25.4 shows that equity, value, and carry premia are earned both during January and in other months, but more consistently in January. Small-cap stock premia have by and large only been earned during Januaries, while the bond term premium has only been earned outside Januaries. Most remarkably, the realized credit premium has been positive in January and net negative in other months, and high-volatility stocks have only outperformed in January and underperformed in other months. Conversely, the equity momentum strategy has lost money in Januaries but more than made up for it in other months. Meanwhile, both short-term and long-term reversal strategies shine in January (not shown). The abrupt shift from high risk aversion to high risk appetite at New Year is one way to explain the January success of reversal strategies and losses in momentum-like strategies.

Evidence on the seasonality of liquidity effects is more mixed. Liquidity conditions tend to be low in the holiday season before the New Year and quickly improve after it. Money market credit spreads exhibited a strong "turn" effect (spreads widening into the year end) until central banks began to tackle year end liquidity shortage with aggressive liquidity provision. A recent study by Watanabe-Watanabe (2008) highlights the strong time variation in equity market liquidity conditions and premia, with a distinct January seasonality. Stocks' liquidity betas are shown to vary across two distinct states: a short-lived, abnormal high-beta state (high liquidity needs, high volatility, wide dispersion in liquidity betas across single stocks, high reward for bearing liquidity risk) and a more persistent, normal low-beta state. The high-liquidity-beta state occurs in  $59\%$  of Januaries but only in  $14\%$  of other months. This evidence is in line with January outperformance of small-cap stocks and corporate bonds—which may reflect these assets' inferior liquidity as much as their greater riskiness. However, illiquidity shocks based on the Amihud measure are only mildly negative in January, and the Pastor-Stambaugh portfolio of high-liquidity-risk vs. low-liquidity-risk beta stocks actually makes its net profits outside January (the third pair of bars in Figure 25.3).

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-22/aa485235-5155-4ade-b88b-be1df0035d75/3001c7f5b352c8b3553c7795f2e768357ed04c80742f81d325f8dca2380dee6c.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-22/aa485235-5155-4ade-b88b-be1df0035d75/66e6fe4450af2644730a707d26b8c133c49c5f74f9bdca500cd2be2204a75df8.jpg)
Figure 25.4. Cumulative (not compounded) returns in Januaries vs. other months, 1927-2009. Sources: Bloomberg, MSCI Barra, Barclays Capital, Citigroup, J.P. Morgan, Ken French's website, Ang et al. (2006), David Blitz, Ibbotson Associates (Morningstar), Brevan Howard, own calculations.

Note: Each panel sums up over time the monthly excess return of a given investment separately for Januaries and for non-January months between 1927 and 2009. Excess return is the difference between the monthly return of a long portfolio and a short portfolio: thus it is expressed as a percentage of the long position's size. The eight panels cover three pages and include (a) the equity premium, (b) value (stock) premium, (c) carry premium (since 1970), (d) small-cap (stock) premium, (e) credit-vs.-Treasury premium, (f) high-volatility (stock) premium (since 1963), (g) bond (term) premium, and (h) momentum (stock) premium. Note that there are 11 "non-January" months for each "January", so if both cumulative return series reach similar levels—see panel (b)—it implies much higher average returns in Januaries than in other months.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-22/aa485235-5155-4ade-b88b-be1df0035d75/54faa5bf97cc17f6a2d9ab57ab869ba7ca01803444cd70d31ba3606d88f6eeb0.jpg)

Sources: Ken French's website, Ibbotson Associates (Morningstar), Bloomberg, MSCI Barra, Barclays Capital, Citigroup, J.P. Morgan, S&P GSCI, Ang et al. (2006), David Blitz, Bryan Howard, own calculations.

# January barometer

"The other January effect", or the January barometer, refers to the finding that equity market performance in January has some predictive ability for the next 11 months' returns: "As goes January, so goes the rest of the year." As I mentioned earlier, one intuitive explanation for the first January effect—high returns for risky assets—applies if there is seasonally strong investor risk appetite at the beginning of a new year. The logic for the January barometer follows from the same intuition: if the general risk appetite is already fragile in the benign January environment, it spells trouble for the rest of the year.

The success of the January barometer has been surprisingly consistent across risky assets (as shown in Figure 25.5). This evidence is consistent with an especially strong risk appetite at the beginning of the year, and a bearish view for the rest of the year if investor risk appetite is not healthy in January. Incidentally, this barometer worked very well in 2008 but poorly in 2009.

# Hallowe'en indicator

Equity market returns in the U.S. and many other countries have been high in the half-year between November and April, and low in the half-year between May and October. The Hallowe'en indicator is thus named because a good time to buy risky assets begins near Hallowe'en. Several famous October crashes have occurred just days or weeks before Hallowe'en, which some observers might take as supportive evidence of seasonal sentiment variation, while skeptics might view this as a coincidence or data-mined result.

The evidence is also consistent with the old Wall Street adage, "Sell in May and go away." The period during which the market is not held covers the summer doldrums and the autumn gloom (sometimes linked to seasonal affective disorder, SAD), while the period when the market is held includes the New Year with heightened risk appetite as well as its anticipation.

Figure 25.6. Hallowe'en effect: Average monthly returns in November-May and April-October, 1927-2009.
![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-22/aa485235-5155-4ade-b88b-be1df0035d75/aee6be1f26153574f0f9e6904c1ffaf93a22b434312795219cf279a4530daa86.jpg)
Sources: Ken French's website, Ibbotson Associates (Morningstar), Bloomberg, MSCI Barra, Barclays Capital, Citigroup, J.P. Morgan, S&P GSCI, Ang et al. (2006), David Blitz, Brevan Howard, own calculations.

Figure 25.6 shows that the Hallowe'en effect in equity markets is echoed in the outperformance of small-cap stocks, high-volatility stocks, and long-run reversals as well as credit and carry strategies.

If the bar charts in Figures 25.3, 25.5, and 25.6 resemble each other, there is a reason. The January effect, the January barometer, and the Hallowe'en effect appear related; the patterns are similar and strong for various risky assets and weak for other assets. While other reasons exist, the core story is seasonally high investor risk appetite at the beginning of a new year.

# 25.3 OTHER SEASONALS

While the January seasonal has plausible rational explanations, many other seasonals do not. However, most seasonal patterns are so weak that trading costs prevent exploiting them—except for modifying the timing of planned buying or selling. Moreover, the possibility of data-mined results is evident.

Within-day regularities are only a curiosity for most investors, and any tradable patterns are weak. In equity markets, average returns do not vary significantly across the day but volatility is clearly higher at the beginning and end of the trading day, and, unsurprisingly, at times of major data announcements. In FX markets there is even some evidence of within-day average return variation. Major currencies tend to weaken during the main trading hours of their own time zone—perhaps reflecting diversification flows from local markets into global assets.

The day-of-the week effect was one of the first documented anomalies. In the past, average equity market returns were negative on Mondays. However, this regularity disappeared soon after it became widely known in the 1970s.

Turn of the month. Equity market returns tend to be higher at the turn of the month, both during the week before and after. McConnell-Xu (2008) show that all U.S. equity market excess returns between 1926 and 2005 were earned over a 4-day window (the last business day of the month and the first three business days); average long-run gain on other days was slightly negative. This pattern is not exclusive to the year end, quarter ends, or to the United States. Investors "putting money to work" (i.e., transferring money from the labor market to the capital markets) in a lumpy fashion near the month end seems like an appealing explanation but mutual fund inflows do not show such a regularity.


Holiday effect. Equity market returns tend to be high on the day before major U.S. holidays (as well as on some subsequent days, such as the day after Thanksgiving and after Christmas Day). Similar patterns are found in most other markets, including holidays not on the U.S. calendar (Chinese New Year, Japanese Golden Week).

Grimbacher-Swinkels-Van Vliet (2010) study interactions between the three previous anomalies, the January effect, and the Hallowe'en effect on U.S. equity market returns between 1963 and 2008. They find that the Hallowe'en effect and the turn-of-the-month effect are the strongest, even subsuming the holiday effect. The turn-of-the-month effect looks especially consistent: in each decade between the 1960s and 2000s, annualized average returns on the five business days starting from the last day of the month are 17-37 percentage points higher than on other days.

Lunar cycle. Average equity market returns are higher in the fortnight around the new moon than in the fortnight around the full moon.

Announcement days. It is not surprising that market volatility is higher on days of major economic announcements, and single-stock volatility is higher on earnings announcement days. More surprisingly, average asset returns tend to be higher on those days—perhaps reflecting compensation for uncertainty and gains from the resolution of uncertainty. Finally, many assets exhibit exceptional momentum in the runup to such announcement days (government bonds before payroll data announcement dates and other key economic announcements, stocks before earnings announcements, oil futures before OPEC meetings, and agricultural commodities before major supply and demand reports).

Periodicity. One curious momentum effect in equity markets is 12-month periodicity. Individual stocks tend to outperform or underperform in the same months each year—and these do not necessarily coincide with earnings announcement months.

Supply-demand seasonals. Certain commodity markets exhibit especially strong seasonal patterns. Indeed, such seasonals are often already "in the price". Many agricultural commodity prices tend to be higher before the harvesting period (amidst seasonal scarcity) and lower after the harvest (amidst abundant supply). In the case of heating or cooling energy, it is demand rather than supply that exhibits regular seasonal patterns. Such predictable price variation may provide no exploitable opportunities if it is fully built into futures prices; contract roll losses just offset expected price gains. However, in many cases these patterns are only partly built into futures prices, so seasonal excess returns may be predictable.

Longer cycles. One example is the presidential cycle, in which U.S. equity markets tend to fare better in the last two years than the first two years of presidential terms in office—presumably due to government largesse in the runup to the new election. Like many other documented patterns, this one has partly broken down. The relation between monetary policy cycles and equity market returns has also become weaker since the 1990s. Business cycles remain important and I turn to them next.

Source notes. On the January effect, see Keim (1983), Haugen-Lakonishok (1988), and Doran-Jiang-Peterson (2009). On the January barometer, see Cooper-McConnell-

Ovtchinnikov (2006) and Stivers–Sun–Sun (2009). On the Hallowe'en effect, see Jacobsen–Bouman (2002). On the within-day effect in FX markets, see Breedon–Ranaldo (2008). On the day-of-the-month effect, see McConnell–Xu (2008). On multiple seasonals, see Grimbacher–Swinkels–Van Vliet (2010). On the recent performance of seasonals, see Dzhabarov–Ziemba (2010). On lunar cycles, see Dichev–Janes (2003). On exceptional momentum before event days, see Ilmanen–Byrne (2003). On return periodicity, see Heston–Sadka (2008).

# 26

# Cyclical variation in asset returns

- Data gathered over business cycles show that, on average, excess returns on risky assets are especially high near cyclical troughs and low near cyclical peaks.
- Forward-looking value indicators (dividend yield, credit spread, curve steepness) tend to decline through expansions and rise through contractions.
- Observing how the growth environment intersects with the inflation or volatility environment gives further insights into asset class behavior. Equity returns have depended on volatility and inflation conditions more than on growth; they excel in disinflationary and stable environments. Bonds have primarily depended on inflation conditions (benefiting from disinflation), while commodities and housing returns have varied most with the growth environment (benefiting from strong growth).

This chapter reviews the way realized returns—and some ex ante carry and value indicators—vary through the business cycle or across different macroeconomic environments. Long data histories are needed for such assessments, which restricts this empirical analysis to core asset classes.

Unlike most of the book, this chapter focuses on contemporaneous empirical relations. The observed relationship between the business cycle and asset returns may be more reflective of unexpected returns (data surprises) than of expected returns. When interpreting the relationship of asset returns to the prevailing macroeconomic environment, we must also keep in mind financial assets' forward-looking nature. Aligning the timing of macroeconomic data series and financial time series is further complicated by the fact that macroeconomic data suffer from publication lags and data revisions (and are measured using noise). Finally, if returns vary predictably over time, it is more useful to study cyclical patterns in ex ante indicators than in realized returns (I document both).

If an investor can predict the economic outlook (not easy!) and if the return regularities documented below are persistent, understanding these relations are essential for a discretionary macro-investor (and indeed are coded in his or her mind). However, the superior macro-investor goes beyond historical regularities and instead asks what is unique in the current situation, how policymaker and investor responses break past patterns, and what relevant structural changes are molding the world. All of which is well beyond the scope of this book.

The evidence in this chapter may also help in understanding rational risk premia or procyclical sentiment, and it may give some clues for market timing, style timing, or factor timing—suggesting good and bad environments for various asset classes and dynamic strategies.

# 26.1 TYPICAL BEHAVIOR OF REALIZED RETURNS AND EX ANTE INDICATORS THROUGH THE BUSINESS CYCLE

Business cycles describe broad fluctuations in aggregate economic activity. The U.S. government considers the National Bureau of Economic Research (NBER) to be the final arbiter of the U.S. business cycle. Evaluating a variety of economic series, the NBER's Business Cycle Committee officially dates—often after a significant delay—the peaks and troughs of economic activity. (I assume that the last recession ended in July 2009.) The periods between these are called expansions (or recoveries) and contractions (or recessions). Among more journalistic definitions, the most popular is to call two consecutive quarters of negative real GDP growth a recession. Business cycles in other countries are even more loosely defined. Business cycles are not regular (e.g., recessions became shorter and shallower after World War II). Yet they share common features (e.g., expansions almost always last longer than contractions).

Why do business cycles exist at all? We know that they have existed for millennia—recall the seven fat years and seven lean years in the Old Testament. In an agricultural society, such cycles may have reflected weather and harvest fortunes; in an industrial society, business cycles may be caused by technology shocks or by demand fluctuations, which are often related to changes in credit conditions. The underlying behavioral story is that humans by nature are prone to fits of optimism and pessimism, high and low animal spirits, fear and greed.

Asset returns and valuations turn out to share some of this cyclicality. Arguably, one should not expect large cyclical patterns in the returns of forward-looking, long-lived assets which should "see through" many cycles [1]. Cyclical patterns may thus be more reflective of time variation and wealth dependence in required risk premia than of the market's growth expectations. The monetary policy cycle is a related explanation for financial assets' procyclical behavior. These assets serve as one part of the monetary transmission mechanism when a central bank eases or tightens policy.

Figures 26.1 through 26.4 give a visual perspective to the business cycle pattern in various asset returns and ex ante indicators. I treat each month between January 1927 and December 2009 as being in either the first, second, or last third of each NBER expansion or contraction (recession); in addition, I assign five months around each NBER-defined business cycle turning point to special categories called peak and trough. I then compute the average value of each series within each subperiod. I leave a space between each expansion phase to visually remind the reader that the U.S. economy expanded in  $80\%$  of the 996 months and contracted only  $20\%$  of the time. I display the cyclical trough—a crucial turning point—at both the left and right extremes of the figure.

Figure 26.1 shows that equities perform well in expansions and poorly in the early and middle phases of contractions. However, equities' forward-looking nature and/or countercyclical risk premium are highlighted by the fact that the stock market's realized returns are high around business cycle troughs and low around business cycle peaks. Although this is not visible, the CRP has a cyclical pattern similar to that of the ERP, although an order of magnitude smaller. In addition, the BRP is low near business cycle peaks but realized bond returns are already very high in the middle of the recession.

In Figure 26.2, I turn to other popular equity market factors for which data have been available since 1927. Size exhibits broadly the same cyclical pattern as ERP and CRP.

Figure 26.1. Mean excess returns of equities, government bonds, and credits over business cycle expansion and contraction phases, 1927-2009.
![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-22/aa485235-5155-4ade-b88b-be1df0035d75/9a5684006069f6d461749f33fc36d423c40cea88f6c6b7b42cd181ed10157e64.jpg)
Sources: Ken French's website, Ibbotson Associates (Morningstar), Bloomberg, Citigroup, Barclays Capital, National Bureau of Economic Analysis, own calculations.

Figure 26.2. More mean excess returns of U.S. equity styles over business cycle expansion and contraction phases, 1927-2009.
![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-22/aa485235-5155-4ade-b88b-be1df0035d75/bc37f1c2771291c5e77e2a2ea3ee43bb864d3ae2ae9ae9c8432f565a3a7dd772.jpg)
Sources: Ken French's website, National Bureau of Economic Analysis, own calculations.

That is, small-cap stocks tend to outperform near cyclical troughs and early expansions, and underperform near peaks and early contractions. Value exhibits almost no cyclical patterns, except slightly lower returns in mid-contractions and late contractions. However, this pattern mostly reflects the 1930s' experience. Using only postwar data, value works a bit better in contractions than in expansions. Momentum has been a good diversifier near cyclical peaks and in mid-recessions when most risky assets suffer. However, market turnaround in the last third of contractions, and near cyclical troughs, often involves a sharp handover from defensive stocks to aggressive cycicals, which is a bad environment for momentum.

Realized returns are noisy proxies of expected returns. They can be especially misleading whenever realized returns are persistently high (low) because of a gradual fall (rise) in the market's expected or required returns; this is sometimes called the "repricing effect" or "discount rate effect". For example, peak realized excess returns for equities and credits around the business cycle trough hint at high and falling required returns. It is useful to study forward-looking valuation indicators more directly. Figure 26.3 shows that key ex ante indicators of asset risk premia share a similar countercyclical pattern. All three series fall throughout an expansion and rise fast during a contraction, but dividend yield (D/P) and credit spread turn before the cyclical trough. Smoothed earnings yield (not shown) and credit spread (shown) exhibit a pattern similar to D/P. Of the variables, curve steepness (YC) is the best matched to the business cycle in terms of timing. While average dividend yield near cyclical peaks is only slightly lower than that

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-22/aa485235-5155-4ade-b88b-be1df0035d75/7a37309b5324ddbba0db711cd2b5142c185da9a8a0449ac7a2395c053a202e43.jpg)
Figure 26.3. Mean spreads and valuation ratios across the business cycle, 1927-2009. Sources: Robert Shiller's website, Haver Analytics, Federal Reserve Board, Moody's, Ibbotson Associates (Morningstar), Bloomberg, Citigroup, Barclays Capital, National Bureau of Economic Analysis, own calculations.


![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-22/aa485235-5155-4ade-b88b-be1df0035d75/f354ce5943cf1248f71aeffd75437076a3d51c2e9137a7bb94eebe0a9b56661c.jpg) near troughs, the yield curve is typically at its flattest at the business cycle trough and steepest near the business cycle peak.
Figure 26.4. Mean rates across the business cycle, 1927-2009. Sources: Robert Shiller's website, Federal Reserve Board, Bloomberg, National Bureau of Economic Analysis, own calculations.


Finally, Figure 26.4 shows that while the long Treasury yield level exhibits almost no cyclical variation, the short rate and the inflation rate clearly vary with the business cycle. Traditionally, the monetary policy cycle and the business cycle were closely related. As capacity utilization and demand-driven inflation pressures rose in a mature expansion, the central bank responded to overheating by raising short-term rates until it killed the boom. When economic growth became anemic, slack and disinflation ensued, and the central bank could again cut rates to revive the economy.

Most documented cyclical patterns are similar if empirical analysis is started after the Great Depression and World War II instead of 1927. One exception is that postwar inflation rates have been higher in contractions than in expansions (see Figure 17.3). Expected inflation and ex ante real bond yield have not exhibited much cyclicality.

# 26.2 TYPICAL BEHAVIOR OF REALIZED RETURNS AND EX ANTE INDICATORS ACROSS DIFFERENT ECONOMIC REGIMES

Business cycles differ in their depth and duration. The most important contrast is between typical post-World War II recessions, arguably caused by Fed tightening, and balance sheet recessions, caused by de-leveraging after financial excesses (as in the 1930s and 2000s); the latter are more severe.

It is also interesting to observe how the cyclical growth environment intersects with other key drivers of asset returns, such as inflation and volatility. I create three composite dummy series—real growth, inflation, and volatility—and document average realized asset returns and proxies for ex ante returns in relevant data subsets. I assign each calendar quarter to one of the following four growth-and-inflation quadrants (and I do the same for growth and volatility):

<table><tr><td>Disinflationary boom</td><td>Inflationary boom</td></tr><tr><td>Disinflationary stagnation</td><td>Inflationary stagnation</td></tr></table>

There are numerous ways to create and combine such composite series; my approach is by no means unique. The real growth proxy is an equally weighted average of (up to) six normalized series: expected next year real GDP growth, realized past year real GDP growth, real earnings growth, the CFNAI index (a composite of a large number of real activity indicators), the ISM measure of business confidence, and the Conference Board measure of consumer confidence. Two of the six series start only in the mid-1960s. Most negative values coincide with NBER recessions. The inflation proxy reflects both the inflation level and its annual changes (averaging data for headline and core CPI, the GDP deflator, and a consensus forecast inflation rate). The volatility proxy reflects the average of realized stock and bond market volatilities based on daily returns over the past quarter. Figure 26.5 shows the history of the three dummies and indicates in which quadrant the economy is functioning in a given quarter. For example, in early

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-22/aa485235-5155-4ade-b88b-be1df0035d75/9015c9c757174dc58f20111bbae6d69cedfbc3fe50d80cf3bb173beb681b8fc4.jpg)
Figure 26.5. Growth, inflation, and volatility dummies used in regime analysis, 1960-2009. Sources: Haver Analytics, Federal Reserve Board, Bloomberg. Federal Reserve Banks of Chicago and Philadelphia, own calculations.

2009, when both growth and inflation dummies had negative values, the regime was clearly "disinflationary stagnation".

My quadrant classification is simply based on the sign of each dummy. For example, boom and stagnation are periods of fast and slow real activity; stagnation does not imply outright recession. The four quadrants will not be equally populated, due to some asymmetry in each dummy series and to interactions between dimensions (a boom is more often associated with disinflation than with inflation, for example). The first columns in Tables 26.1 and 26.2 show the observed frequency of each quadrant during the 1960-2009 period.

Tables 26.1 and 26.2 show 1960-2009 average real quarterly returns in each growth/inflation quadrant and growth/volatility quadrant. The best (worst) performer in each regime is shown in boldface (italics). There is surprisingly little difference in average returns for risky assets between boom and stagnation regimes. Adding either the inflation or the volatility dimension gives clearer patterns. Indeed, both dimensions discriminate equity returns better than real activity. Equities fare better during disinflationary periods than during inflationary periods. (This finding might not hold up if my study period included the Great Depression.) Equities also fare better during stable market conditions than during volatile episodes. Small-cap stocks and value stocks outperform the equity market in most regimes (based on this classification and sample period), but especially amidst inflation. All bonds fare best in a disinflationary or stable stagnation, while commodities and housing fare best in an inflationary boom.

The volatility dimension is most critical for all types of stocks; equities perform best under stable conditions. The inflation dimension is critical for bonds; they lose under inflationary conditions. The growth dimension is critical for commodities and housing; they excel in any kind of strong-growth environment.

Sources: Haver Analytics, Federal Reserve Board, Bloomberg, Federal Reserve Bank of Chicago, Ken French's website, Ibbotson Associates (Morningstar), Citigroup, Barclays Capital, Lincoln Institute of Land Policy, own calculations.

Looking across columns, the stock market outperforms all bonds in most regimes but especially in a disinflationary boom and in the two stable regimes. Bonds mainly outperform stocks in a volatile or disinflationary stagnation, presumably including flight-to-quality and deflationary episodes.

It may be surprising that average real equity market returns are lowest, even negative, during inflationary booms. The counterintuitively poor performance during inflationary booms reflects monetary policy tightening while the counterintuitively strong performance during disinflationary stagnations reflects monetary policy easing. (Average short-rate moves during these quarters were respectively  $+32$  bp and  $-45$  bp, while they averaged about zero for the other two quadrants.) These patterns are just averages: in the last two monetary policy cycles, equities have actually lost money during Fed easing and made money during Fed tightening, perhaps due to recent changes in the timing of Fed policy. Nonetheless, high-frequency data on unexpected Fed moves make it unambiguously clear that the equity market reacts positively to unexpectedly large rate cuts and negatively to rate hikes.


Beyond these exhibits, I also studied the profitability of several strategy styles across these regimes. Highlights from this study reveal that

- Small-cap stocks outperform most in inflationary and/or volatile stagnations and only underperform in disinflationary or volatile booms.
- For equity value strategies, the growth dimension is also critical—but, interestingly, with an opposite sign from small-caps. Value stands out in all boom regimes and only loses money in the stable stagnation regime.
- Equity momentum strategies are profitable in all eight regimes but really shine in inflationary booms. Inflation seems to be the strongest discriminator.
- Trend-following strategies also make money in all eight regimes. They fare best in volatile or inflationary booms and seem to benefit from high growth, high inflation, and high volatility.
- Both the carry composite and the FX carry strategy are profitable in all eight regimes (on average). Both credit carry and FX carry fare better in disinflationary regimes. Credit carry fares best in stable or disinflationary booms, but the evidence for FX carry is mixed.

This type of descriptive analysis is fun but suffers from many limitations. My classification is judgmental as are my regime proxies. Moreover, asset class behavior may vary within each regime (stocks may be more sensitive to growth at the beginning of a boom than at the end) and change over time (bonds' relation to volatility conditions flipped sign after the 1980s). For the results to have investment value, the patterns documented must be reasonably robust and investors need to be able to predict future macroeconomic regimes.

Finally, since realized returns can be misleading proxies of expected returns, we need to study ex ante returns too. Tables 26.3 and 26.4 track the behavior of various ex ante indicators across regimes. This analysis does not show how valuations evolve during each regime, but it reveals that average equity market yields are low during booms and
