high during stagnations—especially during inflationary and volatile stagnations. Yield curve steepness mainly depends on inflation conditions and is steepest in disinflationary stagnations. Credit spreads mainly depend on growth conditions and are widest in volatile stagnations. The last columns show that average bond yields and inflation levels were highest in inflationary stagnation regimes, reflecting stagflationary conditions during the two oil crises of the 1970s. As expected, monetary policy easing was most prominent (short rates fell and the yield curve was steep) in disinflationary stagnations.

# 26.3 NOTES

[1] Stock prices discount expected cash flows until perpetuity. The estimated duration of stocks may be longer than 30 years. Such a long period includes several expansions and contractions. Realistically, markets can only have some ability to forecast cash flow growth during the current business cycle, and such near-term cash flows constitute only a small part of a stock's value. The cyclical fluctuations in cash flows beyond the current cycle should largely wash out, leaving a stable anchor for stock prices if markets are rational and discount rates constant.

Source notes. For examples of similar business cycle analysis, see Naik-Devarajan (2009) and Lustig-Verdelhan (2010). See Kaya-Lee-Pornrojnangkool (2010) and Ang-Bekaert (2002) for applications of regime-switching models.

# 27

# Secular trends and the next 20 years

- The analysis of secular trends is inherently discretionary and speculative.
- The period from the 1980s to 2007 was exceptionally benign for many financial assets.
- I highlight several secular trends that may be reversing: free market capitalism, disinflation, rising leverage, financialization, and asset richening.
- I sympathize with consensus views that the coming decade will be characterized by the continued rise of emerging economies and eventual resumption of inflation.

I start this discussion with some numbers, highlighting how exceptional the two decades before 2008 were. Then I identify major secular trends—some of which are now reversing while others are persisting—and review many of them graphically. Finally, I comment on the shadow of the recent crisis and speculate more broadly on the next 20 years.

This chapter is more impressionistic in its analyses and more tied to the current situation than the rest of the book. It has more the style of discretionary view-takers, while the rest of the book tries to learn from history without resorting to subjective market views. In practice, many smart investors want to combine both approaches.

# 27.1 CONTRASTING 1988-2007 WITH 1968-1987

The period 1988-2007 was in many ways a more benign period for financial markets than the preceding two decades, 1968-1987, and probably also more benign than the next two decades. It was a period characterized by increasing globalization and market capitalism as well as disinflation and across-country convergence of inflation rates. (The world 5-year average inflation rate fell from  $13\%$  at the beginning of this period to  $3.8\%$  at the end.) Global growth was solid ( $3.5\%$  annual real GDP growth) but not extraordinary by the high standards of the post-World War II period. Macroeconomic volatility plummeted even though the period was punctuated by several financial crises. There were no major wars between developed countries. In most countries there were no hyperinflations nor deep depressions. Capitalism, democracy, the rule of law, deregulation, and market-friendly economic policies gained ground across the globe over almost the entire period. No wonder market optimism was rife but, as we know, all did not end well.

In contrast, the preceding period involved an increasing role for the state and doubts about capitalism, a shift to fiat currencies, two oil shocks, rising inflation and economic volatility, declining productivity, falling asset valuations, and relatively low realized asset returns. This period of mostly bad news did not last the full 20 years from 1968 to 1987, but essentially came to an end in the summer of 1982 when the massive 1980s' bull market began.


Table 27.1 contrasts the U.S. experience in five areas across the two 20-year periods:

- Macroeconomy. The rate of change in real activity did not differ much across the periods. The earlier period was characterized by rising inflation (until the early 1980s), the latter by disinflation. The volatility of both real activity and inflation series fell dramatically between the two periods (this change is sometimes called the Great Moderation).
- Debt growth outpaced economic growth in all sectors, and at a faster rate in the second 20 years than in the first; but the sharp rise in public indebtedness occurred after 2007. Leverage of the financial sector rose very fast, from  $9\%$  of GDP in 1967 to  $39\%$  in 1987 and to  $115\%$  in 2007. The household sector was not far behind in its leveraging.
- "Financialization" was a major trend in the 1988-2007 period. Corporate profits rose faster than GDP, equity market capitalization rose faster than profits, and financial companies' share of both profits and market cap grew rapidly. This was a sharp reversal because all these ratios had shrunk during the 1968-1987 period.
- Financial assets performed relatively poorly in the earlier period and very well in the latter period. Average real returns were clearly higher between 1988 and 2007, while return volatility was lower. Another benign feature was that the correlation between equity markets and Treasuries shifted from positive  $(+0.4)$  to negative  $(-0.1)$ , thereby enhancing diversification of balanced stock-bond portfolios. Not surprisingly, real assets such as real estate and commodities outpaced financial assets in the inflationary 1970s but lagged subsequently (not shown).
- High asset returns between 1988 and 2007 partly reflected rising asset valuations. Real and nominal Treasury yields fell by more than half amid falling inflation expectations and a fall in inflation-related risk premia, and the equity market saw significant multiple expansion. In contrast, all asset valuations had deteriorated during the preceding two decades.

# 27.2 REVERSIBLE AND SUSTAINABLE SECULAR TRENDS

Here is a list of 10 secular trends that drove markets in recent decades. I deem half of these trends to be reversing. Of course, it is just a judgment whether any trend is reversing or not, but my views seem close to the consensus.

# Trends that appear to be reversing:

- free market capitalism; disinflation;
- rising leverage;
- financialization;
- asset richening.

Table 27.1. Contrasting the two decades encompassing 1988-2007 with the preceding two decades, 1968-1987

For symmetry, here are five trends that appear sustainable:

- technological progress;
- the "rise of the rest" (China, the rest of Asia, and other emerging economies); aging population;
- globalization; and
- environmental concerns (climate change, limited natural resources, socially responsible investing [1]).

These are all huge topics, and beyond the scope of this book. I turn to a couple of these themes in the next section but for now I focus on reversing trends. I simply remind the reader that all such secular trends, if persistent, create potential landmines for backward-looking mean reversion or fair value models (see Section 12.5).

# Shifting pendulum between markets and state

It is hardest to quantify this trend, but it is clear that the decades since the early 1980s witnessed globally spreading capitalism. Many centrally planned economies turned into market economies and even China embraced economic liberalism while nominally remaining a communist state. Deregulation, liberalization of cross-border flows, and falling top (and other) tax rates were other prevalent trends.

The pendulum is shifting back. A backlash against laissez-faire capitalism is understandable after financial market excesses and turbulence caused the worst global recession in decades in 2008. While the reasons for this crisis can be debated, the consensus view blames free market ideology as much as greedy bankers, reckless home buyers, or misguided government policy. More regulation will certainly follow. Stretched public finances also make higher taxes inevitable down the road. Markets are giving way to the state.

# Disinflation and the Great Moderation

I discuss inflation developments in greater detail in Chapter 17. Here I just remind readers of the mountain shape in postwar U.S. inflation data: an uptrend in the 1960s and 1970s and a downtrend in the 1980s and 1990s (see Figure 27.1). Importantly, many real-economy series and asset return series seem to have shared—or, rather, mirrored—these trends. Many benign developments—lower volatility, stronger real economy and corporate performance, and higher asset valuations—since the early 1980s may be traced back to disinflation, just as many of the malign developments before that may be attributed to soaring inflation and nominal interest rates.

Disinflation since 1980 coincided with a decline in macroeconomic volatility (the Great Moderation) and, to a much lesser extent, in financial market volatility (see Figure 27.2). Here, too, a reversal seems to be taking place, as macro-volatility spiked in 2008, together with financial market volatility.

# Leveraging

American households de-levered for 20 years after the Great Depression, but then began to re-lever, producing an inexorable rise in the debt/GDP ratio (see Figure 27.3). The

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-22/aa485235-5155-4ade-b88b-be1df0035d75/5ff536e638925ad14b53630aaad6f85c30fa391e62f7f94703e85133579f2248.jpg)
Figure 27.1. U.S. inflation mountain after World War II. Sources: Bloomberg, Federal Reserve Bank of Philadelphia, Blue Chip Economic Indicators, Consensus Economics, Sharon Kozicki.

Figure 27.2. Declining macroeconomic volatility: Falling output volatility and inflation volatility since the 1930s.
![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-22/aa485235-5155-4ade-b88b-be1df0035d75/a18ace7f042e4a07684ea96537d9c9d465e0256258acb63b0f4e18a91483f0ec.jpg)
Sources: Haver Analytics, own calculations.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-22/aa485235-5155-4ade-b88b-be1df0035d75/c094e5aa949a0ba5a1e1e8cac2e22f3768c0d7859306ce6b06184fa402d87993.jpg) borrowing binge speeded up in good times—the 1950s, 1980s, and 2000s—and was supported by diverse benign developments: rising wealth (higher collateral value leading to greater creditworthiness, and also to higher risk tolerance), financial liberalization and innovation, loosening lending standards, low policy rates and abundant savings from emerging markets, improving faith in central banks' ability to manage the economy, and other factors. By some measures, the U.S. private savings rate turned negative by the mid-2000s. The corporate sector also borrowed more, but the most dramatic leveraging occurred in the financial sector. Here, too, the pendulum was shifting and the turn in the private debt/GDP ratio in 2008–2009 was likely only the beginning of a longer balance sheet healing process. Aggressive public-sector borrowing is partly offsetting private-sector de-leveraging.
Figure 27.3. Growing leverage of the U.S. private sector. Source: Haver Analytics.


One critical view blames rising financial leverage on repeated bailouts in recent decades. According to the "debt supercycle" theme of the Bank Credit Analyst, the borrow-and-spend process has been a means to sustain politically desirable economic growth. In the background lurked policymakers' fear of another Great Depression, but by keeping deflationary risks contained they created new types of imbalances: ever escalating leverage punctuated by occasional crises, and bailouts that encouraged ever greater risk taking and moral hazard. We may be in the final innings of this supercycle since 2008: the private sector no longer keeps borrowing while the public sector levers up to sustain economic growth. Eventually, stretched public finances will threaten public-sector credibility to the extent that such reflation policy will not be able to continue.

# Financialization

Financialization refers to the growing role of the financial industry in the economy. The financial sector's share of corporate profits ranged between  $10\%$  and  $20\%$  from the 1950s to the 1980s. The share began to rise in the mid-1980s and stayed between  $30\%$  and  $45\%$  in 2001–2007. As a result, financial companies' share of total equity market capitalization doubled from  $13\%$  to  $26\%$  between 1988 and 2006. These unsustainable developments reversed sharply in 2008 (see Figure 27.4).

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-22/aa485235-5155-4ade-b88b-be1df0035d75/9d3174f9349d8f4494f2ebac9e93b36f371f7c49266d567b6550b9ec3f90548c.jpg)
Figure 27.4. U.S. financial sector's share of corporate profits and market cap. Source: Haver Analytics.


Financialization is related to several other trends: rising leverage and asset richening; securitization and its flip side (i.e., disintermediation from banks); deregulation and active financial innovations; expansion of the delegated asset management industry (institutionalization of asset holding and trading); the finance sector's increasing political influence; widening income inequality within countries; and globalization and integration of capital markets. Some of these trends are now reversing. For example, certain securitized asset classes have not recovered their liquidity since both the 2008 crisis and the "flight to simplicity". Other trends, such as growth of the asset management industry, will persist.

# Assetrichening

The quarter-century of free markets, disinflation, and financialization coincided with a general decline in real yields and increase in the valuations of all asset classes.

Since World War II, the share of GDP represented by corporate profits has been mean-reverting in the  $6\%$  to  $12\%$  range. It peaked in 1955, 1966, and 2006 and troughed in 1982—not coincidentally near the peak of inflation. However, the inflationary 1970s were characterized less by the low absolute level of corporate profits and more by the conservative capitalization of these profits. The market-cap-to-profits ratio (a variant of the P/E ratio) troughed at 4 in 1978 and peaked at 20 in 2000. The equity market cap of the U.S. amounted to less than half of the country's annual GDP between 1975 and 1985, but exceeded GDP every year between 1996 and 2007. (Note that the market cap/GDP ratio mixes two effects, market valuation and financialization, the latter in this case being a descriptor for the proportion of total business wealth that is publicly traded.) Equity markets capitalized current production and profits especially aggressively during the tech bubble and less ambitiously in the mid-2000s (see Figure 27.5).

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-22/aa485235-5155-4ade-b88b-be1df0035d75/6ddc83901578092627e0bb36fc51c598465ca79d3bed45c35c4afc89acb75f96.jpg)
Figure 27.5. Relations between profits, market cap, and GDP. Source: Haver Analytics.


# 27.3 THE NEXT 20 YEARS

# The shadow of the crisis

Many of the trends listed above contributed to the 2007-2008 financial crisis; and their reversals are, in part, consequences of the crisis. In this spirit, PIMCO's Bill Gross and Mohamed El-Erian characterize the post-crisis years as the "new normal" environment of slower economic growth and greater government involvement, in which deleveraging, de-globalization, and re-regulation are key features.

Like many others, I expected the crisis to have a long-lasting impact on this generation's risk appetite—echoing that of the conservative "depression babies"—but the sharp risky asset rally in 2009 and early 2010 suggests that the lessons were not very memorable. The mother of all stimulus efforts succeeded in reviving animal spirits in 2009. Asset reflation was the key policy for pulling the economy back from the edge of the precipice. Major contributors were record-low short-term interest rates set by Fed policy; "quantitative easing"; bailouts, guarantees, and support programs; and fiscal expansion. This reflation policy may have worked too well to bolster financial markets but insufficiently to support job markets—the collateral damage to public-sector finances has been substantial.

# Box 27.1. Thoughts on the origins of the financial crisis

The big picture is that the crisis followed the broad underpricing of risk in several asset classes amid persistently loose financial conditions. Recall Alan Greenspan's warning during the boom years: "History has not dealt kindly with the aftermath of protracted periods of low risk premia."

A related interpretation is that the crisis was a modern-day bank run, where a bank run is defined as loss of confidence in a given bank, an asset class, or even the whole financial system. There was arguably too much trust and confidence during the preceding years, leading to complacency and excessive risk taking. Modern financial markets rely on liquidity. Once trust and confidence vanished, so did market liquidity in several assets. Lawrence Summers said in the midst of the crisis: "We got here because there was too much greed and too little fear. We are stuck here now because there is too much fear and too little greed."

Taking an even broader perspective, the latest boom-bust cycle is only one part of the story, the cyclical part. The other, secular, part is the decades-long rise in indebtedness. I already alluded to the debt supercycle; George Soros emphasizes another secular theme, decades of deregulation and market fundamentalism. Yet others stress the paradox of credibility: central bankers' apparent success in taming inflation and the business cycle made investors overconfident of the persistence of the Goldilocks environment, resulting in unsustainable increases in credit growth and asset valuations.

Details make up the rest. There is plenty of blame to go around. Whether most of the blame is assigned to market participants and markets or to politicians, regulators, and monetary policymakers apparently depends more on values than on facts.

I finish this chapter with a few other speculative views—given in what I hope is a very humble tone.

# Unexciting prospects for risky assets

First, I see only moderate returns from risky assets over the medium term. Growth prospects are not promising, given the pendulum shift against free markets and the still stretched private and public balance sheets. Starting yields are historically low. Risky asset valuations are unlikely to improve on a sustained basis, thanks to wealth-dependent risk aversion and lingering memories of the crisis, higher macroeconomic volatility, less trust all around, and less benign inflation prospects for risky assets.

# Relative rise of China and emerging markets

El-Erian (2008) makes a convincing case for a secular handoff of economic power from developed countries to emerging countries, especially from the U.S. to China. Economic growth in Asia ex-Japan is faster and the demographic profile much better than in the

G7 countries. Asian countries have been capital exporters despite their strong growth; even if their economies gradually become more consumer oriented, they are becoming the owners of global capital. By many measures, the economic fundamentals of emerging countries already surpass those of developed countries, and emerging market economic policies have improved dramatically. In contrast, the G7 countries suffered more from the financial crisis, and their aging workforce makes their future almost inevitably bleaker. Current fiscal fundamentals are already better for emerging economies, and the IMF predicts that the gap between the debt/GDP ratios of advanced economies and emerging economies will widen in the coming years, even before the demographic challenge really hits the former.

These favorable structural developments have helped emerging market equities outperform developed market equities for several years. It is also conceivable that an emerging market (or China-specific) bubble will ensue as the secular handoff story garners more followers and as the loose monetary policy that is tailored for the needs of the weak developed economies is exported to the strong emerging economies. As always, the bubble would have a rational precipitating factor, but investors should remember that the link between economic growth and equity market returns is quite tenuous (see Chapter 16).

# The Scylla and Charybdis of deflation and inflation

The disinflationary tailwinds of recent decades—from improvements in central bank independence and credibility as well as from deregulation, globalization, and the massive entry of Chinese workers into the competitive global labor market—may weaken and even turn into headwinds. Moreover, the solutions to some of the major challenges of the 21st century—global warming and the aging population of many countries—are arguably inflationary.

In the shorter term, the financial crisis and the 2008-2009 recession, as well as the subsequent need for belt tightening, are deflationary in their impact. It is hard to see inflation rising in the next year or two, given the elevated unemployment rate and economic slack. Yet, higher inflation down the road is a serious concern and is perhaps made even more likely by any near-term deflation:

- Rising public deficits due to the recession and slow recovery mean that current fiscal challenges will meld into the eventual huge fiscal costs associated with baby-boomers' pensions and health care, with no time for recovery or adjustment. Higher taxes would choke off economic recovery, and suddenly cutting benefits would be painful when people have not had enough time to prepare by saving more privately. Increasing retirement ages will certainly be part of the solution, with the added benefit that in a "knowledge society" older people can be more productive, in contrast to muscle-based pre-industrial societies (idea from Laurence Siegel).
- The central bank is the backstop that can resist or accommodate inflationary pressures. Improved central bank credibility was one major reason for anchoring long-term inflation expectations. We simply do not know what it takes to unmoor those expectations, what it takes to get the genie back in the bottle if this happens, and if the Fed has the stomach for it. Bond vigilantes worry about the Fed's priorities and its independence.

Even if the unmooring of inflation expectations to the upside is scary, the impact of global deflation, if it occurs, is even worse. Debt deflation and hyperinflation are the worst destroyers of wealth. Most policymakers now fear the former more than the latter, which makes inflation (though not hyperinflation) the more likely medium-term outcome.

# Sovereign creditworthiness may be reassessed

Consistent with both themes discussed above, re-rating of perceived creditworthiness may take place. Given the rising fiscal challenges in G7 markets, we could see G7 government bond yields rise above those of top-rated emerging nations and top-rated corporations. Advanced countries do benefit from their record of very rare sovereign defaults—although Reinhart-Rogoff (2008, 2009) show that sovereign defaults have been surprisingly frequent in a longer view of history—especially at times of global inflation pressures (see Figure 27.6). In Reinhart-Rogoff (2010), the authors add that the history of the global public debt/GDP ratio shares similar contours with both lines: peaks in 1932 and 1990 (the 2010 level challenging these peaks) and troughs before World War I, the first oil shock (1973), and the latest financial crisis. Given a hard choice, many countries might opt to resort to the printing press (inflation) before defaulting on their external debt, because the latter action normally carries heavier economic penalties. However, within the EMU, the debt monetization option is precluded for individual countries.

The long-term fiscal challenge from demographics easily overwhelms the problems caused by the financial crisis and recession. Yet, government bond yields remain historically low—aided by near-zero short-term interest rates, central bank purchases, safe haven demand, and a still low inflation risk premium. Some quip that the riskless return in Treasuries has morphed into a returnless risk. After a quarter-century when bearing duration risk was amply rewarded, Treasuries may provide neither safety nor performance in the coming decade.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-22/aa485235-5155-4ade-b88b-be1df0035d75/9cd36944a62e8d4a57f7112243343a1a2e5b4b2db96844a39a36def5fe887d09.jpg)
Figure 27.6. Global perspective on inflation and external default histories. Source: Carmen M. Reinhart and Kenneth S. Rogoff (2008), "This time is different: A panoramic view of eight centuries of financial crises," NBER working paper 13882. Reproduced by permission of Carmen M. Reinhart and Kenneth S. Rogoff.

Figure 27.7. Sovereign CDS spreads, July 2007-July 2010.
![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-22/aa485235-5155-4ade-b88b-be1df0035d75/9263c79ddd4cc487406fef79ebb15ad4dca5be8aea1e82ef4a9584ad2c8567df.jpg)
Source: Bloomberg.

Finally, the last innings of the debt supercycle may arrive even before the demographic costs balloon. Market participants are turning their focus on the magnitude of the fiscal problem just when G7 economies face extraordinary borrowing needs and sharply rising debt/GDP ratios. Figure 27.7 shows how dramatically sovereign credit spreads have widened, especially in countries with more urgent debt troubles. Before 2008 these spreads were razor thin with little differentiation across countries. If, in the future, the market assigns larger required risk premia to highly indebted countries, their real yields will rise and/or their currencies may falter. Major central banks would then face tougher choices between inflation and real economic growth than they have for a generation.

# 27.4 NOTES

[1] Abstracting from important ethical issues and focusing only on financial returns, empirical evidence on the benefits of socially responsible investing (SRI) has been mixed. Derwall-Koedijk-Ter Horst (2010) argue that this ambiguity reflects, on the one hand, the high returns of controversial stocks ("sin stocks") that are negatively screened away from many SRI portfolios and, on the other hand, the high returns of stocks identified and overweighted by positive screens involving environmental and social issues.

Source notes. The crisis has brought a bull market on secular analysis and macro-views. Examples of high-quality sweeping views include Greenspan (2007), El-Erian (2008), Soros (2008), Bank Credit Analyst (2009), Dalio (2009), Reinhart-Rogoff (2009), and Siegel (2009). Thoughtful academic analyses of the crisis and responses to it include Akerlof-Shiller (2009), Brunnermeier (2009), New York University Stern School of Business (2009), French et al. (2010), Gorton (2010), Rajan (2010), and Shin (2010).

# 28

# Enhancing returns through managing risks, horizon, skill, and costs

- This chapter reviews four paths for improving portfolio performance and drills into each of them.
- Risks. The simple way to boost long-run returns is to raise the weight of riskier assets in the portfolio. The more complex but better rewarding approach requires leverage. Within each asset class, buying the riskiest assets almost invariably gives worse long-run returns than levering up lower volatility assets. Even across asset classes, investors can reduce portfolio volatility by smart diversification and then convert improved risk-adjusted returns into higher raw returns by applying moderate leverage.
- Horizon. Investors with a long time horizon can exploit it by over-allocating to illiquid asset classes and reaping related illiquidity premia. Other possibilities include contrarian market timing and opportunistic reinsurance underwriting.
- Skill. Active investors may try to add value through skillful security selection and market timing, either in house or externally. Even when markets are not fully efficient, they are competitive enough to make the task challenging. Building an active management culture and resources internally is not easy, nor is it easy to identify consistent outperformers among external managers.
- Costs. Reducing fees and other implementation costs is often the easiest way to enhance net returns. It is harder to decide on the allocation between (cheap, abundant) beta products and (costly, scarce) alpha, because the higher fees and trading costs in actively managed investments are sometimes worth it. Relative weights should depend on investor views regarding market efficiency and one's own skills at active investing or manager selection.
- After an overview, this chapter drills into each path and also contains essays on related topics such as rebalancing and diversification return, looking beyond the Sharpe ratio, and time diversification.

# 28.1 INTRODUCTION: HOW CAN INVESTORS ENHANCE RETURNS?

It is time to start drawing the investment lessons from the preceding 27 chapters. Chapters 28 and 29 summarize my thinking on how investors can add value. This book's focus is on harvesting various (beta) risk premia, but I also review here debates about skill-based management (alpha seeking). This chapter also drills into certain ways of improving investment performance that go beyond the expected returns of particular investments.


Investors can pursue four paths to improve portfolio performance: managing risks, horizon, skill, and costs. The boundaries between the four paths are admittedly ambiguous and certain activities could be classified under at least two paths. This subsection gives a bird's eye view on each path before turning to more detailed treatments in Sections 28.2-28.5.

This book is primarily about the long-term expected returns of certain asset classes and investment strategies—not about risk management or portfolio construction. However, it turns out that smart diversification can indirectly boost portfolio returns as well as reduce risk.

# 28.1.1 Risks: Bearing more risk and monetizing risk reduction

Paradoxically, expected portfolio returns can be augmented by either increasing or reducing the weight of the most volatile asset class. The classic naive approach is to raise the weight of "high-risk, high-return" equities and reduce the weight of "low-risk, low-return" assets such as cash and government bonds. Bearing more risk in this way, and getting rewarded for it, is indeed the easiest way to boost long-run returns for investors who cannot use leverage. Those who can lever have an arguably better alternative: instead of taking more risk they can take better quality risk. They can reduce portfolio risk through smarter diversification and then "monetize" (capitalize on) the volatility reduction benefit by scaling up position sizes to reach the original volatility level.

Section 28.2.1 reviews one way investors can use better diversification to remedy the classic problem with institutional portfolios, namely concentrated equity market risk. Portfolio rebalancing, volatility targeting, and smart diversification are other ways to reduce portfolio risk and improve risk-adjusted returns (see Sections 28.2.2-28.2.4).

# 28.1.2 Exploiting a long horizon

Investors with a long horizon do not need short-term liquidity. If the market rewards illiquid assets with an illiquidity premium, it makes sense that long-horizon investors over-allocate to such assets. These include private equity and other alternative asset classes as well as certain pockets of traditional asset classes (small-cap stocks, corporate bonds). However, it is folly to expect illiquidity premia to be constant over time or to be realized when extra money happens to be needed. Many investors raised their allocations to illiquid asset classes in 2005–2007 just when their valuations were high and prospective premia negligible.

More generally, long-horizon investors should be better able to take advantage of time-varying expected returns by contrarian market timing. The combination of a long horizon and deep pockets gives some large sovereign wealth funds (SWFs) a natural edge in being the liquidity provider within major asset classes when assets are distressed. These investors could also follow Warren Buffett into some unconventional strategies where patience and deep pockets help. Examples: Opportunistic entries into reinsurance underwriting after recent catastrophes have made reinsurance capital scarce and boosted the prospective rewards for underwriting. The capital infusions many SWFs made into financial institutions in 2007-2008 were in similar spirit, albeit implemented too early.


It is best to be conservative in assessing one's horizon length. Institutional and individual horizons tend to get shorter during financial turmoil, due to true acute cash flow needs or to psychological factors. In preceding years, "true" long-horizon investors had to compete with many "long-horizon wannabes", a situation that pushed ex ante liquidity premia to negligible levels. During the crisis these premia shot up, which to a rational investor is an indicator to hold on to their investments and maybe even exploit new buying opportunities. Yet even some endowments and pension plans—the prototypical long-term investors—felt obliged (or were obliged by cash flow requirements) to sell illiquid holdings into unreceptive markets.

Investors increasingly appreciate the cost of illiquidity (and the risk in assets whose losses covary with deteriorating aggregate liquidity conditions). It is becoming more common to design contracts with external managers in which the end-investor gets appropriately compensated for lockups and other undesirable terms and accepts higher fees for easy liquidity terms. However, the contracted liquidity terms should be realistic, or managers may honor end-investor redemption calls by selling assets at fire-sale prices.

# 28.1.3 Benefiting from skill

Active investors can try to add value through skillful security selection and market timing, sector timing, or style timing. Active investing may be done internally or externally, neither of which is easy in competitive financial markets. Developing sufficient skill in house and maintaining the talent is a major challenge, but so is identifying consistent alpha producers among external managers.

One key distinction is between discretionary and systematic styles. Discretionary managers tend to focus on specific companies, industries, or other individual securities (say, commodity futures) and are usually specialists. Systematic managers emphasize value-added through broad diversification (applying key ideas such as value and momentum in many markets), smart portfolio construction and risk management, and cost-effective implementation. These activities also require skill, but according to some definitions only discretionary managers can provide pure alpha; anything that can be written down is, at best, alternative beta.

Skill-based investing is increasingly associated with HFs. The idea is that alpha is best reaped by well-motivated unconstrained managers using some combination of leverage, long-short investing, and derivatives. Yet, to beat high-fee structures, you need some expertise or luck to pick top-half managers. Success at HF selection is made more plausible because alternative managers exhibit wider performance dispersion as well as greater performance persistence and predictability than traditional managers.

A common view is that skill is best applied in illiquid asset classes and in market segments where mispricings are harder to arbitrage and thus they survive longer. A counterview is that it is easier to identify skill vs. luck in liquid assets with frequent performance measurement. With illiquid assets, effective sample sizes will be tiny. Moreover, if you reassess a manager's skill over time, there will be times (say in 2008) when you may want to get out but you have no flexibility to do so.

# 28.1.4 Cost control

Passive investing minimizes trading costs. However, some costs are worth paying. Buying an equity index fund costs more than investing in a bank deposit, but the equity premium should make the extra cost worthwhile in the long run. Value-oriented non-market-cap weighting involves higher turnover and fees than market cap indices, but proponents of fundamental indexation argue that long-run net returns will be better. Beta products are cheap, while scarcity makes alpha costly; yet it does not automatically follow that alpha allocations should be avoided.

Minimizing costs is not always smart; being cost-effective and avoiding wasteful expense is.

In assessing tradeoffs, such as the relative weights of active and passive (alpha and beta) products, reasonable answers vary across investors. Clearly, a retail investor with high trading costs and few natural edges is almost always better off with low-cost beta products. In contrast, a resource-rich institution with low trading costs has a better chance at succeeding in active management. (A caveat: Large investors often suffer from higher market impact costs.)

In general, investors should allocate more to alpha products the less they believe in market efficiency and the more they believe in their own skills (in active investing or manager selection). Skepticism is healthy. It should be remembered that most active long-only managers underperform their benchmarks after fees and that retail investors fare even worse. The record of HF managers, as a group, looks better but it is hard to fully adjust for the impact of voluntary reporting biases and various risks. To end ahead after fees, you must be able to identify the better managers, which is a challenge.

Alternative managers charge pricey fees, especially if they can boast a good track record. However, trying to beat the markets in-house "on the cheap"—with poorly resourced and incentivized internal managers—may be even more costly. To be cost-effective, some end-investors go for an alpha-beta barbell (combining HFs and passive market indices), while others try to save costs by buying into alternative beta products rather than actual HFs.

Investors should look beyond visible costs even with passive strategies: if there is no cost advantage in staple indices, you should favor customized benchmarks to avoid synchronous portfolio adjustments with big index players. For example, the S&P 500 index loses  $20\mathrm{bp}$  to  $30\mathrm{bp}$  each year due to the market impact of concentrated buying and selling around index additions and deletions. Meanwhile, the way that S&P GSCI Commodity index owners synchronously roll into new futures contracts each month causes a predictable temporary supply-demand imbalance that hurts these index investors.

# 28.2 RISK

This book does not cover the important topics of risk management and portfolio construction except for their mechanical impact on expected returns. Risk reduction through diversification is famously the one free lunch that markets offer, but it improves risk-adjusted returns rather than raw returns. However, Sections 28.2.1 and 28.2.2 show how diversification can boost portfolio returns, almost magically, through leverage or rebalancing. The more typical approach to boosting expected returns is to take more risk, but I remind the reader that not all risks are rewarded and certainly they are not all rewarded equally well. Section 28.2.3 stresses that markets do not treat all volatility as equal, a theme closely related to the shortcomings of the Sharpe ratio, the most common measure of risk-adjusted returns. Section 28.2.4 briefly reviews smart diversification or portfolio construction methods for efficient risk reduction.


# 28.2.1 Monetized risk reduction via leverage

Many retail investor portfolios are insufficiently diversified, perhaps consisting of only a few stocks and having plenty of diversifiable, unrewarded risk. It turns out that typical institutional portfolios are also poorly diversified, but not from holding too small a number of stocks; the reason is that a 60/40 nominal stock/bond allocation implies over  $95\%$  of the risk budget (portfolio volatility) coming from the equity component. Such concentrated risk taking is inefficient; a more balanced risk budget would give higher risk-adjusted returns. One way to improve the portfolio's SR is to lever up the bond component to a volatility level similar to that of the equity component. This approach is often called risk parity investing.

Table 28.1 shows that an equal volatility stock/bond portfolio (i.e., 50/50 volatility weights for stocks and bonds) has a tad higher Sharpe ratio (0.40 vs. 0.38) and a clearly lower correlation with the equity market (0.76 vs. 0.96) than an equal nominal weight stock/bond portfolio. All position sizes can be scaled up or down to reach any desired portfolio volatility target. Investors who accept more leverage can select a higher return for equity-type volatility (the last column in the table) instead of lower volatility for an equity-type expected return. Empirical results are similar whether I use "in-sample" or (trailing) "out-of-sample" volatilities.

The Sharpe ratio improvement in this example is not too compelling, partly because the stock-bond correlation was still positive over this 40-year window. Gains from risk parity investing would rise with a lower stock-bond correlation, lower relative volatility between bonds and stocks, and a higher relative Sharpe ratio between them. Broadening

Table 28.1. Example of how a more balanced stock-bond portfolio can be created, 1970-2009

<table><tr><td></td><td>Stocks</td><td>Bonds (Treasury 7–10)</td><td>50/50 nominal weights</td><td>50/50 volatility weights</td><td>50/50 volatility weights levered to stock market level</td></tr><tr><td>Geometric mean return</td><td>9.80</td><td>8.29</td><td>9.57</td><td>10.31</td><td>11.20</td></tr><tr><td>Arithmetic mean return</td><td>11.25</td><td>8.59</td><td>10.18</td><td>11.13</td><td>12.64</td></tr><tr><td>Volatility</td><td>16.2</td><td>7.6</td><td>10.6</td><td>12.3</td><td>16.2</td></tr><tr><td>Sharpe ratio</td><td>0.31</td><td>0.31</td><td>0.38</td><td>0.40</td><td>0.40</td></tr><tr><td>Bond weight</td><td>0\%</td><td>100\%</td><td>50\%</td><td>106\%</td><td>139\%</td></tr><tr><td>Stock weight</td><td>100\%</td><td>0\%</td><td>50\%</td><td>50\%</td><td>66\%</td></tr><tr><td>Leverage</td><td>0\%</td><td>0\%</td><td>0\%</td><td>56\%</td><td>105\%</td></tr><tr><td>Stock market correlation</td><td>1.00</td><td>0.16</td><td>0.96</td><td>0.76</td><td>0.76</td></tr></table>

Sources: Bloomberg, Citigroup, own calculations.

into several moderately correlated asset classes would improve diversification and the portfolio's SR faster, even if the generally high correlations of most assets with equities limit the benefits.

Leverage can be used to monetize any increase in the portfolio SR. Leverage is needed for two reasons: offsetting the shift into a lower volatility asset and converting the higher portfolio SR into a higher portfolio return:

- Investors who do not seek high raw returns do not need leverage. They benefit directly from better risk-adjusted returns (lower return but much lower volatility).
- Many relatively risk-tolerant long-horizon investors want high returns; yet they may dislike leverage even more than portfolio volatility. When faced with a choice between (1) boosting portfolio returns through leverage and (2) holding concentrated unlevered positions in the most volatile asset class or classes, they may select the latter even if the resulting expected portfolio return is lower.
- One reason for HFs' ability to outperform long-only investors is their capacity and willingness to lever up many small standalone opportunities. Admittedly, leveraged positions come with a greater likelihood of having to de-lever in bad times at fire sale prices.
- More generally, if you monetize diversification gains via leverage, and diversification fails in bad times—recall the correlation spikes in 1998 and 2008—a broadly diversified portfolio can experience sharper losses than a simple equity-concentrated portfolio.

What has been the impact of leverage aversion and leverage constraints on asset returns in the past?

- Return-seeking institutions that face leverage constraints may overpay for asset classes that provide embedded leverage (private equity, hedge funds, structured products) so that they get maximal bang for the buck for their unlevered positions.
- Such return seeking, combined with leverage constraints, has helped make the most volatile assets in each asset class systematically overpriced. That is, the long-run reward for volatility often has a counterintuitive negative sign within asset classes. High-volatility and highly levered stocks have especially low returns; duration extension has paid much better at the front end than at the back end of the curve; in credit markets, low-rated (Caa/CCC and below) high-yield bonds have delivered the worst long-run performance of all rating buckets; and shifting from Treasuries to credits gives consistent rewards only at short maturities. Investors free to apply leverage would get better long-run results in all the above cases by scaling up the opportunities in low-volatility assets (although funding spreads would eat up some of the benefits).
- Avoiding overpriced high-volatility assets gives a second edge to the "levering short-volatility assets" approach. Even without overpricing, merely achieving superior diversification gives this approach an edge.

Leverage has now a worse reputation than before 2007-2008 because it is widely blamed for the crisis. At the very least, the leverage of banks will soon be more tightly restricted by new regulations. The practical impact will be more pronounced risk premia for low-volatility assets, more sustained mispricings among them, and greater opportunities for those who can still apply leverage.

# 28.2.2 Rebalancing and "diversification return"

Rebalancing asset classes toward target weights is a common risk control approach. Somewhat miraculously, under relatively undemanding assumptions (but not in all possible scenarios), rebalancing also boosts a portfolio's long-run compound return. In most cases this effect is quite mild, but it is interesting that this return enhancement does not require leverage.

Market moves can make asset class weights drift from their target weights—say, a 60/40 stock/bond allocation. Rebalancing refers to trading activity that counters such a drift. It involves buying asset classes that recently underperformed and selling recent outperformers. Rebalancing may be calendar based or trigger based, and a rebalancing rule may push asset weights all the way back to the target or only to the edge of a "no-trading band".

It makes intuitive sense that rebalancing is profitable if asset class returns are mean-reverting (at frequencies well synchronized with rebalancing activity). More surprisingly, rebalancing turns out to boost long-run compound returns even if asset returns exhibit no mean reversion tendency. My analysis is mainly based on three papers: Booth-Fama (1992), Bernstein-Wilkinson (1997), and Erb-Harvey (2006).

Here are the key ideas behind how this gain arises in a world with zero-autocorrelated returns (neither mean reversion nor momentum bias in returns, and asset prices follow a random walk):

- The technical reason can be traced to a distinction between arithmetic and geometric means. An asset's or a portfolio's geometric mean (GM) (i.e., its compound return) depends both on its arithmetic mean (AM) and its variance. Higher volatility erodes compound returns and thus wealth accumulation over time.
- Diversification reduces a portfolio's variance and thus boosts its GM, given constant AMs. Diversification return (DR) is the excess of a portfolio's GM over its constituents' weighted average GM.
- Rebalancing to constant weights is needed to earn the DR. Rebalancing ensures diversification—by not letting one asset class grow to dominate the portfolio.

# Box 28.1. Arithmetic and geometric means

As noted in Section 4.3, the arithmetic mean of a series is always higher than the geometric mean (AM > GM) except when there is zero volatility (AM = GM). A simple Taylor series expansion shows that a good approximation is:  $\mathrm{GM} \approx \mathrm{AM} - \mathrm{Variance}/2$ .

There has been an ongoing debate in financial circles for decades as to which measure is more appropriate for assessing long-term expected returns and terminal wealth. Textbooks have traditionally sided with AM, while practitioners often prefer GM, perhaps because the latter is correct for measuring realized compound return.

The AM-GM distinction is related to the difference between mean and median. Long-horizon cumulative asset returns are highly skewed: expected returns are unbounded from above—asset prices can rise hundreds of percent or more—but have a bounded downside as they can "only" lose  $-100\%$ . Due to positive skew- ness caused by a few extremely positive outcomes, the mathematical expected return (AM) can be a very atypical outcome. While AMs overstate the long-run future return investors are likely to realize, GMs are more like medians that give more realistic forecasts for long-horizon investors. In the presence of estimation errors, an unbiased estimate of future portfolio value depends more on the GM the longer the time horizon (for a given historical sample length).


The  $GM \approx AM -$  Half the variance approximation holds for a single asset and for a portfolio, including a market index. For example, if equities have a long-run annualized volatility of  $20\%$ , variance is  $20\%^2 = 4\%$ . Thus, the expected variance drain (the gap between AM and GM) is  $2\%$ . (Actual data on the annual returns of U.S. large-cap stocks between 1926 and 2006 agree: the AM was  $12.3\%$ , GM  $10.4\%$ , and volatility  $20\%$ .) For bond indices with annual return volatility of  $6\%$  to  $7\%$ , the variance drain is only about  $0.2\%$ .

Because the geometric mean reflects both the arithmetic mean and variance, some academics and practitioners have long recommended the multi-period strategy of maximizing the portfolio's expected compound return as an alternative to mean variance optimization.

# "Turning water into wine"

Here is an example of diversification return from Erb-Harvey (2006): an annually rebalanced portfolio consisting of  $50\%$  heating oil and  $50\%$  stocks had an excess return (GM) of  $11.0\%$  over a decade, much higher than either component. Heating oil had an excess return (GM) of  $8.2\%$  but an AM near  $18\%$ . Stocks had an excess return (GM) of  $6.8\%$  but an AM near  $9\%$ . Diversification return was about  $3.5\%$ , thanks to variance reduction.

# Diversification reduces the variance drain and thereby boosts portfolio geometric means

A portfolio's return equals the (weighted) average AM return of its constituents whereas variances are not additive unless constituents are perfectly correlated. Diversification is the rare free lunch available for all investors: we can reduce portfolio volatility without reducing its AM. This volatility reduction improves the portfolio's GM by reducing its variance drain. It follows that the GM of a portfolio exceeds the average GM of its constituents. The difference is the DR. That is, investors can raise their portfolio GM by identifying a new investment that is uncorrelated with the original portfolio and has comparable return. This gain (DR) is proportional to half the new asset's variance. I stress that diversification boosts the GM but not the AM. Yet, since diversification reduces volatility, investors who can lever up their portfolio back to the original volatility level can also enhance their AM.

# Importance of rebalancing

In practice, earning the DR requires rebalancing to some constant weights (not necessarily equal). Rebalancing reduces the weights of outperforming assets. If asset weights are allowed to drift significantly over time, the most successful assets over long samples may come to dominate portfolio weights and dilute any diversification effects.

Erb and Harvey stress that the DR really reflects two features: the variance drain and rebalancing. They give the label "covariance drag" to the negative impact on GM of not rebalancing; by definition, this drag is zero for continuously rebalanced strategies. In practice, unrebalanced strategies (with "let-it-run" weights following some initial weighting) often have a near-zero DR. The variance reduction benefit and covariance drag roughly offset each other, thereby making the portfolio GM roughly equal to the constituents' average GM. This is also true for a market-weighted portfolio, which is just one example of an unrebalanced strategy.

# Factors that drive diversification return

The DR is especially large for portfolios with high asset volatility and low correlations. Because the constituents of typical equity portfolios (or bond portfolios) tend to be relatively highly correlated, their DR is modest. Moreover, since typical equity and bond portfolios are rarely rebalanced to constant weights, their covariance drag likely offsets the variance drain effect. When rebalancing asset allocation weights between equity and bond portfolios (and other asset classes), the DR should be higher, given the low correlations between asset classes.

As a good approximation, expected equally weighted rebalanced portfolio DR (1) rises with the number of assets in the portfolio, (2) is proportional to average variance, and (3) is negatively proportional to the average correlation among portfolio constituents.

# Extension 1 Relatively similar long-run expected returns across assets favor rebalancing

The analysis of Bernstein-Wilkinson (1997) is more general than that of Erb and Harvey and highlights the role of expected return differences across assets better. First, the intuition: If assets in the portfolio have persistently different expected returns, rebalancing involves gradual selling of high-return assets. A no-rebalancing strategy would let the highest return asset eventually grow to constitute the entire portfolio and thus make the portfolio's expected return equal the highest returning asset's expected return. Given sufficiently large expected return differences across assets, the benefit from this effect must exceed any diversification benefit maintained via rebalancing.

This study shows that the GM gap between rebalanced and unrebalanced portfolios can be proxied by two terms:

- the first term favors the unrebalanced portfolio; it is large when GMs are highly dispersed across assets and is zero when all assets have equal GMs—in line with the Bernstein-Wilkinson generalization;
- the second term favors the rebalanced portfolio; it is large when individual assets have large variances and low/negative correlations—in line with Erb and Harvey's analysis.

Empirical exercises suggest that the latter term dominates. Only when GM differences are very wide (say, rebalancing between low-yielding Treasury bills and high-return assets) does the net result favor unrebalanced portfolios.

# Extension 2 Mean reversion or momentum bias in returns can influence the optimal rebalancing interval

The above calculations assume returns with zero autocorrelation, so diversification return does not rely on mean-reverting asset prices. If asset returns are negatively autocorrelated, performance may be further boosted by picking an expedient rebalancing interval. (This is much easier with hindsight.) Conversely, if returns exhibit short-term momentum, very frequent rebalancing can detract from performance.

In an ideal world of zero-autocorrelated returns and no trading costs, very frequent rebalancing makes sense because it reduces both tracking error and (typically negative) covariance drag. However, the presence of trading costs and, possibly, short-term return momentum make longer rebalancing intervals more attractive.

# A close cousin: Volatility targeting

Instead of targeting certain nominal weights, leveraged investors often target some overall volatility level and certain relative volatility (or VaR contribution) weights for strategies or asset classes. Indeed, return volatility is a more natural measurement unit (than dollar allocation) for strategies or assets whose unlevered standalone volatilities differ hugely. HFs use various ways of scaling or targeting volatility to improve their portfolios. When trading a long-short position, pairwise volatility scaling (weighting long and short legs by their empirical volatilities or betas) reduces and (if risk estimates are accurate) potentially eliminates the position's market directionality. Assigning equal volatilities to an active portfolio's risk sources or constructing a minimum variance portfolio tends to improve diversification and thereby risk-adjusted returns. Targeting volatility over time seems to help stabilize risk (reduce the volatility of volatility) and even enhance performance because higher near-term volatility does not enhance near-term expected return, for almost any investment is more predictable in the near term than returns are. Simply targeting a constant volatility often improves long-run returns compared with a strategy of maintaining constant nominal position sizes over time.

# 28.2.3 Risk adjustment: Looking beyond the Sharpe ratio

The SR is by far the most popular measure of risk-adjusted return for absolute return managers and investment strategies. It is rightly viewed as a tremendously useful tool for investment analysis and portfolio construction. Indeed, investors often make SR maximization the key objective for their portfolio [1].

Yet, the SR has its limitations, mirroring those of volatility as a risk measure for a portfolio. The world is too complex for any single number to capture its multi-faceted risks. Simply put, not all volatility is equal. Reasonable investors may accept a lower portfolio SR in exchange for improving other portfolio characteristics. I stress four arguments that justify looking beyond volatility as the risk measure and thus beyond SR as the performance measure:

- losses in bad times hurt more than bull market losses—thus protecting the portfolio in bad times is essential;

- large losses are particularly disliked—thus investors should differentiate between upside and downside volatility:
- illiquid assets warrant higher SRs as a result of illiquidity premia and understated volatilities:
- adverse tail events are made more likely by complexity, operational risk, and other unquantifiable features.

Each argument implies a tradeoff between the SR and some other portfolio attribute. This subsection brings together the many dimensions of the problem and provides empirical examples.

# Tradeoff #1 Timing of losses

The SR does not care whether gains or losses occur under good or bad economic conditions. Most investors do. For a given SR, they prefer an investment that made money in 2008 over one that made money in 2006.

The year 2008 was the annus horribilis for today's investors. Besides re-acquiring general respect for risk in 2008, investors re-learned to differentiate between volatility sources:

- Nearly all risky and illiquid assets, as well as positive-carry strategies and absolute return managers, turned out to be bad diversifiers: they became even more positively correlated than in normal times.
- Good diversifiers were rare. The scarcity of safe haven assets makes them especially valuable, as they provide diversification when it is most needed. Trend-following strategies were among the few safe havens, besides government bonds.

These patterns are not unique to 2008. We can use two contrasting trading styles—carry seeking and trend following—as illustrations of bad and good diversifiers. This example has broader applicability because many fund managers and HF style indices are well characterized by their exposure to either style (or to both). Figure 28.1 highlights the

Figure 28.1. Monthly returns for carry and trend-style proxies in the 15 worst months for global stocks, 1985-2009.
![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-22/aa485235-5155-4ade-b88b-be1df0035d75/bb9abcbf87ed679e81326b288d9b5f6893a1ae49762301174fafde6c279cfe9b.jpg)
Sources: Bloomberg, Barclays Capital, Citigroup, J.P. Morgan, Brevan Howard, own calculations.

opposite directional risks of broad carry and trend composites, two of our simulated strategy portfolios [2]. Looking at the 15 worst months for global equities since 1985 (5\% of the left tail), the carry strategy lost money in 11 of the 15 months, while the trend strategy was profitable in 13. The carry strategy is also positively correlated with equity market indices, broad hedge fund indices, and large institutional portfolios, while the trend strategy is negatively related to most of them.

# Tradeoff #2 Return asymmetry

Volatility treats gains and losses equally. Investors of course like gains and dislike losses—especially large losses (negative skewness). When it comes to return asymmetry, the trend proxy has greater upside volatility than downside volatility while the opposite is true for the carry proxy (and most risky assets). Moreover, the trend proxy has much smaller maximum drawdown and better skewness characteristics (where positive skewness is preferred to negative) than the carry proxy.

Both style proxies have been profitable in recent decades, with appealing long-run paper SRs near one [3]. When we look beyond the SR, the trend style is clearly the more benign source of return, given its superior diversification ability and positive skew. In contrast, the carry style resembles "selling lottery tickets that pay off in bad times", combining negative skewness with toxic timing of losses—besides being vulnerable to abuse by unskilled managers.

# Tradeoff #3 Liquidity

The SR ignores liquidity considerations, but we know that illiquid assets have artificially smooth returns and thus overstated SRs. Moreover, illiquid assets should offer some compensation (illiquidity premium) for their higher trading costs and lesser flexibility as well as for their tendency to underperform during liquidity droughts.

Both considerations imply that investors should require higher measured SRs for illiquid asset classes than for liquid asset classes. Recall that Figure 2.2 showed a positive relation across asset classes between our illiquidity estimates and long-run SRs. It is possible, however, that the desire of many investors to avoid mark-to-market volatility makes less liquid assets overpriced, reducing the reward for illiquidity.

# Tradeoff #4 Unquantifiable tail risks

Some investors may also trade off the SR against complexity, opacity, novelty, funding leverage, overcrowding, or operational risk. These attributes are less quantifiable than the portfolio's diversification or asymmetry characteristics but they all contribute to tail risks. Being simpler and more robust, more transparent and better tested, not being hostage to a lender's whims or to sharp liquidations, and not being subject to blatant operational risks are all factors that arguably make tail events less likely. Investors may focus too much on measurable "normal times" risks and too little on unquantifiable risks. The latter often dominate tail events and disproportionately occur in bad times. Operational risks (errors and fraud) are a good example; the SR of Madoff's track record was hard to beat but it came with huge operational risk.

# Conclusions

The portfolio SR is a good starting point but it needs to be supplemented with other portfolio attributes. All of the desirable attributes discussed above may be worth some SR sacrifice. However, no single risk-adjusted return measure can capture them all, and many of these tradeoffs can only be assessed in a qualitative fashion. Multiple performance metrics are needed, given the multi-dimensional nature of the problem.

# 28.2.4 Smart risk taking and portfolio construction

There now follow some intuitive rules of thumb for smart investing: a recipe for optimal diversification and the "fundamental law of active management".

First, here is a recipe for smart portfolio construction, which sums up mean variance optimization in a nutshell: allocate equal volatility to each asset class (or return source) in a portfolio, unless some assets' exceptional SRs or diversification abilities justify deviating from equal volatility weightings. Asset classes with high SRs or with negative correlations (vs. the rest of the portfolio) should be overweighted until the portfolio SR can no longer be improved.

- If assets are equally attractive in terms of expected returns and diversification abilities, they deserve equal volatility shares in an optimal portfolio. Allocating equal volatility shares maximizes diversification if all assets have similar diversification abilities.
- Equal volatility portfolios ignore information about expected returns—as do minimum variance or maximum diversification portfolios. These approaches implicitly assume that all assets have the same reward-risk ratio.
- Since this recipe loosely mimics mean variance optimization, it shares any shortcomings of the mean variance framework, such as ignoring liquidity considerations.

The hugely influential fundamental law of active management, discovered by Richard Grinold, states that, under certain simplifying assumptions, a portfolio's risk-adjusted return in excess of its benchmark (i.e., the portfolio's information ratio or IR), reflects skill and breadth:

$$

\mathrm {I R} \approx \mathrm {I C} * \sqrt {\text {B r e a d t h}}

$$ where the information coefficient, IC, is the amount of investment skill per trade, measuring the investor's forecasting ability by the correlation between predictions and realizations; and breadth is the number of independent trades (i.e., trades not using the same information) per year, representing diversification opportunities. (Note that the IR is the part of the SR due to active management against a benchmark, the rest is due to variation in the returns of the benchmark. If the portfolio's benchmark has zero volatility, then  $\mathrm{IR} = \mathrm{SR}$ .)

The breadth concept underscores the shortcomings of market-timing strategies where few independent views are taken each year. Breadth can be improved by selecting portfolios from a large set of securities, by combining many strategies, or by taking views more frequently. That is, for a single strategy, updating a view with a given predictive correlation (IC) on a weekly basis boosts the SR or IR about seven times ( $\sqrt{52}$ ) more than updating a view with the same correlation on an annual basis. Apparently, if you market-time, you should change your views often (as long as your views have a reasonably high probability of being correct). In reality it is harder to make accurate forecasts every week than once a year; moreover, higher trading costs may fully offset any benefits of fast-moving signals.

It seems likely that widening breadth comes at the expense of lesser skill. Breadth also assumes that trades are uncorrelated, while in reality many trades tend to use the same information, making them positively correlated. Highly positively correlated trades are poor diversifiers, while negatively correlated trades are excellent diversifiers. The latter are rare but are often worth adding to the portfolio even if they (modestly) reduce its expected returns. Examples of pairs of such super-diversifiers include pro-growth equities and anti-growth Treasuries, the value or contrarian style and the momentum style, and carry and trend styles.

Extensions to the fundamental law incorporate constraints, trading costs, or illiquidity; allowing correlations between forecasts and assets; recognizing the uncertainty of inputs, or relaxing other underlying assumptions such as normally distributed asset returns or mean variance preferences:

- When assets and/or strategies have symmetrically distributed returns, volatility and downside risk measures are interchangeable. When not, it is useful to track portfolio skewness or downside risk measures as well as overall volatility. For many traders, stop-loss limits and other risk management rules are essential tools, but the use of risk control techniques may reduce expected returns.
- Robust approaches and simple measures bring more stable performance in a world where non-normalities, outliers and rare events, estimation errors, and time-varying relations abound. Moreover, we rarely find that complex approaches really improve real-world performance, though this is naturally a topic of debate among quants.

Diversification of course has limitations, one of which is the tendency for correlations to approach one during crises [4]. Many good traders and absolute return managers distinguish themselves by managing downside risk instead of just relying on diversification. They use options, stop-loss rules, and innovative ways to buy tail risk insurance to limit the downside. Absolute return managers often emphasize that risk management and avoidance of large drawdowns is key, first, to their survival and, second, to their good long-run compound returns.

# 28.3 INVESTMENT HORIZON

# Exploiting a long horizon

Long-horizon investors are well suited to capturing positive illiquidity premia, term premia, and even other risk premia (as they can tolerate such risks better than the average investor in the market). True long-horizon investors are a minority in the marketplace. Indeed, the relative share of short-horizon and long-horizon investors in the market should influence the size of such premia. Arguably, the share of both true and wannabe long-horizon investors grew rapidly in the mid-2000s, a situation that contributed to excessive narrowing of these premia in 2005–2007. Long-horizon investors may not only have a natural edge in generally holding illiquid and risky assets but also in being opportunistic buyers and liquidity providers whenever the market's risk aversion and liquidity needs are especially high [5].

# Box 28.2. Compound returns

A probably apocryphal quote has Einstein calling compound interest the eighth wonder of the world. Table 28.2 illustrates the power of compounding. Over 50 years, an investment earning a  $4\%$  annual return grows sevenfold. If a  $10\%$  return can be achieved, it grows 117-fold! Any investment marketer worth her salt knows that showing final wealth over a multi-decade window is much more impressive than showing annualized return. To really benefit from compounding, an investor needs time, return, and re-investing. The last two rows of Table 28.2 show that 111 of the 117 final dollars were due to re-investment return.

The best known story of compound interest is the legend of Indians selling the island of Manhattan in 1626 to a Dutch merchant for  \$24 worth of glass beads. Had these Indians invested and earned a compound return near 6\%$  for 380 years, their stake would have grown to about 100 billion with which they could have bought back a good chunk of Manhattan real estate in 2006—with improvements. Had the Indians managed to compound their wealth at an  $8\%$  rate, the price of the beads would have grown into 120 trillion—close to the best current estimate of all financial wealth in the world.

Of course, nobody could ever compound wealth over centuries at  $6\%$ , let alone at  $8\%$ . Even if the U.S. equity market had offered close to an  $8\%$  nominal compound return over the past 200 years, taxes, fees, and trading costs would have chipped away a decent chunk of this return. Just as importantly or more so, no family or institution has had the patience to keep re-investing for centuries without redemptions and spending.

In Table 28.2 I underline two values near 2. They are examples of the convenient "rule of 72" which says that the number of years in which you double your money is approximated by the number 72 divided by the percentage rate of return. Doubling your money takes 72 years at  $1\%$ , 16 years at  $4\%$ , about 10 years at  $7\%$ , and about 7 years at  $10\%$ .

Table 28.2. Compound growth of 1

<table><tr><td>Rate (row)/Horizon (column)</td><td>1 year</td><td>10 years</td><td>30 years</td><td>50 years</td><td>70 years</td></tr><tr><td>1\%</td><td>1.01</td><td>1.10</td><td>1.35</td><td>1.64</td><td>2.01</td></tr><tr><td>4\%</td><td>1.04</td><td>1.48</td><td>3.24</td><td>7.11</td><td>15.57</td></tr><tr><td>7\%</td><td>1.07</td><td>1.97</td><td>7.61</td><td>29.46</td><td>113.99</td></tr><tr><td>10\%</td><td>1.10</td><td>2.59</td><td>17.45</td><td>117.39</td><td>789.75</td></tr><tr><td>10\%, no re-investment</td><td>1.10</td><td>2.00</td><td>4.00</td><td>6.00</td><td>8.00</td></tr></table>

The investment horizon can be intimately tied to an institution's liabilities, explicit or implicit. For many pension funds and life insurance companies, long-dated (nominal or real) liabilities imply that long-dated (nominal or real) bonds are effectively riskless assets. Indeed, these institutions' demand for long-dated bonds has contributed to the flatness or inversion of the back end of many yield curves. In many real-world cases, the investment horizon is uncertain (and the perceived horizon is often positively related to market conditions and investor risk appetite). Banks, central banks, and corporations tend to have a short horizon, while customer deposits, FX reserves, and excess cash tend to be held for long periods. These institutions need to be prepared to satisfy sudden cash demands (such as are caused by a bank run, currency crisis, or various corporate expenses). Endowments, foundations, and sovereign wealth funds are closest to having permanent capital, but the first two have recurring spending needs each year, whereas the sovereign wealth funds of some commodity-rich countries can expect their net inflows to grow for another decade and net outflows to start only in the distant future.

# Time diversification

Time diversification—the idea that stock market investing becomes less risky with a longer horizon—is a contentious issue. Jeremy Siegel, who wrote several editions of a book entitled Stocks for the Long Run, has studied over two centuries of U.S. equity market returns and publicized the empirical fact that, although short-term returns are nearly as likely to be down as up, the probability of losing money over a 20-year window has been negligible. Realized variances of annualized returns over long horizons are significantly lower than those over short horizons. These results could reflect long-run mean reversion and/or exceptionally benign market experience in the U.S., but they may be more generally true.

Indeed, it is a mathematical fact that if stock returns have zero autocorrelation (i.e., if they are a random walk) the volatility of average annual returns falls with a longer horizon, and if stock returns are a random walk with upward drift (i.e., positive expected return) the likelihood of negative cumulative returns falls with a longer horizon. Critics counter that the volatility of cumulative returns and the magnitude of potential losses increases with the time horizon.

Kritzman (2000) reminds us of Paul Samuelson's mathematical result that under certain conditions expected utility maximizers should hold a constant share of their wealth in risky assets irrespective of their investment horizon. Merely having a longer horizon does not make investors more risk tolerant as long as they have constant relative risk aversion, asset returns follow a random walk, and future wealth depends only on investment returns—not on human capital. Relaxing any of these assumptions can justify a greater weight in risky assets for long-horizon investors. Thus stock market investing can be safer for them:

(i) if they exhibit risk aversion that is less wealth dependent than that of typical investors (whose risk aversion may increase sharply when they become poorer and approach their subsistence level); or

(ii) if stock returns are mean-reverting (losses over one period are more likely to be offset by higher returns in the future, thereby reducing multi-year volatility); or

(iii) if they are young with flexible and plentiful human capital (first, in case equity returns disappoint, they can offset the impact by working a little harder to supplement their labor income; second, the mere fact that human capital is a larger share of their total wealth when they are young suggests that they can take more risk with their financial wealth).

One strand of the debate over time diversification studies the preference-free environment of option pricing; some argue that the cost of insurance against declines in the market rises with the length of the time horizon, others that the cost of such insurance declines on a per annum basis.

New research adds a powerful argument against time diversification. Pastor and Stambaugh stress the uncertainty about mean returns, which is a more important long-term risk than the classic measure (short-term volatility around the mean). Mean stock market return has been near  $10\%$  in the past, but this is only an estimate, and the future mean will almost certainly be different. When uncertainty about the true mean (and other parameters) is included, long-horizon investors face more volatility than short-horizon investors.

# Box 28.3. Retail investors and the time dimension

Lifecycle investing. As noted above, young people can more easily compensate for poor realized market returns with greater work effort; therefore young people can hold a larger share of their wealth in stocks. Many financial advisors use the rule of thumb "percentage in bonds and cash equals your age". This idea is at the heart of so-called target date funds. While the proximate goal is to ensure sufficient retirement income, the broader goal is consumption smoothing over the lifecycle. Others emphasize the importance of guaranteeing some minimum level of inflation-protected pension income by purchasing real lifetime annuities. Pension investment decisions are further complicated by the uncertainty of personal health costs and longevity (health care inflation has long outpaced general consumer price inflation), by insufficient pension savings, and by concerns about pension providers' or governments' ability to fulfill their (often excessive) commitments. Given the large demographic changes expected in the first half of the 21st century, these issues will surely have a large impact on asset returns. Unfortunately, these important topics are outside the scope of this book.

Dollar cost averaging. One common investment approach is to invest a constant dollar amount into the equity market on a regular basis. (This approach is called dollar cost averaging, because with a constant outlay every month you end up buying more shares at low prices and fewer at high prices.) The main advantage is that dollar cost averaging gives discipline to an individual's plan for saving by investing in equities.
