It can also reduce the regret associated with mistiming purchases and it may benefit from any mean reversion in aggregate market valuations. However, excluding the mean reversion argument (on which empirical evidence is weak), standard finance theory says that dollar cost averaging is suboptimal. If investors postpone equity investments despite having earned and saved a given amount of cash income, they delay earning the equity premium and thus reduce long-run returns. If the optimal portfolio is a given combination of riskless assets and risky equity, theory advises instantly allocating any new cash income to riskless and risky assets in these optimal proportions. Newer ideas about time-varying risk premia may justify the contrarian market timing implicit in dollar cost averaging, but only for those investors who differ from the average market participant. Finally, in a world of low risky asset premia, the benefits of discipline come more cheaply.

# 28.4 SKILL

# Finding skill

Besides trying to reap risk and illiquidity premia, trying to add value by skillful active management is the other popular way to try to boost the prospective returns on one's portfolio. It is well known that beating the market is difficult, but few deem it impossible. (Over  $80\%$  of U.S. equity mutual fund assets are actively managed, and over  $50\%$  of institutional mandates are for active management.) Traditionally, most academics argue that markets are broadly efficient (see Section 5.3), while many practitioners laugh at the notion. In addition to deciding whether the market is too efficient for them to bother to try to beat it, investors need to judge their own ability to be among the winners in a negative-sum game.

Given that prior views on this question are so polarized, it is perhaps not surprising that there are two public puzzles that ask virtually opposite questions: (i) Why is most delegated portfolio management still allocated to active managers? and (ii) Why is such a small share of institutional risk budgets allocated to active managers?

- Equilibrium accounting shows that the sum of all active risk-taking positions—deviations from a passive market portfolio—must amount to zero. Thus, active management is a zero-sum game before costs and fees, and a negative-sum game after they are included. Any gains one active investor makes must come at the expense of a losing active investor. The average active investor must deliver a lower net return than the average passive investor who benefits from much lower costs and fees. In jargon, the net alpha is negative.
- Robert Litterman's "active risk puzzle" notes that only a small share of institutional risk budgets is allocated to active management. (Even when capital is allocated to long-only active managers, tracking error targets tend to be much lower than the volatility of underlying assets.) Given that equity market risk takes up  $90\%$  of many risk budgets, and the related SR is about 0.3, the small share of active risk must imply a much lower expected SR for active management. Yet, virtually anyone marketing active management services promises a higher SR, so a higher allocation to active risk should be easy to justify.

The two puzzles address two distinct issues. The first puzzle is about the inability of the average active investor to beat the market, while the second one is about identifying superior managers (or being one oneself). Overconfidence, salesmanship, and ignorance make most investors—amateur or professional—believe that they can beat the market or select winning managers. It is part of human nature that we believe that we are above average like the mythical children of Lake Wobegon and, certainly, all professional active managers (must) profess to be superior investors. Only past winners market their track record; others toil silently or leave the field. Most people prefer to overlook these facts.

Truly superior investors do exist, but they are rare, hard to find in advance, and costly. Likewise, identifying winning individual securities requires a lot of effort, or luck:

- There will always be some manager who is outperforming—and a small minority have consistently succeeded over very long time periods. Many academics argue that most superstars may just be lucky, given the broad universe of candidates. Indeed, evidence on performance persistence among mutual fund managers is scant, although it is better among alternative asset managers.
- Manager return predictability is improved if there are shared characteristics among the few consistent outperformers. Warren Buffett famously responded to academic claims about lucky coin-flippers that a disproportionately large number of winners in the investment game are residents of Graham-and-Doddsville (i.e., value-oriented investors). In contrast, successful market-timers are a rare species. (Not even purists assign all the success of Warren Buffett to luck rather than skill, but they may insist that individual superstars are identified with hindsight. For example, the star status of Bill Miller and Ken Griffin was questioned after both stumbled in 2008.)
- One promising shared characteristic is manager willingness to take active risk instead of hugging the benchmark. There is evidence that mutual funds with a high "active share" (fraction of portfolio that differs from the benchmark index), or those with a low  $R^2$  against the market, tend to outperform peer funds. Also, in the HF space, the most distinctive or crowd-bucking managers perform especially well. Echoing these results, both mutual fund managers' best ideas and HF managers' high-conviction trades demonstrably add value. So why do managers with apparent stock-picking skills tend to underperform their benchmarks? It is lukewarm positions added to the portfolio to boost diversification and to reduce tracking error against the index that appear to detract from value.
- Investor skill does not automatically ensure gains for end-investors. The influential Berk-Green (2004) model of delegated asset management implies that competition for skillful managers eliminates any gains for investors in active funds even when skillful managers and performance persistence exist. Investors correctly take past outperformance as a signal of investment quality, but performance-chasing demand allows fund managers to extract all the rents they can from their skill; they raise their AUM until they no longer offer excess profits after fees and transaction costs.

Life would be easier if some clear investor group could be identified as exhibiting superior performance and steadily extracting alpha from other market participants:

- The old idea was that professional investors outperform at the expense of individual investors. However, hundreds of studies since the 1960s confirm that the majority of professional active managers underperform their benchmarks (not retail investors' benchmarks, but institutional investors' own benchmarks). Most studies compare active mutual fund manager returns with index returns; for example, Wermers (2000) and Fama-French (2009) find that average returns in excess of a relevant index are mildly positive before fees but negative after fees. Taking a broader approach, French (2008) estimates various costs and fees that the U.S. active equity management industry accrues; he concludes that end-investors have on aggregate overspent about  $70\mathrm{bp}$  per year, compared with a passive alternative. The aggregate cost has been surprisingly stable over time because lower trading costs have been offset by a shift to higher fee products and higher turnover.
- Evidence is more mixed for managers of institutional funds. These funds tend to outperform their mutual fund peers, likely reflecting lower fees and lower agency costs. However, pension plan sponsors do not exhibit manager selection skills; newly fired managers tend to subsequently outperform newly hired managers [6]. Endowment funds show generally good performance and the largest endowments have the best track records. This evidence is consistent with the idea that large, resource-rich institutions can add value, but it could also reflect the one-time effect of early entry into alternative assets.
- Hedge funds are the prototypical skilled investors of the 21st century. It looks like HFs can outperform even as a group by extracting alpha from others—long-only institutions, retail investors, corporations, and the public sector. Section 11.4.2 reviews various good reasons for HFs' success (expertise, flexibility, motivation, breadth) but also doubts about their record and its sustainability (reporting biases, hidden risks, growing competition, high fees, and asymmetric incentives). Finally, even those who question whether the average HF can provide positive net alpha tend to recognize that more investors with superior skills exist in this universe than anywhere else.

Alpha-beta separation and portable alpha have been popular themes during the past decade. Traditional fund managers bundle alpha and beta together at relatively unattractive combined costs. (Active long-only managers take modest active risks and deliver even more modest active returns, but the fees, while modest by alternative investment standards, are often shockingly high as a proportion of value added, often actually exceeding the value added.) End-investors found that by separating alpha bets and beta bets, they could save costs and make more efficient risk allocations. End-investors have therefore consistently de-allocated capital from traditional active managers and moved it toward an alpha-beta barbell (as well as to alternative asset classes). HFs, ideally market-neutral ones, form the natural alpha (skill) source, while low-fee index funds or index derivatives form the natural source of beta (risk premia). The combined fees for this barbell can be lower than for traditional managers, and combined after-fee returns higher. There are limits to this trend, however: separating alpha and beta is much harder, perhaps infeasible, in less liquid asset classes such as private equity and real estate.

# Applying skill

It is too simplistic to equate skills and HFs, alpha and HFs, or skills and alpha. Not all HF managers are skilled and not all skilled investors work for HFs. HFs do not only deliver alpha but also traditional and alternative betas (see Section 11.4.3, which stresses that alpha is always defined in the context of a factor model as the uncorrelated, unexplained part of returns). Skill is not only used in alpha generation (narrowly defined as view taking) but also in smart portfolio construction, risk management, and cost-efficient implementation. Critics emphasize fees and agency problems which can prevent the fruits of skillful management from reaching the end-investor. Last but not least, ex post alpha reflects luck (randomness) as well as skill, and possibly also overfitting or reporting biases. (For these reasons, it is important to evaluate investments by the ex ante quality of decision making, not just by ex post success.)

Skill can be applied in various ways. If we focus narrowly on view taking, the classic distinction is between actively timing the market and selecting securities, or, in between, allocating across asset classes, countries, sectors, styles, or managers. Another distinction is between discretionary and systematic managers, again with a fuzzy boundary. Most managers use similar information—both fundamental and technical indicators—but the discretionary ones make subjective decisions based on them, while the systematic ones try to code up their week-to-week decision rules. Finally, skill can be applied in in-house active management or in selecting external managers.

Skill does not alone determine expected value-added; one also needs opportunities to apply any skills. Mechanically, the fundamental law of active management says that expected risk-adjusted return depends on investing skill and the breadth of the opportunity set to which the skill is applied. For a single trade, the expected risk-adjusted alpha depends on skill and signal strength (size of the current opportunity). Discretionary traders quickly dilute their skill when they widen their interests. Systematic investors can better exploit breadth (with their admittedly narrower information set).

Where is skill best applied? A common view is that alpha is best found in illiquid markets with inefficiently priced assets—where assets are harder to value, information travels more slowly, mispricings survive longer, and arbitrage is more difficult (because of high trading costs and a lack of substitutes). An alternative view favors liquid markets, because in illiquid markets it takes a lifetime to distinguish a skillful alpha producer from a lucky one, or from an investor whose apparent alpha is really beta. In contrast, frequent performance measurement in liquid assets enables skill to be differentiated from luck over a reasonable evaluation period. Moreover, only liquid markets give investors the flexibility to terminate, at a moderate cost, a manager that has disappointed.

# 28.5 COSTS

Cost control is important because fees and costs feed directly to the bottom line of net returns. John Bogle likes to stress that cost differences across mutual funds can explain differences in net fund returns much better than any other feature can. (Among HFs, fee differences do not explain net return differences.) I review in Section 28.1 various ways to reduce costs but I also note that the virtue can be taken too far. The costs of systematic investment mistakes can be much higher than direct trading costs and fees; recall the examples of "dumb money" in the previous subsection (see Note 6).

For small investors, explicit costs, such as commissions and bid-ask spreads, tend to dominate. For large investors, implicit costs (market impact, front-running, etc.) matter more.

External manager contracts involve relatively straightforward fixed fees and performance fees but also less visible costs related to asymmetric incentives, high-water marks, lockups, and other compensation specifics with HFs, and an even greater variety of costs with PE funds.

Trading costs are naturally higher for less liquid assets and for strategies that involve greater turnover. Trading only the most liquid assets and using buy-and-hold strategies are two ways to keep costs down—but they may keep returns down as well.

Trading costs influence the relative attractiveness of active styles by causing the tradeoff between horizon and signal decay to differ between styles. Persistent (slow-moving) value and carry signals always require less turnover than fast-decaying momentum/reversal and underreaction/overreaction signals. The former can be traded patiently without much signal decay (long-horizon trading); the latter require speed (short-horizon trading). All else equal, rising trading costs make patient trading more optimal and should result in persistent signals having a larger weight. In reality, falling trading costs have made high-frequency trading more economical, but only for market participants facing the very lowest trading costs, since before-fee profits from high-frequency trading tend to be razor-thin.

Long-horizon investors often ignore information from high-frequency signals—such as reversal, momentum, or seasonal effects—due to the turnover required. New research by Israelov-Katz (2010) shows that even long-horizon investors can benefit from such signals by delaying or avoiding trading implementation when "fast" signals counsel patience.

Urgency of implementation and demand for liquidity are linked at the hip. Liquid assets satisfy urgency needs, illiquid ones do not. One way to reveal urgency and demand liquidity is to place a market order. Conversely, limit orders can be used to supply liquidity (although care is needed with limit orders; recent research shows that stale limit orders are among the key reasons for retail investors' "dumb" losses). Demanding liquidity is especially costly when market volatility is high; conversely, liquidity provision is better rewarded amidst turbulent conditions. Short-term reversal strategies are one way to supply liquidity to other market participants; such strategies exploit temporary price dislocations due to large traders' market impact. The performance of such reversal strategies comoves closely with the VIX equity volatility index, which is a decent proxy for the market's liquidity conditions.

Patient trading may also reduce market impact costs as long as other players (counterparties, high-frequency traders, and competitors) are not able to predict and front-run your flows. And it is not only your own trading that matters. You can save on market impact costs by avoiding trading that is synchronous with that of other large players: for example, you should not rebalance at month ends, or trade popular index products, when the crowd is likely to act. As already noted, even so-called passive management can suffer from hidden costs. Exact index matching can be detrimental due to price pressure effects when many index holders synchronously buy (sell) index additions (deletions). You can improve long-run returns if you customize benchmarks and execution strategies that do not mechanically trade together with the index crowd. And a final mishmash of advice:

- Do not naively think that high fees are justified. Invest in costly products with care and thought (e.g., pay high fees only for credibly expected alpha). Pay less for alternative beta and even less for traditional bulk beta. Pay lower fees if you have to agree to lockups and other liquidity-hampering restrictions that favor the manager.
- Think twice before paying up for bad economic reasons, such as avoiding mark to market or earning high upfront yield. Do not ignore taxes (as this book by and large does; see Box 28.4).
- New alternative trading platforms and electronic trading venues are worth exploring.

# Box 28.4. Taxes

Taxes are a form of costs or slippage. This book ignores taxes, even though taxable investors should care only or mostly about (real) after-tax expected returns. Tax effects can even influence assets' pre-tax expected returns. Here are some examples of tax effects on financial markets:

- Taxation causes many forms of market segmentation. Certain assets—municipal bonds, low-coupon bonds, and zero-dividend stocks—are especially attractive for investors with high tax rates, which makes these assets overvalued from the perspective of non-taxable investors.
- The income tax rate is often higher than the capital gains tax rate. Even at the same tax rate, capital gains are a more attractive source of revenue than income because they allow greater tax flexibility. Investors can defer realizing gains and paying capital gains taxes, while harvesting capital losses that may offset other taxable income. Active strategies that involve high turnover are less tax efficient than passive strategies that delay taxation.
- Changes in tax laws over time can influence absolute and relative pricing. Falling tax rates from the 1980s to the 2000s contributed to rising equity market valuations, while the dividend tax cut in 2003 boosted demand for high-dividend-yield stocks.
- Mortgage interest rate deductibility influences both bond and real estate pricing. The yield spreads of mortgages and corporates vs. Treasuries partly reflect differential (state and local) tax treatment. Tax-loss selling contributes to turn-of-the-year and January effects.

# 28.6 NOTES

[1] Clearly, the riskiness of individual investments is not well captured by standalone volatility, which ignores their diversification abilities and timing of losses. For a portfolio, however, volatility could be a sufficient risk statistic. In standard mean variance analysis, a portfolio is optimal if it has the highest Sharpe ratio of those from which it is possible to choose.

[2] The carry style proxy is a composite of yield-seeking strategies in currency and fixed income markets. The trend style proxy is a composite of trend-following strategies in dozens of liquid assets from four asset classes: bond or interest rate futures, currencies, equity country futures, and commodity futures. These style proxies are presented in Chapters 13 and 14.

[3] Estimated trading costs, but not fees, have been subtracted from returns on style proxies. Net-of-fee returns of the main CTA indices imply long-run Sharpe ratios of 0.2 to 0.5, clearly lower than our trend style proxy.

[4] Defenders of diversification rightly stress that even if in systemic crises diversification temporarily fails "just when it is most needed", it still works well in the long run and really serves to reduce a portfolio's long-run volatility, which should matter more for long-horizon investors.

[5] However, state-of-the-art literature on long-horizon investing—such as Campbell and Viceira's (2002) work—does not capture the intuition that long-horizon investors can, like Warren Buffett, ride through short-term market fluctuations better than myopic investors, and thus also serve as opportunistic value buyers. Instead, intertemporal hedging demands can cause long-horizon investors in these models to be even more sensitive to changing opportunities than myopic investors are. Perhaps the reason is that this literature relies on utility functions without time-varying risk aversion. In reality, the key distinction seems to be that most investors exhibit wealth-dependent risk aversion (which would discourage them from buying more risky assets in bad times when time-varying risk premia are elevated) whereas long-horizon investors may have more stable risk preferences over time (and would derive utility gains from buying low-priced risky assets in bad times).

[6] A parallel finding is that investors in mutual funds and HFs hurt their long-run performance by poor timing and/or allocation decisions, likely by performance chasing at horizons where the reversal effect has overtaken momentum (buying multi-year winners and selling multi-year losers). As a result, dollar-weighted returns (which reflect inflows and outflows and better capture actual returns that fund investors have earned) are much lower than the same funds' time-weighted or buy-and-hold returns (which reflect average manager performance). Some estimate the gap at  $1.5\%$  for investors in mutual funds and at  $3\%$  to  $7\%$  (see Section 11.4.2) for investors in HFs. Such evidence (plus the stale limit order effect, overtrading, etc.) are used as justifications for arguing that smart investors have a great deal of opportunity to extract alpha from "dumb money" investors even given the zero-sum nature of overall active management.

Source notes. On risk reduction and return enhancement by levering up low-volatility asset classes, see Dalio (2005), Asness-Kabiller-Mendelson (2010), Frazzini-Pedersen (2010), and Leibowitz-Bova (2010a).
