On arithmetic and geometric mean returns, see Jacquier-Kane-Marcus (2003), Stutzer-Yung-Hughson (2006), and Hudson (2009). On the multi-period strategy of maximizing expected compound returns, see Wilcox (1999) and Wilcox-Fabozzi (2009). On smart portfolio construction and investment management, see Grinold-Kahn (1999), Litterman (2003), Qian-Hua-Sorensen (2007), Fabozzi-Focardi-Kolm (2010), Ghayur et al. (2010), Scherer (2010), and Schneeweis-Crowder-Kazemi (2010).

On exploiting long investment horizons (and a great survey on active management), see Ang-Goetzmann-Schaefer (2009). On time diversification, see Bodie-Merton-Samuelson (1992), Thorley (1995), Kritzman (2000), and Pastor-Stambaugh (2009). On lifecycle investing, see Campbell-Viceira (2002), Ibbotson et al. (2007), and Bodie-Siegel-Sullivan (2009).

On the costs and benefits of active investing, see Wermers (2000), Berk-Green (2004), Litterman (2004), Fama-French (2008), French (2008), Ang-Goetzmann-Schaefer (2009), and Swensen (2009). On the costs of index investing, see Petajistö (2010). On pockets of evidence for active skill, see Cremers-Petajistö (2009), Sun-Wang-Zheng (2009), Cohen-Polk-Silli (2010), and Amihud-Goyenko (2010). On evidence of poor decisions on fund timing/allocation, see Braverman-Kandel-Wohl (2007), Dichev (2007), Friesen-Sapp (2007), Frazzini-Lamont (2008), Goyal-Wahat (2008), Stewart et al. (2009), and Dichev-Yu (2010). On the impact of trading costs, see Wilcox (1999), Garleanu-Pedersen (2009b), and Serbin-Bull-Zhu (2009). On long-horizon investors exploiting fast signals in their trade-timing, see Israelov-Katz (2010).

# 29

# Takeaways for long-horizon investors

- There are many ways to improve investment practices to enhance long-run returns. Several can be pursued in parallel: collect risk premia from diverse sources; benefit (in moderation) from illiquidity premia, leverage, contrarian timing, and view-driven active management; be aware of risks and costs as well as own natural edges and limitations.
- Equity and illiquidity premia are good return sources but should not dominate the portfolio, especially when their ex ante rewards are slim. Entry and exit valuations matter.
- Returns may be enhanced by exploiting value, carry, and momentum tilts; valuation-based timing of any return source; and view-based alpha seeking.
- Prudent leverage enables effective diversification and helps investors avoid overpriced high-volatility assets.
- Do not seek comfort by avoiding leverage or headline risk while buying popular assets or lottery tickets. These errors hurt long-run performance.
- Long-horizon investors have certain comparative advantages that they should fully exploit.

In this "takeaways" chapter, I summarize theories about expected return determination, list which plausible sources of expected returns have and have not worked in practice, and address several relevant debates among investors. Institutional practices have already evolved from 60/40 local market stock/bond portfolios to globally diversified portfolios, often including emerging markets and alternative assets. The "Yale" or endowment model became increasingly popular in the 2000s. This model seeks high returns by holding equity-like asset classes, harvesting illiquidity premia from alternatives, and avoiding market timing. Large endowment losses in 2008 raised many questions about the wisdom of this strategy, however. Maybe market timing and drawdown control are not such bad ideas, after all, and maybe illiquidity is not such a great return source? Should equity orientation be replaced by more balanced portfolios, even if earning an acceptable return from a more balanced portfolio requires leverage? If valuations matter, shouldn't value tilts be cemented into asset allocation and security selection processes? Could it be more effective to diversify across underlying risk factors than across asset classes? Here, I review all the ideas in which I see merit, but I also note their shortcomings.

The next generation of best practice for enhancing returns involves pursuing several paths in parallel, instead of embracing one idea. It is up to every institution to decide, based on its goals, constraints, resources, and inclinations, what its priorities are. Diversity in choices is a good outcome for markets where investment approaches that are too homogeneous can lead to systemic problems. The same diversity also helps investors avoid overcrowded positions.

# 29.1 KEY TAKEAWAYS FROM THEORY

Not all volatility is equal. Investment returns reflect multiple factors, both rational risk premia and investor irrationality. Different return sources deserve different long-run rewards. An investment's typical timing of losses matters more than its standalone risk. Investors assessing risk and returns should therefore look beyond the Sharpe ratio and penalize investments that perform poorly in bad times. The key insight in modern finance theory is that such investments warrant high required risk premia, while valuable safe haven assets can justify negative risk premia.

The ex ante reward for each factor can vary over time and is often higher after poor medium-term performance. If return predictability reflects time-varying risk premia, exceptionally good market opportunities tend to materialize just when investor risk aversion and liquidity needs are high. Thus, contrarian market timing may be of greatest benefit to truly long-horizon investors whose risk tolerance is more stable and liquidity needs less than those of the average investor.

# 29.2 EMPIRICAL RETURN SOURCES

Here is my take on 10 potentially important sources of investment returns. Do you get rewarded with higher long-run returns for …?

1. High volatility and high beta? Not within asset classes. The most volatile assets tend to be overpriced due to skewness preferences or leverage constraints ("overpaying for lottery characteristics or getting bang for the buck"). Across asset classes, the riskier ones tend to outperform the safe ones in the long run.
2. Illiquidity? Yes. Less liquid assets do compensate for their illiquidity and tendency to lose money amidst liquidity droughts. This compensation, like others, is not constant over time.
3. Fast growth? Not unless you can predict future growth better than the market can. One of the most common errors of investors is over-extrapolating the past growth rate of a firm, sector, or country ("overpaying for the hope of growth"), leading to subsequent disappointments.
4. Cheap assets? Yes. The flipside of the above growth story is that value stocks (which sell at a price that is cheap relative to some fundamental metric) tend to outperform in the long run. The reason for low valuation ratios among stocks and other assets may be irrationality or rational compensation for risk. Both give rise to higher future returns.
5. Positive carry? Yes. Long-run reward has been especially good in cross-country trades and less compelling in within-country trades. Poor timing of large losses makes the strategy risky, but this riskiness should sustain long-run reward. Exception: When carry seeking is implemented through complex structured products, fees and risks often overwhelm any yield advantage.
6. Recent distress? Not in equity markets where low-rated and distressed stocks often keep underperforming for several months. (Over longer horizons, however, surviving stocks rebound smartly and contribute to the value effect.) Among corporate bonds, "fallen angels" recently downgraded from investment-grade rating offer high subsequent returns following forced selling by institutional investors.
7. Recent success? Yes. A positive trend, or momentum, does tend to persist in most asset classes, but the historical window matters. The best information regarding momentum is often in 6-to-12-month histories. A common error involves chasing returns over a multi-year horizon when reversal is more likely than continuation of the existing trend.
8. Leverage? Yes. Higher leverage boosts expected returns but also risks, sometimes substantially. Enhanced risk controls and differentiation between more and less risky leverage are important.
9. Diversification? Yes, if you are able and willing to use leverage to monetize the risk reduction benefits of diversification. Combining negatively correlated return sources is especially valuable and is often best done via alternative assets. Even long-horizon investors should not waste the complementary nature of value and momentum or carry and trend exposures.
10. Timing? Yes. The rewards for most of the above items vary over time in a partly predictable way. For example, fast growth and high liquidity are appealing features, but only at the right price. Yet, market timing, factor timing, and style timing are not easy and should be done in moderation.

One of my key recommendations is harvesting excess returns from a broad range of sources, not only relying on the equity premium. Admittedly, there is no consensus as to which other premia give a sustainable edge. The equity premium has the most compelling theoretical underpinning and it is larger (on an unlevered basis) than most other premia. No source of excess return can guarantee profits each year, but the diverse sources I highlight—illiquidity, value, carry, momentum—have given investors a solid long-run edge and deserve the benefit of the doubt. Beating the market is hard enough that it would seem wasteful not to use the tailwinds that these return sources give to long-run performance.

# Box 29.1. How going for comfort reduces long-run returns

Conventional or comfortable approaches to boosting expected returns tend to become overpriced and offer poor reward for risk, maybe even detracting from expected return. Common bad habits include

- to avoid (explicit) leverage, weighting asset classes or strategies with embedded or non-recourse leverage;
- to avoid leverage, weighting the highest volatility assets within an asset class;
- to avoid leverage, staying with equity-centric portfolios instead of monetizing gains from diversification into fixed income and other asset classes;
- to earn a possible illiquidity premium and avoid mark-to-market volatility, hoarding illiquid asset classes irrespective of valuations; to boost portfolio yield, buying corporate bonds and structures with embedded options.

Other conventional or comfortable choices that give rise to poor expected returns include return chasing and buying popular assets (hence the existence of profits for contrarian or value strategies), avoiding well-rewarded pockets in credit markets (BB-rated fallen angels and levered short-dated top-rated debt), and avoiding investments with headline risk (e.g., providing "insurance" after large losses when capital is scarce).

# 29.3 MY TAKE ON KEY DEBATES

# 29.3.1 Passive betas vs. active alpha seeking

One camp argues that all investing should be done passively by holding cheap betas—perhaps allocating  $60\%$  to stock indices and  $40\%$  to bond indices, with market cap weights within the asset classes and no market timing. Another extreme advocates only active investing, targeting absolute returns, with virtually no investment constraints nor benchmarks. There is a whole spectrum of views between these extremes.

Beliefs are crucial: the less investors believe in market efficiency and the more faith they have in their own ability to beat the markets or to select winning managers, the more they will favor active management. Ability should be judged objectively (to resist overconfidence); and resources and governance matter. A large, resource-rich institution may have a better chance of being among those rare consistent outperformers (although small, nimble funds may have an edge in activities that cannot be easily scaled up). Casual attempts to beat the market are likely to fail because active management is a zero-sum game even before costs and fees, and only players with well-above-average skill are likely to win. If an institution knows that it cannot be nimble and has historically acted procyclically, it should outsource market-timing efforts or else give them up. If it cannot incentivize its in-house managers well but believes that it is able to pick superior external managers, it should outsource all alpha efforts.

All else equal, lower fees and trading costs should encourage more active management. The choice between external and internal management also depends on relative costs, among other considerations.

The decision of where an institution should harvest risk premia depends on its assessment of where risks are rewarded. This book should help investors make better judgments when they allocate capital dynamically across various traditional and alternative beta sources. Flexible investors can cast the net out wide across multiple factors. Investors looking for high raw returns, if they are unable or unwilling to use leverage, must focus on inherently volatile asset classes such as equities.

The decision of where an investor should try to hunt for alpha depends on its views on relative market efficiency in different market segments—as well as on the manager's own relative advantages, skills, and costs. Fund size is one source of relative strength or weakness. Very large AUM may give an investor special opportunities to make capital infusions at preferential terms. However, large AUM also implies capacity constraints in smaller markets. Contrarian market timing may be less capacity-constrained than security selection.

A common view states that inefficiencies and alpha are most readily available in less liquid markets—small-cap stocks, frontier countries, unlisted assets—on which fewer investors and analysts shine light. One counterargument notes that non-economic actors and behavioral biases create macro-inefficiencies in liquid markets and that low transaction costs make it more realistic to exploit such opportunities. Moreover, in liquid markets it is more feasible to distinguish alpha gains from beta gains and to exit from disappointing investments. Net of all these considerations, I suspect that asset selection, relative value opportunities, and micro-inefficiencies are more lucrative in less liquid markets, while the reverse may be true for timing and macroinefficiencies.

Alpha-beta barbells make sense. Most of the risk budget should be accessed cheaply (bulk betas, alternative betas, in-house timing). Being simple and cheap with at least the core portfolio prevents active fee paying with all the AUM. The more costly active risk budget should be the smaller part, in which investors ideally only pay alpha fees for true alpha efforts—judged as much by ex ante qualities as by ex post performance. Alternative betas and other systematic approaches deserve fees between those that apply to bulk beta and to alpha, and may be brought in house in large organizations that have ample resources.

# 29.3.2 Lottery/Insurance selling or buying?

Lottery or insurance selling in financial markets is a blatantly risky strategy, but at least it tends to make money over long periods. It sure beats lottery buying as a long-run strategy. If investors want to counterbalance their exposures to pro-risk and short-volatility strategies, trend and momentum styles offer surprisingly appealing long-run returns given that they also have favorable risk-diversifying characteristics. In contrast, lottery-type small growth stocks and high-volatility segments in each asset class offer surprisingly poor long-run returns and unexceptional diversifying characteristics. As a rule, investors should stay away from lottery assets, except as needed to implement tactical views. Lottery buyers—or tail risk hedgers—need to pick their battles, either through smart timing or trade selection.

# 29.3.3 Leverage: Not all bad

Leverage has a bad name, and especially so after 2008. Real-money investors have preferred non-recourse leverage to explicit leverage, and such preferences may now be even more pronounced than in the past. It is true that leverage can increase blowup risk for a given investor and it can also amplify systemic risks overall; moreover, the need for forced de-levering is greater during financial crises and liquidity droughts if one has explicit leverage. But it is also true that prudent leverage can boost long-run returns by enabling effective diversification and avoiding overpriced high-volatility assets.

My most controversial recommendation is for institutions to accept some leverage as one way of enhancing returns, as long as it is not precluded by regulations. Differentiating between more and less risky leverage, rather than by explicit leverage and embedded non-recourse leverage, may be useful. Risky leverage includes boosting leverage to offset falling risk premia or falling volatility (as in 2006-2007), or levering up illiquid or inherently risky assets, or leveraging via borrowing in ways that are more likely to need to be unwound during financial crises or amidst capital scarcity. Less risky leverage includes levering up low-volatility assets to improve diversification, especially when done using liquid assets and vehicles that are less likely to cause forced liquidations or to involve counterparty risk (e.g., exchange-traded short-dated options and futures). Even the less risky approaches to leverage raise the standards needed in risk management and governance. Like derivatives, leverage can be abused.

Even without the benefit of avoiding overpriced assets, leverage can enhance long-run returns by monetizing the diversification benefits of better balanced, less equity-centric portfolios. Low-volatility asset classes tend to improve diversification and risk-adjusted returns. These improvements can be converted into higher expected returns by applying moderate leverage. Yet many real-money investors reject the use of leverage and instead opt for equity-centric risk budgets and/or (overpriced) high-volatility assets or investments offering non-recourse leverage.

Leverage aversion and leverage constraints made low-volatility assets relatively attractive on a risk-adjusted basis even in recent decades, when use of leverage was more widespread. In the coming years, with many investors newly shy of using leverage, the benefits of moderate amounts of explicit leverage seem likely to increase further.

# 29.3.4 Liquidity—overvalued or undervalued?

The standard argument in the endowment model is that illiquid assets offer a premium both for illiquidity and for the fact that systematic liquidity risk materializes mostly in bad times. By holding liquid assets investors forfeit these premia, thereby reducing long-run average returns. For long-horizon investors who do not need short-term liquidity, harvesting illiquidity premia seems to be the most obvious natural edge. Long-horizon investors' demand for illiquid asset classes arguably went too far in the mid-2000s. Illiquidity is not worth bearing if it is not properly rewarded. Although ex ante illiquidity premia are not directly observable, it is evident that institutional interest drove these premia to levels that were too thin.

Critics of the endowment model argue that real-money investors undervalue liquidity and ignore(d) the time-varying nature of liquidity premia. Liquidity gives you several benefits. Effectively, an investor in liquid assets is long an option that gives him or her flexibility to sell and to adjust the portfolio at will. Liquid holdings ensure that you must never rely on forced selling in bad times and instead can take advantage of exceptional opportunities in those times. To get this reward, you must pay a (tail) insurance premium in good times by holding cash and other low-earning liquid and safe haven assets (keeping your powder dry), or by explicitly purchasing insurance in the options market.

I stress that, even for long-horizon investors investing in illiquid assets, starting valuations can matter greatly. A contrarian timing approach may even boost long-run returns, as investors provide liquidity opportunistically only when it is well rewarded—not at all times. At least it should help reduce drawdowns in those bad times when the value of one extra dollar is especially high, when even long-horizon investors see their investment horizons shrink, and when even capital-rich investors feel the scarcity of capital.

# 29.3.5 To time or not to time?

Until recently, the consensus view among academics and institutional investors was negative on market timing. Successful timing is hard, the risk is undiversified, and staying in cash involves forfeiting a positive long-run premium. Being "correct but early" in calling a market turn exposes investors to serious career risk. The experience of the two boom-bust cycles in the 2000s has changed many minds, however.

Not only equity market risk but also most return sources have time-varying ex ante rewards. Static allocations to rewarded factors give useful average tilts but it is wasteful to keep the same exposures when estimated ex ante rewards are exceptionally small or large (as in credit investments in 2006 and 2008, respectively).

Contrarian timing signals are a natural starting point. Simple valuation signals possess some ability to predict medium-term returns. Other indicators can complement these value signals. Most return sources exhibit short-term momentum and long-term reversal tendencies, in part reflecting their fluctuating popularity. (I do not quantify here the gains from timing because overfitting and uncertain trading costs make any estimates highly uncertain.)

Even discretionary market-timers use systematic signals as one input to their decision making, but often their market views are driven more by their macroeconomic forecasts or perceived structural changes. Investors with ample resources should study valuations and market conditions, and then consider dynamic adjustments on either a discretionary or a systematic basis. If they lack expertise or nimbleness in house, they may also outsource the task to experts—say, by allocating mandates to global macro-funds.

Thus, I endorse moderate market timing, factor timing, and style timing. However, timing is not easy, so humility is warranted. Many organizations may not tolerate sustained underperformance—say, when a manager sells an overpriced market too early. Also, in active investing, the first rule should be "Do no harm". Some thoughtful institutions may find that in their organization—given an unclear mandate or other governance problems—any timing activity attempted would be likely to turn out to be of the common procyclical and return-chasing variety. In that case, they are better off resisting the siren songs of past performance by "tying themselves to the mast" and sticking to constant weight asset class allocations. At least over time they would gain discipline and modest profits from rebalancing.

# 29.3.6 Effective diversification

A key challenge to achieving diversification is reducing the dominance of equity risk. More balanced portfolios diversify better—notwithstanding the 2008 experience. However, balanced risk budgets often require leverage to achieve high returns, while unlevered diversification "only" achieves high risk-adjusted returns (i.e., low returns but with even lower volatility). If correlations spike, the well-diversified but levered portfolio may underperform an equity-centric unlevered portfolio. Even if diversification tends to fail in systemic crises, it can still be useful in the long run—which matters more for long-horizon investors who face less liquidation pressure during crises.

Most investments have positive exposures to the equity market and to economic growth. This directional risk is difficult to diversify away, making the elusive negative beta a valuable characteristic. The challenge is to find safe haven investments that are not too costly and work reliably. Of course, effective growth risk diversifiers are either very expensive (buying the VIX) or unstable (gold and oil worked well 30 years ago, Treasuries and the yen in 2008). It is not clear what will work in the 2010s; I stress the possibility that government bonds will lose their safe haven status.

Diversification should not ignore valuations. Especially if many asset classes are overpriced in good times, it seems likely that they may suffer together when bad times hit. The best results arise by combining the general goal of broad diversification with the underweighting of expensive and overcrowded asset classes or strategies.

Diversification across strategy styles or risk factors is often more effective than diversification across asset classes (recall the three-dimensional cube in Figure 1.2). Enabling purer style and factor exposures is therefore a key underappreciated benefit of investing in alternative assets (albeit not exclusive to them).

Style diversification. Combining value and momentum tilts (or related carry and trend tilts), which often are negatively correlated, provides more effective diversification than most static asset classes. A value or contrarian approach is a more natural style for long-horizon investors, but even they should add a dose of momentum or trend tilts that are complementary to their core (pro-risk, pro-reversal) positions. Negative correlation is simply too valuable a trait to ignore. At least momentum implies that long-horizon investors can be patient when apparent value opportunities arise, delaying part of their intended trading until the short-term momentum signal agrees with the long-run value signal. They can leg into contrarian positions gradually, keeping some ammunition until the market has turned.

However, even diversifying across negatively related styles can fail. For example, both value and momentum had a bad decade in the 1930s and both suffered sharp losses in August 2007. The latter episode reminds us of overcrowding risk, which can make any strategy hurt—typically, following persistent successes and excesses.

# Box 29.2. Style diversification vs. asset class derivatives

As a simple exercise, I study combinations of four asset classes (U.S. stocks, bonds, real estate, and funds of hedge funds) and the four strategy styles reviewed in Chapters 12-15 (equity value, currency carry, commodity trends, and volatility selling). Each composite is rebalanced to equal weights every month between 1990 and 2009. The asset class composite has greater market-directional risk: its correlation with global equities is 0.80, compared with 0.27 for the strategy style composite. The asset class composite also has average correlation among its four components (0.30) than does the strategy style composite  $(-0.05)$ . Thanks to its superior diversification, the strategy style composite has a double Sharpe ratio compared with its constituents' average Sharpe ratio (1.2 vs. 0.6). This simply reflects the fact that with four uncorrelated strategies, portfolio volatility is about half the constituents' average volatility, consistent with the fundamental law of active management (see Section 28.2.4). In contrast, the asset class composite sees a much more modest volatility reduction and Sharpe ratio increase.

This example focuses on diversification and deliberately steers away from average returns (which happen to be higher for the strategy style composite). Performance differences could reflect period-specific tailwinds or headwinds as well as differing volatilities. More importantly, the paper performance of strategy styles is more prone to being overstated (due to overfitting or selection biases as well as ignored or understated trading costs) than the paper performance of asset classes.

Risk factor diversification. The standard approach to asset allocation, which is to determine the nominal weights of asset classes, is gradually giving way to risk budgeting, or allocating risk weights (contributions to portfolio volatility). A more dramatic change will be to shift from allocating nominal or risk weights across assets to allocating risk across underlying risk factors.

The expected return and risk of a large portfolio is dominated by some key risk factors. Even if the investment approach involves fundamental bottom-up management (e.g., stock picking), or absolute return strategies (e.g., hedge funds), total risk is often driven by top-down factor exposures. Thus it is important to understand these exposures and to know whether they are well rewarded and well diversified. I have argued that underlying risk factors really determine the risk-reward tradeoff. The risk factors are then mapped into asset and/or strategy weights. For example, both a corporate bond and a small-cap stock reflect some exposures to growth, inflation, illiquidity, and volatility—as well as to the premia associated with these risks and characteristics.

I select these four factors, but stress that there is little consensus on the number and identity of factors. Open questions also remain on mapping between assets and risk factors (how one can best estimate the risk factor exposures of a given asset, how stable the relations are, etc.). The factor allocation approach has several advantages: it enables one to focus on the primary return drivers, more effective diversification, and more effective extraction of risk premia. Yet, it is less intuitive and less familiar to most investors than traditional asset class allocation, raising meaningful communication challenges. Thus, uptake of the risk factor approach may be slow and may begin with only the most sophisticated investors.

# 29.3.7 Diversification vs. drawdown control

Even though the endowment model made alternative assets more popular among institutional investors, some absolute return managers have been critical of the model. One strand of criticism against the endowment model is that it focuses excessively on equity-like asset classes and relies too heavily on an illiquidity premium as a source of returns. Another strand criticizes the model for having diversification as the only risk management tool and for avoiding market timing. All these features contributed to sharp drawdowns in endowment portfolios in 2008—though most absolute return managers lost money as well.

More generally, absolute return managers argue that benchmark-oriented managers pay too little heed to drawdown control. While benchmarks serve useful roles for ex ante guidance (the neutral "viewless" portfolio) and are the basis of ex post performance evaluation, they make it too easy to accept large absolute losses that hamper long-run return compounding. In contrast, absolute return investing centers on taking active market views while managing risk to preserve capital. The absolute return approach may be especially attractive when the world is in flux and facing structural changes, with macro-uncertainty and regulatory upheavals.

Admittedly, controlling drawdowns by stop-loss rules or by lower risk profiles comes at the expense of lower expected returns—unless the manager exhibits skill in market timing or in creating cheap tail risk insurance.

I find risk consciousness as important for active investors as return seeking and cost consciousness. Drawdown control may be done proactively or imposed from the outside (through regulatory calls or margin calls); the former seems preferable. Institutions that reduced risks before the fall of 2008 looked smart while those that did the same a few months later, near the market bottom, now look foolish with hindsight. Yet, I would not draw too broad a lesson from this experience because there was a real chance of further market declines, which might have forced many holdouts to capitulate later at even worse levels.

# 29.4 KNOW THYSELF: LARGE LONG-HORIZON INVESTORS' NATURAL EDGES

Investors should take into account their relative strengths and weaknesses. Here are some specific suggestions for exploiting the long investment horizon:

- harvesting illiquidity premia (e.g., infrastructure, non-city land);
- opportunistic insurance selling (against financial and natural catastrophes);
- contrarian market timing and asset allocation;
- contracts designed to mitigate agency problems—among the desired outcomes are making sure a delegated manager shares your long horizon and making sure you get compensated for long lockups.

The above apply for pure asset portfolios. Liabilities have obvious horizon implications: duration or cash flow matching between assets and liabilities.

Very large investors cannot be too active given their big footprint in the market, nor can they make much difference to their bottom line by investing in small, novel asset classes. For such investors, it is essential to get the major asset class decisions right and to implement core beta portfolios in a cost-effective manner:

- Being cost-effective does not necessarily mean market cap weighting if non-cap weights are deemed to give a return advantage sufficient to offset higher trading costs. But the larger weight an investor has in the marketplace, the larger the market impact cost its activity generates. It may be harder for large funds to deviate from market cap weights.
- A clearly contrarian active timing or allocation strategy may be possible, though, as there will be many investors on the other side. Large AUM funds with a long horizon thus have a natural edge in being opportunistic liquidity providers when capital is scarce.
- Despite capacity constraints, the largest and most resource-rich institutions should diversify very broadly across asset classes and strategies. This includes developing in-house competence in smaller market segments that may grow, to give the investor the edge that pioneers can have in growing markets.
- Large funds may have greater potential to be winners in the active management game. Successful in-house management is feasible but requires proper resources, governance, and incentivization.
- A smaller capital base has its benefits, and not just because it enables exploiting opportunities in smaller markets without capacity problems. Moreover, smaller funds can more easily lever up their capital base and benefit from superior opportunities in low-volatility assets, whereas super-sized funds may have to restrict themselves to seeking excess returns from markets that are inherently volatile.
- For the largest investors, it is especially important to avoid activities that are synchronous with those of other large players. One way this can be accomplished is to use customized benchmarks. It is also important for these investors to prevent their large flows from being gamed by counterparties or by other market participants.
- A prerequisite for successful investment management in any organization is getting the investment culture and governance structures right. Without these, investing may yield substandard results as well as organizational instability.

# 29.5 INSTITUTIONAL PRACTICES

As is well known, a typical U.S. pension fund assumes an  $8\%$  long-run return for its portfolio. If bond yields are near  $5\%$ , and the portfolio is split 60/40 between stocks and bonds, the implicit expected return on equities is  $10\%$ . Many critics (rightly) point out that this long-run expectation is unrealistic and we have not seen  $5\%$  yields in a while. Cynics argue that expected returns are deliberately overstated because higher expected return assumptions reduce the apparent present value of pension obligations (using the dubious, but often legally mandated or at least accepted, practice of using expected asset return as the discount rate for an associated liability). Aggressive return assumptions improve pension plans' funding ratio and can help pension-sponsoring firms publish higher top-line earnings and justify lower pension contributions.

Such pension accounting shenanigans were already under discussion a decade ago when typical return assumptions were even more aggressive (near  $10\%$ ). Practices have thus improved, albeit slowly. Private pension plans are hardly alone in engaging in such mischief. Overoptimistic assumptions have long enabled public pension plans to avoid facing their dire fiscal conditions [1].

Any fixed return targets for long-term returns can be criticized for ignoring some of the most basic market realities, such as time variation in inflation and ex ante real yields. For example, if investment portfolios over the past 30 or 40 years have achieved an  $8\%$  average nominal return amidst a  $5\%$  to  $6\%$  average realized inflation rate, it hardly follows that we can extrapolate nominal returns further, given current expected long-run inflation in the  $2\%$  to  $3\%$  range. Nominal targets are especially naive because of the obviously time-varying nature of inflation, but even real return targets should be reviewed occasionally because real rates and risk premia also change over time. In any case, the problems caused by missed real return targets are trivial compared with those caused by nominal guarantees. More generally, a sense that "we need  $X\%$  returns" irrespective of market reality typically leads to excessive and ill-informed risk taking.

Institutional investors cannot analyze their assets' expected returns and risks in isolation. In reality, their decision making must take into account their liabilities and regulatory constraints, as well as more nebulous issues: peer comparisons and competitive pressures, prospects for bailouts and regulatory forbearance, own governance structure, and non-financial considerations (such as social responsibility and sustainability, or a country's desire to hedge its core revenue source or to ensure access to strategically important resources). These topics require a book of their own and are best left for future research, even if they can influence investor behavior and thereby expected asset returns.

# 29.6 NOTES

[1] Separately, current U.S. law can be criticized on many grounds. The discount rates for pension liabilities should not vary across institutions. In fact, if pension liabilities are iron-clad, they should be discounted using the Treasury rate (assuming we still assign riskless status to Treasuries despite the fiscal troubles). A lower discount rate would push many overfunded pension plans to underfunded status—arguably making them face the reality and, more generally, forcing society to deal with the pension time bomb.

Source notes. On investment lessons, see Swensen (2009), Ang-Goetzmann-Schaefer (2009), Asness-Kabiller-Mendelson (2010), Drobny (2010), and Leibowitz-Bova-Hammond (2010).

# Appendix A

# World wealth

This appendix reviews evidence on the relative sizes of various parts of world wealth. A market's size may indirectly influence its expected return; smaller markets can be less liquid and create capacity concerns for large investors, thus implying higher required returns. Any assessment of global wealth is plagued by problems of definitions (which wealth categories to include) and measurement (how to quantify values, how to treat leverage). Let's start with a simple story from major indices. At the end of 2009, the size of global equity markets was  \$45 trillion and the size of global bond markets was\$ 40 trillion. But what about non-public equity and debt, cash and money market instruments, real estate and infrastructure, durable goods and other personal holdings, commodities and other resources, art and other collectibles, convertibles and other hybrids, derivatives and structured products? Most importantly, what about human capital? The challenges are immense, as already highlighted in Ibbotson-Siegel-Love (1985), though more manageable than when assessing the amount of capital allocated to strategy styles:

- Investability is an important qualification. (i) Many important parts of wealth are not included in this analysis because they are not investable (or are illiquid and poorly measured). Some estimates suggest that natural resources (e.g., oil underground), privately held firms, and owner-occupied housing can each be worth tens of trillions of dollars, and thus comparable in size with public equity or debt markets. (ii) Securitization has in past decades made many previously illiquid assets or income streams investable and liquid. Yet, for many novel types of securitized debt this improvement proved ephemeral, as "good-times liquidity" vanished in 2007-2008.
- What counts as net wealth? Among publicly traded assets, only equity is unambiguously net wealth. Derivatives and debt are arguably not net wealth but side bets that cancel out. Bonds and other debt contracts are one party's asset and another party's liability. According to Ricardian equivalence, even government bonds are net wealth only if their value exceeds the present value of future tax liabilities. Larger public debts do not make citizens wealthier as prospective taxes rise. Along a similar line of thought, rising house prices may not raise a society's net wealth because the houseowners' gain is offset by the higher future cost of non-owners' housing services.
- Performance vs. net issuance. I will not try to track world wealth over time, but I remind the reader that any time series of market capitalization evolves over time both due to asset returns and to net issuance. For example, the fast growth of securitized assets or the Chinese stock market capitalization in the past 20 years reflects more new issuance than high returns. For equity investors it is important to understand that existing investors of listed shares only benefit from growth in current firms; historically, this includes less than half of aggregate real earnings growth (see Sections 8.4.3 and 16.4.1).
- Some wealth classifications add up management vehicles instead of assets: pension assets, insurance assets, mutual fund assets, private equity, and hedge funds. How do SWFs (sovereign wealth funds) and HNWIs (high-net-worth individuals) fit these classifications? And should we treat PE, HF, and structured products as wealth classes or management vehicles; the former choice may imply double-counting?

Without being pedantic about definitions, I quote here some interesting classifications and quantifications.

# A.1 GLOBAL TOTAL

- McKinsey Global Institute (2009) reports the size of global financial assets at 178 trillion (194 trillion) at end-2008 (end-2007). The main three parts are equities \$34 trillion (62 trillion), debt \$83 trillion (77 trillion)—of which 51 trillion is private debt and \$32 trillion is government debt—and bank deposits \$61 trillion (56 trillion). The total had grown from \$48 trillion in 1990 to \$112 trillion in 2000 and \$155 trillion in 2005. Separately, McKinsey estimates global residential real estate market size to be \$87 trillion at end-2008.
- For comparison, International Monetary Fund (2009) estimates end-2008 financial assets at  \$214 trillion. This total comprised equities\$ 34 trillion, debt \$84 trillion (private/public \$52 trillion/\$32 trillion), and bank assets \$98 trillion.
- Some assessments focus on the asset management vehicle rather than final investments. Boston Consulting Group (2010) estimates global (long-only) assets under professional management in 2009 at  \$53 trillion, of which\$ 32 trillion were institutional and \$21 trillion retail assets [1]. Alternative managers held much smaller AUM—HFs near \$2 trillion and PE funds a little less. SWFs held comparable amounts, though the size would multiply if central bank reserves were included.
- Yet other comparisons quantify the ultimate owner. Capgemini (2010) estimates the wealth of HNWIs at  \$39 trillion in 2009, after\$ 41 trillion in 2007 and 33 trillion in 2008. HNWI assets were split into 29\% equities, 31\% FI, 17\% cash/deposits, 18\% real estate, and 6\% alternatives. (Privately held enterprises and collectibles are excluded.)
- Many estimates involve double-counting (e.g., estimates of real estate, infrastructure, or resources involve listed equities, while HFs are net long equities). Most major equity indices nowadays adjust for cross-holdings.
- Such estimates typically exclude derivative instruments. Derivatives market notionals can be huge but misleading. Real risk exposures and cash outlays are orders of magnitudes smaller, especially after netting. Of the  \$532 trillion notional amount of financial instruments covered by OTC derivatives in June 2008, potential exposures to counterparties are estimated to be only\$ 2.7 trillion. These exposures are further reduced by collateral held against counterparty risk. Derivatives are best thought of as side bets.

# A.2 ASSET CLASS DETAIL

- Publicly listed equities are arguably the most important part of world wealth, least debatable, and most easily measured. (Even here questions exist about cross-holdings, free float, and investability.) The global market cap rose steadily from  \$30 trillion at end-2003, crossing\$ 50 trillion at end-2006, and peaking at \$62 trillion in October 2007. The market cap then halved over the next year, troughed at \$26 trillion in March 2009, before recovering to \$45 trillion by the year end. (For comparison, global GDP was \$50 trillion to 60 trillion, but this comparison mixes stock and flow variables.) The U.S. had about 30\% of world market cap, followed by 6\% to 8\% in each of Japan, China, and the U.K.
- The share of emerging markets in global market capitalization had risen from  $2\%$  in 1980 to  $12\%$  by end-2009. This share excluded many emerging market stocks that remain inaccessible to global investors and remains well below the  $30\%$  share of emerging countries in global GDP.
- Privately held enterprises may, on aggregate, be as valuable as publicly traded stocks. Vissing-Jorgensen-Moskowitz (2002) estimate that in the 1990s nonpublicly held equity by U.S. entrepreneurs was similar in size to the public equity market. Asian family enterprises, German "Mittelstand", and sovereign assets are global examples of wealth not captured in listed equities, but I have not seen good estimates. The values could be very high, but in any case these are not investable.
- Combining major fixed income market indices, the global bond markets amounted to  \$40.3 trillion in December 2009. Over half was government or government-related debt; this share will likely increase in coming years. The market value of all investment-grade bonds in the BarCap Global Aggregate index was\$ 35.2 trillion: \$17.4 trillion government, \$5.3 trillion government related, \$5.8 trillion corporates, \$6.7 trillion securitized (of which \$5 trillion U.S. MBS). To this I add smaller BarCap indices: global inflation-linked debt (mainly government) \$1.5 trillion, global high yield (mainly corporates) \$1.1 trillion, and global emerging market debt \$2.5 trillion (hard currency debt \$0.5 trillion and emerging local currency debt \$2.0 trillion—much of which may not be investable for foreigners).
- Other estimates of fixed income markets were twice as high,  \$80 trillion to 90 trillion. The above BarCap indices do not include money markets (debt with maximum maturity of 12 months), floating rate debt, municipal bonds, or convertible bonds (\$ 2 trillion and 0.2 trillion markets just in the U.S.), let alone the still huge segment of unsecuritized bank loans.
- Real estate markets are huge but only a small segment is investable. The most liquid segment—listed REITs and global property stocks—amounted to less than  \$1 trillion, while the U.S. real estate market alone totaled\$ 34 trillion. Francis-Ibbotson (2009) estimate \$18 trillion residential and \$16 trillion commercial real estate in the U.S. McKinsey Global Institute (2009) estimates higher numbers, likely using a different classification: the U.S. residential real estate market peaked at over \$30 trillion in 2006 and the global market peaked at \$91 trillion in 2007 (doubling in size since 1996, losing "only" 3.4 trillion in 2008).
- Some estimate the global infrastructure market at  \$8 trillion, but this presumably is largely listed equities. Among other alternative assets, the hedge fund and private equity fund markets were largest (near\$ 2 trillion each). Commodity funds have grown fast but their assets amounted to only about 0.25 trillion, a fraction of the size of listed natural resource companies or of privately held or sovereign-held natural resources.

# A.3 NOTES

[1] The largest groups in the institutional category were  \$17 trillion pension assets and\$ 8 trillion insurance assets. Assets managed for retail included mutual funds ( \$10 trillion), unit-linked pensions and insurance (\$ 6.5 trillion), and private banking ( \$4.5 trillion). Ineichen (2008) quotes higher estimates:\$ 62 trillion managed by traditional long-only institutions, of which \$23 trillion managed by pension funds, \$22 trillion by mutual funds, and 17 trillion by insurance firms.

Source notes. On estimating world wealth, see Ibbotson-Siegel-Love (1985) and Ibbotson-Brinson (1993) for earlier efforts and McKinsey Global Institute (2009) for a recent one.

# Appendix B

# Data sources and data-series construction

This appendix discusses the sources and construction of the various time series used in this book. In the main text, I list sources briefly beneath figure and table captions.

Most underlying data series are extracted from Bloomberg, including MSCI Barra's equity indices, Barclays Capital and other banks' bond indices, and S&P GSCI commodity futures indices. Other key sources include Kenneth French's website (visit http://mba.tuck.dartmouth.edu/pages/faculty/ken.french/data_library.html), Robert Shiller's website (visit http://www.econ.yale.edu/~shiller/data.htm), Arnott-Bernstein (2002), Dimson-Marsh-Staunton (2002, 2010), and Ibbotson Associates (Morningstar) yearbooks.

To create long data histories for major asset classes, I sometimes splice best quality recent data and best available older data series. Most exhibits display total returns denominated in U.S. dollars; but some exhibits show real (inflation-adjusted) returns or excess returns over cash or over maturity/duration-matched Treasuries.

I especially thank Elroy Dimson, Paul Marsh, and Mike Staunton for the use of their 110-year-long return histories and Robert Arnott for even older data used in Arnott-Bernstein (2002). I am grateful to Michael Afreh (Nomura), Andrew Ang, David Blitz (Robeco), Giuliano de Rossi (UBS), Eric Falkenstein, Kenneth Froot, Robin Greenwood, Campbell Harvey, Sharon Kozicki, Carmen Reinhart, Matti Suominen, Arthur Warga, and Mungo Wilson for sending me some of their research data. I also thank Professors Kenneth French, Morris Davis, Amit Goyal, Lubos Pastor, Robert Shiller, Stephen Wilcox, and Jeffrey Wurgler for data available on their websites. Finally, a few graphs are directly extracted from other works, as credited below them: Figures 9.8 Rosenberg-Maurer, 17.4 Reinhart-Rogoff, 18.1 Hasbrouck, 18.2 Naes-Skjeltorp-Odegaard, 19.7 Buraschi-Kosowski-Trojani, 19.9 Frazzini-Pedersen, 27.6 Reinhart-Rogoff.

# B.1 ASSET CLASS AND SECTOR RETURNS

For global equity market returns, I use MSCI Barra monthly data for developed markets since 1970 and for emerging markets since 1988. For longer histories, I use Dimson-Marsh-Staunton (2002, 2010) annual returns dating back to 1900 for developed markets and to 1976 for emerging markets.

For U.S. equity returns (i.e., value-weighted market index; value, size, momentum, and other style portfolios; as well as industry portfolios) I use data from Kenneth French's website, dating back to 1926. This is also the source for global value style portfolios and the one-month Treasury bill rate. For certain analyses I use stock price, dividend yield, and earnings yield series since 1871 from Robert Shiller's website. For the longest histories (U.S. market returns back to 1802), I use Arnott-Bernstein (2002) data, which in turn is a composite of data from Professor G.W. Schwert (pre-1871 data), Ibbotson Associates, and First Quadrant; I extend 2000s' data using S&P 500 total returns. Some volatility and correlation analyses are based on S&P 500 daily returns.

For U.S. Treasury returns and yields, I combine data from the Center for Research in Security Prices (CRSP) since 1952 and Bank of America Merrill Lynch (ML) Treasury index subsector data since 1978. (Since maturity subsectors differ somewhat between CRSP and ML, some interpolation is needed. Given the very high correlations between adjacent maturity Treasury returns, this should not distort the results.) I also use Ibbotson Associates' long (20-year) Treasury return and yield as well as the one-month Treasury bill rate since 1926. For earlier histories, I use data from Arnott-Bernstein (2002) and my own compilations. Data series before 1926 are almost always based on Homer-Sylla's (1991) yield histories, and often are based on high-grade municipal bond, corporate bond, or commercial paper series, given the absence of default-free Treasury yield histories. More recent daily yield histories are from Bloomberg.

For global government bond returns, I use Dimson-Marsh-Staunton (2002, 2010) data for currency-unhedged returns since 1900 and Citigroup (formerly Salomon Brothers) world government bond index data for currency-hedged returns since 1985.

For other fixed income returns and yields, the main source is Barclays Capital (BarCap, formerly Lehman Brothers) indices: investment-grade, Treasury, credit, and other sectors since the 1970s, as well as the high-yield index since the 1980s. These data are partly sourced via Bloomberg, partly via the Barclays Capital website. In early years only total returns and yield-to-worst measures are available (much of it based on Arthur Warga's pioneering research on early Lehman index data); they are linked to roughly maturity-matched Treasury subsectors to compute ex post excess returns and ex ante credit spreads. Since 1988-1989, I use better quality duration-adjusted excess returns and option-adjusted spreads (both over Treasuries). For certain rating/maturity subsector portfolios I use Citigroup and Merrill Lynch index data, but broad results are similar to BarCap/Lehman index data. Before Lehman indices became available, I use Ibbotson Associates' corporate bond returns (AAA/AA-rated long-dated bonds) for investment-grade bonds (since 1926). To proxy for high-yield bond returns from 1953 to the 1970s, I use a time series of low-quality (Baa and lower) bond returns that I got in grad school 20 years ago, which apparently was compiled by Roger Ibbotson in 1979 (no surprise!). For old corporate yields I use Moody's yields of seasoned, long-dated non-financial corporate bonds since 1919.

For emerging market debt, I use J.P. Morgan's "EMBI Global" index returns since 1994, extending the series to 1990-1993 by using Citigroup's Brady bond index data.

Sovereign CDS spreads are from Bloomberg.

For convertible bonds, I use Merrill Lynch's U.S. convertible bond index returns since the late-1980s.

For direct real estate in the U.S., my preferred index is the MIT-CRE "transactions-based" index since 1984 which uses NCREIF data but in a smarter way. The NCREIF index and its sector subsets date back to 1978. For listed real estate, I use the FTSE NAREIT composite index since 1972 and Global Property Research's global property stocks index (and its regional subsets) since 1984. For longer histories, I use the house price series compiled by Robert Shiller (annual data back to 1890 on his website), house price and rental yield data (back to 1960) collected by Davis-Lehnert-Martin (2008), data are located at Land and Property Values in the U.S. (Lincoln Institute of Land Policy, visit http://www.lincolninst.edu/resources/). (The latter series may not subtract lumpy capex-form net rents and thus may overstate investor returns.) In Chapter 3, I use estimates of 1960s' and 1970s' annual real estate returns from Dimson (2004):  $6.5\%$  and  $10.1\%$ , respectively.

For infrastructure, I use the UBS Global Infrastructure and Utilities (listed stock) index since 1990 as a proxy.

For timber, I use quarterly data since 1987 from the NCREF website.

For art price indices, I use Art Market Research data since 1976 and artprice.com data since 1990.

For commodity futures returns, I mainly use the S&P GSCI commodity index and its subsectors, dating back to the 1970s. For longer spot price histories I use oil price data from Knut Kjaer (1900), gold price data from Bloomberg (1920), and the Commodity Research Bureau's (CRB) index data (1957). In Chapter 3, I approximate 1960s' commodity returns by the sum of CRB spot index changes and Treasury bill returns. I also use self-collected total returns and roll return series for single-commodity futures.

For hedge fund returns (and funds of funds, and subsectors), I mainly use indices from Hedge Fund Research since 1990 but also compare it with indices from Dow Jones Credit Suisse since 1994.

For private equity and venture capital fund returns, I use Cambridge Associates' benchmark indices dating back to the 1980s. Quarterly indices are based on internal rates of returns. For listed private equity firms' performance I use the LPX 50 index since 1994.

# B.2 STRATEGY STYLE RETURNS

Value. Simulated long-short equity portfolio returns based on sorting stocks by the book/price (as well as cash flow/price, dividend/price, and earnings/price) ratios. Data are from Professor Kenneth French's website. Some analysis focuses on the U.S. book/price factor portfolio, called "value minus growth" (VMG) or "high minus low" (HML), others on a four-indicator composite. A global composite is the broadest value proxy I use, based on four U.S. indicators (since 1926, some start only in 1952) and four non-U.S. indicators (since 1975, extended with MSCI Barra data for 2008-2009). The ex post value outperformance and ex ante value spread in Figure 12.4 for raw and sector-neutral strategies since 1990 are from Giuliano De Rossi at UBS, who uses high-minus-low spreads after sorting U.S. Russell 3000 stocks each month into three tritiles.

Other equity trading style factors from Kenneth French's website include the size premium SMB (outperformance of small-cap vs. large-cap stocks), the momentum proxy MOM (outperformance of stocks with relatively high returns over the past year, excluding last month), short-term reversal STREV (outperformance of stocks with relatively low returns during the past month), and long-term reversal LTREV (outperformance of stocks with relatively low returns between 12 and 60 months ago). All of these style factors are based on long-short portfolios across a broad universe of U.S. stocks. Details on these factors as well as on style and industry portfolios are available on Kenneth French's website. I use the popular FF factors and sorted portfolio returns near the top of the page (visit http://mba.tuck.dartmouth.edu/pages/faculty/ken.french/ data_library.html). Professor French advised using these "research returns" instead of the "historical benchmark returns" near the bottom of the page, which are constructed in a somewhat different fashion.)

**Carry.** Simulated currency carry strategies are based on my own compilations using G10 spot exchange rates and one-month deposit rates (and extending back to other available money market rates in the 1970s). The base case strategy each week buys the top-three G10 currencies with the highest yields and sells the bottom-three with the lowest yields. The composite carry index also includes an emerging currency carry strategy, a fixed income carry strategy across G10 markets based on relative curve steepness, and a credit carry basket. Data are mainly from Bloomberg.

Trend. Simulated commodity momentum strategies are based on self-collected commodity futures return series since 1990 and earlier on the Mount Lucas Management Commodities trend index. This composite trend style index applies a simple trend-following rule each week on commodity futures, equity country futures, bond/rate futures, and foreign exchange forwards: go long (short) if the current price is above (below) the 12-month moving average. Data are mainly from Bloomberg.

Volatility. Volatility-selling returns are based on the simulated Merrill Lynch Equity Volatility Arbitrage Index since 1989, which tries to gain from the typically positive gap between market-implied volatility and subsequent realized volatility of the S&P 500 index's Bloomberg ticker MLHFEV1 index. Chapter 15 also analyzes the simulated covered-call-writing indices and put-writing indices (BXM, BXY, PUT in Bloomberg) on the S&P 500 index from the Chicago Board of Exchange, dating back to the 1980s. These indices are not delta neutral so their returns reflect market-directional exposures as much as volatility exposures.

Data on volatility-sorted stock portfolios are from Andrew Ang (Columbia University), David Blitz (Robeco), and Giuliano De Rossi (UBS).

Importantly, no trading costs have been subtracted from any of the strategy style returns.

# B.3 FACTOR PROXIES

Growth. Monthly change in the consensus forecast of U.S. real GDP growth over the next year, based on a survey by Consensus Economics.

Inflation. Monthly change in the consensus forecast of U.S. consumer price inflation rate over the next year, based on a survey by Consensus Economics.

Illiquidity. Monthly change in the Bank of England Liquidity Index, with sign flipped, and extended by proxy series in 1990-1991 and 2009 (Citi liquidity index). The Bank of England index is a composite based on several normalized liquidity-related series: bid-ask spreads in bond, FX and stock markets, return-to-volume ratios for individual stocks as well as for bond and stock markets, and yield spreads in corporate bond and money markets. Bank of England (2007) states: "The liquidity index shows the number of standard deviations from the mean. It is a simple unweighted average of nine liquidity measures, normalised on the period 1999-2004. The series shown is an exponentially weighted moving average. The indicator is more reliable after 1997 as it is based on a greater number of underlying measures."

Volatility. Monthly change in the VIX index of implied volatilities in S&P 500 equity index options.

# B.4 FORWARD-LOOKING YIELDS AND SPREADS

The main equity market yields I use are the smoothed real earnings yield (10-year average earnings divided by price, adjusted for inflation), standard trailing earnings yield, and dividend yield from Robert Shiller's website. The operating earnings yield is from Standard & Poor's website, the adjusted earnings yield in Wilcox (2007) is from Stephen Wilcox's website. Net buyback yields and M&A yields are from Nomura Securities.

Rental yields for U.S. housing are from Davis-Lehnert-Martin (2008); data are located at Land and Property Values in the U.S. (Lincoln Institute of Land Policy, visit http://www.lincolninst.edu/resources/). The cap rate series for U.S. commercial real estate is from the NCREF.

I have already described bond yield sources above under "Asset class and sector returns". In particular, the real yields in Figure 2.7 are Moody's long-dated Baa yield and Ibbotson Associates' 20-year Treasury yield minus the expected long-term inflation rate described below. Liquidity spreads in Figure 10.8 are from J.P. Morgan and Bloomberg. Ex ante value spreads for equity long-short value strategy in Figure 12.4 are from Giuliano De Rossi (UBS). Money market rates in Figure 3.12 and Table 3.5 are from Bloomberg and the St. Louis Fed website, while some yield curve analysis is based on data from the Federal Reserve Board website.

The ex ante equity premium is equity yield (measured by some variant of market earnings yield) and Treasury yield (long-dated or short-dated). The survey-based bond risk premium is the market yield (of the 10-year Treasury) minus the consensus forecast of short-term rates over the life of the bond. Credit spread is the difference between corporate bond yield and the somehow matching Treasury yield, preferably adjusted for embedded options. The break-even inflation rate (BEI) is the difference between nominal Treasury yield and a matching inflation-linked bond (TIPs) yield. The "net illiquidity premium vs. inflation premium" in Figure 17.9 is the difference between the survey-based long-term inflation forecast and the BEI.

# B.5 SURVEY DATA AND EXPECTED INFLATION

Consensus forecast returns on asset returns are from several survey sources: UBS Gallup poll, Duke University CFO survey, University of Michigan consumer confidence survey, Merrill Lynch fund manager poll, Survey of Professional Investors, and academic surveys by Ivo Welch and Pablo Fernandez. Analysts' earnings forecasts are from Credit Suisse. Economists' forecasts of future growth are from Blue Chip Economic Indicators.

Economists' forecasts of future U.S. inflation rates, to construct ex ante real yield series, are based on several survey sources (see below). Forecasts of inflation outside the U.S. are from Consensus Economics and private compilations.

Economists' forecasts of future Treasury bill rates, to construct survey-based bond risk premium series, are mainly based on Blue Chip Economic Indicators, but also

Goldsmith-Nagan (courtesy Kenneth Froot), Wall Street Journal, and Consensus Economics.

The expected long-term inflation series, which is displayed in Figure 17.5 and used in various places, is a spliced series and the most reasonable history I could come up with. Obviously, sources get better over time; distant histories can only be estimated statistically. I describe my series construction in some detail.

Actual survey data on long-term inflation start in 1978, with Hoey (private collection later passed to the Fed), the Livingston survey and the Survey of Professional Investors (both available at the Philly Fed website), *Blue Chip Economic Indicators*, and *Consensus Economics* who conduct surveys at different times. I take an average of two to three available surveys over the past 30 years.

Between 1955 and 1978 I use the statistical estimate of long-term inflation expectations by Kozicki-Tinsley (2006). Their "Survey-based estimates of the term structure of expected U.S. inflation" goes beyond an exponentially weighted average of past inflation rates because it also uses information in consensus forecasts of next year inflation. Short-term inflation forecasts in the Livingston survey date back to about 1950. (Fortunately, the contours of these statistical estimates nicely match those of available long-term consensus forecasts during the overlapping period after 1978.)

Before 1955 I use my own statistical estimates, combining two simple methodologies:

- One just takes a weighted average of past 10-year inflation rates, with gradually declining weights. Until the U.S. abandoned the gold standard in 1933 I average this time series estimate with zero (thereby anchoring the inflation forecast), based on the idea that during the gold standard inflations and deflations were equally likely to occur and not likely to become sustained.
- Another regresses next decade inflation on past decade inflation and uses the fitted value as the expected inflation estimate. Coefficients are re-estimated each year with new data using an expanding window that begins in 1871. This approach has very few independent observations; so I only start it in the 1930s.

Both approaches give broadly similar contours for inflation expectations. Later evidence suggests that survey expectations tend to be adaptive (reflecting past inflation experience, with declining weights). However, it seems reasonable that knowledge of the gold standard and experience of near-zero inflation over very long histories anchored expectations better at zero until the gold standard was abandoned.

# B.6 MISCELLANEOUS OTHER

The illiquidity estimates in Table 2.1, Figure 2.2, and Figure 18.4 are based on subjective assessments for each asset class on a scale from 1 to 5. When making these assessments I got some help from multi-asset-class experts at the giant Dutch pension fund asset manager APG.

Consumer price inflation (CPI) histories are from Bloomberg and Haver Analytics. Longer histories are from Robert Shiller's website and Arnott-Bernstein (2002). In Figure 3.10, Table 3.6, and Table 3.7 all asset returns are deflated using the CPI, except for ex ante 10-year real Treasury yield, which is based on expected long-term inflation.

Real GDP growth (and GDP-per-capita growth) series are from Bloomberg and

Haver Analytics. Longest histories—U.S. data going back to 1802—are from Arnott-Bernstein (2002).

Most stock market predictors in Table 8.6 are sourced from the Haver Analytics, Amit Goyal, and Robert Shiller websites, as well as from Mungo Wilson. Bond market predictors in Tables 9.3 and 10.4 are from Cochrane-Piazzesi (bond risk premium), Kim-Wright (bond risk premium), Greenwood-Vayanos (debt maturity share), the Chicago Fed (CFNAI or Chicago Fed National Activity Index), Consensus Economics (expected fiscal balance), Bloomberg (VIX and MOVE implied volatility indices for stocks and Treasuries), and other macro-data from Haver Analytics.

The default rates in Chapter 10 are from Moody's (2010).
